The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Six Productive Moods of the Third Figure
Lists the six productive moods of the third figure in a table.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six productive moods of the third figure and apply them to validate categorical syllogisms in Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Only six mood combinations yield valid conclusions in the third figure, all producing particular results.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism is defined by the middle term serving as the subject in both premises. This figure is particularly useful in Islamic sciences for deriving particular rulings from universal principles. This lesson will help you memorize the six productive moods presented in the classical table, a foundational tool for logical analysis in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Decode the symbolic notation
The symbols (ك م), (ج م), (ك س), (ج س) represent universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, and particular negative propositions, respectively. Commit these to memory as they are used consistently in syllogistic logic.
These are abbreviations in Arabic: كلي موجبة, جزئي موجبة, etc.
2
List the six productive combinations
The passage enumerates six moods: (ك م)+(ك م)→(ج م), (ك م)+(ك س)→(ج س), (ج م)+(ك م)→(ج م), (ك م)+(ج س)→(ج س), (ج م)+(ك م)→(ج م), (ج م)+(ك س)→(ج س). Notice that all conclusions are particular (starting with ج). Memorize these patterns.
The order is minor premise first, then major premise.
3
Use the summary table for recall
The table at the end organizes the moods: rows for major premises, columns for minor premises. The cells show the conclusion type or indicate sterility (الفراغ). Practice filling in the table from memory.
Empty cells correspond to non-productive (sterile) moods.
4
Apply a mood to a real Islamic syllogism
Take a valid third figure syllogism from fiqh or kalam, identify its mood, and confirm that it matches one of the six productive patterns. This connects memorization to practical use.
Example: 'Every act of gambling (M) is prohibited (P). Every act of gambling (M) is a transaction (S). Therefore, some transactions are prohibited (S is P).' This is mood 1 (ك م)+(ك م)→(ج م).

Commentary
This passage presents the six productive moods of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The third figure is defined by the middle term being the subject in both premises. The author first lists the six productive combinations, each expressed by symbols: (ك م) stands for universal affirmative, (ج م) for particular affirmative, (ك س) for universal negative, (ج س) for particular negative. The list shows that the third figure yields only particular conclusions (all start with ج). The table following the list summarizes the productive and sterile combinations: the rows represent the major premise, columns the minor premise, and the cells indicate the conclusion type or 'sterile'. For example, universal affirmative minor with universal affirmative major yields particular affirmative; universal affirmative minor with universal negative major yields particular negative, etc. The empty cells (الفراغ) indicate sterile moods that do not produce a valid conclusion. This memorization is essential for recognizing valid syllogistic forms in Islamic legal reasoning, such as in usul al-fiqh.

Categorical Example of the Third Figure's First Mood
Categorical example of first mood of third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the middle term and mood in a categorical syllogism of the third figure and verify its validity.

Core Idea
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises, demanding a particular conclusion.

This lesson examines the first mood of the third figure using categorical propositions. We will see how universal affirmative premises yield a particular affirmative conclusion, illustrated with examples from common knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify premises and conclusion
In the example, the premises are 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is capable of knowledge and writing'. The conclusion is 'Some animal is capable of knowledge and writing'.
2
Determine the mood
Both premises are universal affirmative (A) and the conclusion is particular affirmative (I). The mood is AII (Darapti) in the third figure.
The first mood of the third figure is defined as (Universal Affirmative) + (Universal Affirmative) → (Particular Affirmative).
3
Find the middle term
The term 'human' appears as the subject in both premises, acting as the middle term connecting the two extremes ('animal' and 'capable of knowledge and writing').
4
Check the figure
Since the middle term is the subject of both premises, this syllogism belongs to the third figure.
5
Verify the conclusion
The conclusion is particular affirmative. It follows logically because all humans share the properties mentioned, so some animals (those that are human) must have those properties.
Validity is confirmed by the mood and figure conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ - على الضرب الأول منه ( ك م ) + ( ك م ) ∴ ( ج م )
من الاقتراني الحملي :
• كل إنسان حيوان وكل إنسان قابل للعلم وصنعة الكتابة
∴ بعض الحيوان قابل للعلم وصنعة الكتابة
• كل ذهب معدن وكل ذهب لا يتأكسد (أي : لا يتأثر بالأحماض)
∴ بعض المعدن لا يتأكسد
من الاقتراني الشرطي :
• كلما كانت الثمرة تفاحاً كانت فاكهة
وكلما كانت تفاحاً كانت قابلة لأن تعصر شراباً .
∴ فقد يكون إذا كانت الثمرة فاكهة كانت قابلة لأن تعصر شراباً .
• دائماً إما أن يكون الحيوان الماشي يمشي على بطنه أو على أرجل.
ودائماً إما أن يكون الماشي يمشي على رجلين أو أكثر.
∴ فقد يكون إما أن يمشي الحيوان على بطنه أو على رجلين أو أكثر.
A - For the first mood: (Universal Affirmative) + (Universal Affirmative) → (Particular Affirmative) From categorical syllogism: Every human is an animal and every human is capable of knowledge and writing; therefore some animal is capable of knowledge and writing. Every gold is a mineral and every gold does not oxidize (i.e., is not affected by acids); therefore some mineral does not oxidize. From conditional syllogism: Whenever a fruit is an apple, it is a fruit; and whenever it is an apple, it is squeezable for juice; therefore it may be that whenever a fruit is a fruit, it is squeezable for juice. Whenever a walking animal walks on its belly or on legs; and whenever a walker walks on two legs or more; therefore it may be that a walking animal walks on its belly or on two legs or more.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage provides examples of the first mood of the third figure, which has two universal affirmative premises and yields a particular affirmative conclusion. The author first gives categorical examples: from 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is capable of knowledge and writing', we conclude 'Some animal is capable of knowledge and writing'. Note the middle term is 'human' (subject in both premises). Similarly, the gold example yields 'some mineral does not oxidize'. Then conditional examples are given: a conditional (متصلة) example: if it is an apple then it is a fruit, and if it is an apple then it is squeezable, so maybe if it is a fruit then it is squeezable. The last example is disjunctive (منفصلة): a walking animal either walks on belly or legs, and a walker either walks on two legs or more, so possibly a walking animal walks on belly, two legs, or more. These examples illustrate how the third figure is used in Islamic logic across different proposition types.

شكل ثالث
ضرب أول
كلي موجبة
جزئية موجبة

B) Particular affirmative

D) Particular negative

Key Takeaway
The first mood of the third figure requires two universal affirmative premises to yield a particular affirmative conclusion, with the middle term as the subject in both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a categorical syllogism in the third figure using Islamic terminology, e.g., 'Every 'ibada is an act of worship; every 'ibada is prescribed; therefore some act of worship is prescribed.' Then verify its mood and figure.

The Syllogism

Conditional and Disjunctive Examples of the Third Figure's First Mood
Conditional and disjunctive examples of first mood of third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse conditional and disjunctive syllogisms in the third figure and determine their validity.

Core Idea
The third figure also works for conditional syllogisms, where the middle term is the common antecedent or disjunct.

This lesson extends the third figure to conditional syllogisms (اقتراني شرطي). We will examine both a conditional (mutasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila) example, noting how the particular conclusion is expressed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional syllogism
The first example is a conditional (mutasila) with 'if-then' structure: 'Whenever a fruit is an apple, it is a fruit' and 'Whenever a fruit is an apple, it is squeezable'. The second is a disjunctive (munfasila) with 'either-or': 'A walking animal walks on its belly or on legs' and 'A walker walks on two legs or more'.
2
Extract the components
For the conditional: the antecedent in both premises is 'fruit is an apple'. The consequents are 'it is a fruit' and 'it is squeezable'. For the disjunctive: the shared element is 'the walking animal (or walker) is a walker', and the disjuncts differ.
3
Identify the middle term
In the conditional example, the middle term is the antecedent 'fruit is an apple' (or 'being an apple'). In the disjunctive example, the middle term is the subject 'the walking animal' (or 'the walker'), which appears in both premises.
4
Determine the form and conclusion
The conditional example yields the conclusion 'it may be that whenever a fruit is a fruit, it is squeezable'—a particular conditional. The disjunctive yields 'it may be that a walking animal walks on its belly or on two legs or more'—a particular disjunctive. Both are particular (possible) because the third figure always gives a particular conclusion.
5
Check validity
Both conclusions follow from the premises. For the conditional: if the antecedent is common, then some relationship between the consequents is possible. For the disjunctive: if the same subject has two alternative properties in each premise, then combined alternatives are possible.
Validity relies on the shared middle term and the particular nature of the conclusion.

Identifying the Second Figure (الضرب الثاني) of Conjunctive Syllogism
Author presents the second figure mood pattern with categorical and conditional examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second figure pattern (ك م)+(ك س)∴(ج س) in both categorical and conditional conjunctive syllogisms and verify its validity.

Core Idea
The second figure has the middle term as subject in both premises, yielding a negative particular conclusion.

The second figure of a conjunctive syllogism is defined by a specific mood pattern where the middle term (ك) is the subject in both premises. The conclusion relates the remaining two terms (ج and س). Understanding this pattern allows you to construct and evaluate syllogisms in Islamic logic. We will examine examples from both categorical and conditional syllogisms to solidify the concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term (ك)
In each example, locate the term that appears as the subject in both premises. It is the common term that connects the premises.
For instance, in 'كل نائم يفقد حسه الظاهر ولا نائم مكلف شرعاً', the middle term is 'نائم' (sleeper).
2
Verify the mood pattern
Check that the premises are (ك م) and (ك س) i.e., the middle term is subject in both. The conclusion should be (ج س).
The pattern is explicitly given as (ك م)+(ك س)∴(ج س).
3
Determine the type of premise
Note whether the premises are categorical (using كل/لا) or conditional (using كلما/إذا/دائماً إما). This affects the form but not the figure pattern.
4
Confirm the conclusion type
In the second figure, the conclusion is always negative (سلب) and particular (جزئي). Check if the conclusion matches this characteristic.
For categorical examples, the conclusion begins with 'فليس بعض'.
5
Evaluate validity
The pattern (ك م)+(ك س)∴(ج س) is always valid for the second figure. Ensure that the terms are consistent and no term is missing.
All examples in the passage are valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دائماً إما أن يكون الحيوان الماشي يمشي على بطنه أو على أرجل. ودائماً إما أن يكون الماشي يمشي على رجلين أو أكثر. ∴ فقد يكون إما أن يمشي الحيوان على بطنه أو على رجلين أو أكثر.

ب ـ على الضرب الثاني منه (ك م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س)
من الاقتراني الحملي:
• كل نائم يفقد حسه الظاهر ولا نائم مكلف شرعاً ∴ فليس بعض من يفقد حسه الظاهر مكلفاً
• كل مجنون عديم التفكير السوي ولا مجنون هو مكلف شرعاً ∴ فليس بعض عديم التفكير السوي مكلفاً شرعاً
• كل خطاط ماهر صنعة البنان ولا خطاط أعمى ∴ فليس بعض ماهر صنعة البنان أعمى
من الاقتراني الشرطي:
• كلما كان الحيوان إنساناً كان قابلاً للتعلم وصنعة الكتابة. وليس البتة إذا كان الحيوان إنساناً كان طيراً. ∴ فقد لا يكون إذا كان قابلاً للتعلم وصنعة الكتابة كان طيراً.
• دائماً إما أن يكون أحد الأبوين أباً أو أماً. ولا أحد من الآباء أنثى. ∴ فقد لا يكون إما أن يكون أحد الأبوين أماً أو أنثى.
صغرى كبرى نتيجة
Always, either the walking animal walks on its belly or on legs. And always, either the walker walks on two legs or more. Therefore, it may be that the animal walks on its belly or on two legs or more.

B. On the second figure of it: (K M) + (K S) ∴ (J S)
From categorical conjunctive:
• Every sleeper lacks their external sense, and no sleeper is legally accountable. ∴ So some who lack external sense are not accountable.
• Every insane person lacks sound thinking, and no insane person is legally accountable. ∴ So some who lack sound thinking are not legally accountable.
• Every calligrapher is skilled in the art of the finger, and no calligrapher is blind. ∴ So some who are skilled in the art of the finger are not blind.
From conditional conjunctive:
• Whenever an animal is human, it is capable of learning and writing. And absolutely not if the animal is human, it is a bird. ∴ So it may not be that if capable of learning and writing, it is a bird.
• Always, either one of the parents is a father or a mother. And no parent is female. ∴ So it may not be that either one of the parents is a mother or a female.
Minor premise, major premise, conclusion.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the second figure (الضرب الثاني) of the conjunctive syllogism (الاقتراني). The author first gives a disjunctive conditional example to illustrate the mood pattern, then explicitly labels the pattern as (ك م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س). He then provides examples from categorical syllogisms (الاقتراني الحملي) and conditional syllogisms (الاقتراني الشرطي). In the categorical examples, each valid syllogism has a universal affirmative major premise and a universal negative minor premise, yielding a particular negative conclusion. For instance: 'Every sleeper lacks external sense' (major) and 'No sleeper is legally accountable' (minor) leads to 'Some who lack external sense are not legally accountable.' This demonstrates that the second figure produces a negative particular conclusion. The conditional examples use temporal conditionals (كلما) and disjunctive conditionals (دائماً إما) to show the same mood pattern. The pattern is consistent: the middle term (ك) appears as subject in both premises, and the conclusion relates the other two terms (ج and س).

Key Terms
الضرب الثاني
الاقتراني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) (ك م)+(ج م)∴(ج م)
C) (ك م)+(ج س)∴(ج س)
D) (م ك)+(م س)∴(ج س)

Key Takeaway
The second figure of a conjunctive syllogism has the middle term as subject in both premises, and the conclusion is always a negative particular proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

5 min
The Third Figure of Conjunctive Syllogism
Author presents the third figure mood pattern with categorical and conditional examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third figure mood pattern (ك م)+(ج م)∴(ج م) and construct both categorical and conditional syllogisms following this pattern.

Core Idea
The third figure places the middle term as predicate in both premises, producing a particular conclusion.

This passage introduces the third figure of conjunctive syllogism, defined by the mood pattern (ك م)+(ج م)∴(ج م). The author provides categorical and conditional examples to demonstrate how this figure works. Understanding this pattern is crucial for analyzing arguments in Islamic logic, as it appears in kalam and fiqh reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the mood pattern
The pattern is (ك م)+(ج م)∴(ج م). Note that the middle term (م) appears as predicate in both premises.
This pattern is specific to the author's system; standard logic has the middle term as subject in the third figure.
2
Break down the categorical example
In the first example, 'Every calligrapher is skilled in the art of the finger' and 'Some calligraphers are painters' yields 'Some skilled in the art of the finger are painters.' Identify the middle term as predicate in both premises (here the middle term is the subject in standard terms, but follow the author's variables).
Be aware of the shift: the author's notation may differ from standard Aristotelian logic.
3
Break down the conditional examples
The conditional examples follow the same pattern: e.g., 'Whenever the soul is content, it is happy' and 'Some content souls are pious' yields 'It may be that if the soul is happy, it is pious.' The conclusion is probabilistic (قد يكون).
Conditional forms include temporal and disjunctive types.
4
Practice constructing your own
Using the pattern, create a new categorical syllogism in the third figure with Islamic content, such as 'Every prayer is obligatory' and 'Some prayers are recited silently' leading to 'Some obligatory acts are recited silently.'
Ensure the middle term is the predicate in both premises according to the pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جـ ـ على الضرب الثالث منه (ك م) + (ج م) ∴ (ج م)
من الاقتراني الحملي:
• كل خطاط ماهر صنعة البنان. وبعض الخطاطين رسّامون. ∴ فبعض ماهري صنعة البنان رسّامون
• كل رسّام مفنّ وبعض الرسامين مجانين ∴ فبعض المفنين مجانين
من الاقتراني الشرطي:
• كلما كانت النفس رضية كانت سعيدة. وبعض النفوس الرضية تقية مؤمنة. ∴ فقد يكون إذا كانت النفس سعيدة كانت تقية مؤمنة.
• دائماً إما أن يكون الماء سائلاً أو متجمداً. وقد يكون إذا كان الماء سائلاً كان ملحاً أجاجاً. ∴ فقد يكون إما أن يكون الماء متجمداً أو ملحاً أجاجاً. أو: ∴ فقد يكون إذا كان الماء متجمداً كان ملحاً أجاجاً.
صغرى كبرى نتيجة
C. On the third figure of it: (K M) + (J M) ∴ (J M)
From categorical conjunctive:
• Every calligrapher is skilled in the art of the finger, and some calligraphers are painters. ∴ So some skilled in the art of the finger are painters.
• Every painter is an artist, and some painters are insane. ∴ So some artists are insane.
From conditional conjunctive:
• Whenever the soul is content, it is happy, and some content souls are pious and faithful. ∴ So it may be that if the soul is happy, it is pious and faithful.
• Always, either water is liquid or frozen, and it may be that if water is liquid, it is salty and bitter. ∴ So it may be that either water is frozen or salty and bitter. Or: ∴ So it may be that if water is frozen, it is salty and bitter.
Minor premise, major premise, conclusion.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the third figure (الضرب الثالث) of the conjunctive syllogism. The author specifies the mood pattern as (ك م) + (ج م) ∴ (ج م), where the middle term (م) is predicate in both premises. The conclusion connects the two remaining terms (ج and م). Categorical examples follow: e.g., 'Every calligrapher is skilled in the art of the finger' and 'Some calligraphers are painters' yields 'Some skilled in the art of the finger are painters.' This shows that the third figure produces a particular affirmative conclusion. The conditional examples include temporal and disjunctive forms: for instance, 'Whenever the soul is content, it is happy' and 'Some content souls are pious' produces 'So it may be that if the soul is happy, it is pious.' The pattern remains consistent across both types.

Key Terms
الضرب الثالث (al-ḍarb al-thālith): The third figure of a conjunctive syllogism, with mood pattern (ك م)+(ج م)∴(ج م) where the middle term is predicate in both premises. [Note: In standard Aristotelian logic, the third figure has the middle term as subject in both premises; here it is predicate.]
النتيجة (al-natīja): The conclusion of a syllogism, derived from the premises. In the third figure, it is always particular affirmative in the categorical examples. [Note: No significant shift.]

B) (ك م)+(ج م)∴(ج م)
C) (ك م)+(ج س)∴(ج س)
D) (م ك)+(م س)∴(ج س)

Key Takeaway
The third figure of conjunctive syllogism is defined by the pattern (ك م)+(ج م)∴(ج م), where the middle term is predicate in both premises, producing a particular conclusion.

Recognising the Fourth Figure of Categorical Syllogism
Author presents the fourth figure mood pattern with categorical examples only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth figure mood pattern and apply it to categorical syllogisms in Islamic juristic reasoning.

Core Idea
The fourth figure uses a unique pattern to derive a negative conclusion from a universal major and particular minor.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth figure (الضرب الرابع) of the conjunctive syllogism as presented by the author. Unlike the standard four figures of Aristotelian logic, this figure has a distinctive mood pattern: (ك م)+(ج س)∴(ج س). The author illustrates it with two categorical examples drawn from Islamic legal topics—prayer of children and purity of water. By the end, you will be able to recognise this pattern and analyse its components.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood pattern
The pattern is (ك م)+(ج س)∴(ج س). Note that the conclusion has the same structure as the minor premise (ج س), but the subject of the conclusion is actually the predicate of the major premise. In the first example, 'كل صبي هو دون التكليف الشرعي' (major) has subject 'صبي' (K) and predicate 'دون التكليف' (M). The minor 'ليس بعض الصبيان تصح صلاته' has subject 'بعض الصبيان' (J) and predicate 'تصح صلاته' (S). The conclusion 'فليس بعض من هو دون التكليف الشرعي تصح صلاته' has subject 'بعض من هو دون التكليف' (M) and predicate 'تصح صلاته' (S). So the conclusion swaps the subject of the major to the predicate of the minor.
The letter notation: K, M, J, S are placeholders. K is the subject of major, M its predicate; J is the subject of minor, S its predicate.
2
Determine the mood (quality and quantity)
In the first example, the major is universal affirmative (A), the minor is particular negative (O), and the conclusion is particular negative (O). So the mood is AOO. The second example: major universal affirmative (A), minor particular negative (O), conclusion particular negative (O) — also AOO. Note that the pattern is fixed: major is always universal, minor particular, and conclusion negative.
The text does not list other moods for this figure.
3
Identify the middle term
The middle term is the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. In the first example, 'صبي' (child) appears as the subject of both premises but does not appear in the conclusion. Thus 'صبي' is the middle term. In standard categorical logic, the middle term must be distributed at least once, but here it is not distributed in the major (universal affirmative distributes subject) but also not distributed in the minor (particular negative distributes predicate only). This may indicate an invalid syllogism, but the author presents it as a valid figure.
The fourth figure here does not conform to the usual rules of syllogism (e.g., middle term distribution). It may be considered a special case or a different system.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Consider the second example: 'كل ماء بقي على خلقته الأصلية طاهر' (Every water that remains in its original state is pure) — major. 'وبعض الماء الباقي على خلقته الأصلية ليس بماء خال من الشوائب' (Some water remaining in its original state is not free of impurities) — minor. Conclusion: 'فبعض الماء الطاهر ليس بخالٍ من الشوائب' (Some pure water is not free of impurities). Here the middle term is 'ماء بقي على خلقته الأصلية' (water in original state).
The second example is from fiqh al-tahara (purity). This shows how the fourth figure can be used in legal reasoning.
5
Check the alternative expression
The author adds an alternative conclusion: 'فقد لا يكون الماء الطاهر خالياً من الشوائب' (So it may be that pure water is not free of impurities). This expresses the particular negative as a possibility (قد + negative). This is a weaker statement but still captures the same logical relation.
The use of 'قد' (may) suggests a modal nuance, but the logical core remains the same.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
د ـ على الضرب الرابع منه (ك م) + (ج س) ∴ (ج س)
من الاقتراني الحملي:
• كل صبي هو دون التكليف الشرعي. وليس بعض الصبيان تصح صلاته. ∴ فليس بعض من هو دون التكليف الشرعي تصح صلاته
• كل ماء بقي على خلقته الأصلية طاهر. وبعض الماء الباقي على خلقته الأصلية ليس بماء خال من الشوائب. ∴ فبعض الماء الطاهر ليس بخالٍ من الشوائب. أو ∴ فقد لا يكون الماء الطاهر خالياً من الشوائب.
D. On the fourth figure of it: (K M) + (J S) ∴ (J S)
From categorical conjunctive:
• Every child is below legal responsibility, and some children are not valid in their prayer. ∴ So some who are below legal responsibility are not valid in their prayer.
• Every water that remains in its original state is pure, and some water remaining in its original state is not water free of impurities. ∴ So some pure water is not free of impurities. Or: ∴ So it may be that pure water is not free of impurities.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth figure (الضرب الرابع) of the conjunctive syllogism. The mood pattern is (ك م) + (ج س) ∴ (ج س). Only categorical examples are given. The first example: 'Every child is below legal responsibility' (major) and 'Some children's prayer is not valid' (minor) yields 'Some who are below legal responsibility have invalid prayer.' This is a particular negative conclusion. The second example: 'Every water that remains in its original state is pure' and 'Some water remaining in its original state is not free of impurities' yields 'Some pure water is not free of impurities.' An alternative formulation 'So it may be that pure water is not free of impurities' is also provided. The fourth figure thus produces negative conclusions.

The Fifth Mood of the Syllogism: (ج م)+(ك م) ∴ (ج م)
Defines and illustrates the fifth mood with categorical and conditional examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fifth mood pattern in both categorical and conditional syllogisms and verify its validity.

Core Idea
The fifth mood combines a particular affirmative with a universal affirmative to yield a particular affirmative, across proposition types.

Here the author introduces the fifth mood of the syllogism symbolically as (ج م)+(ك م) ∴ (ج م). This pattern holds whether the premises are categorical or conditional. Let's examine how the same logical form works across different proposition types.

Guided Path
1
Decode the notation
The letters ج, ك, م represent the three terms. In this pattern, the minor premise links ج and م, the major premise links ك and م, and the conclusion links ج and م. This yields a particular affirmative conclusion.
The exact mapping to figure is not specified, but the mood pattern is clear.
2
Examine the categorical example
Look at the first categorical example: 'Some people are poets' (particular affirmative) and 'Every person breathes with their lungs' (universal affirmative) lead to 'Some poets breathe with their lungs' (particular affirmative). Identify the middle term (person).
3
Test the conditional example
Consider the conditional example: 'It may be that if the place is illuminated, it is from the light of a lamp' (particular conditional) and 'Whenever the place is illuminated, it is suitable for vision' (universal conditional) yield 'It may be that if the place is illuminated by the light of a lamp, it is suitable for vision' (particular conditional).
This shows the same mood pattern with conditional propositions.
4
Verify the logical validity
For the categorical case, the mood is IAI in the third figure (Disamis), which is valid. For the conditional case, the inference holds because the universal conditional ensures the consequent follows for all instances, so combining with a particular conditional yields a particular conditional conclusion.
5
Compare both types
Notice that in both types, the pattern of quantities is the same: particular + universal = particular. The propositions differ in their internal structure (categorical vs conditional), but the mood pattern is preserved across both.
This demonstrates the universality of the mood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صغرى كبرى نتيجة
هـ ـ على الضرب الخامس منه (ج م) + (ك م) ∴ (ج م)
من الاقتراني الحملي:
• بعض الناس شعراء وكل الناس يتنفسون برئاتهم
∴ فبعض الشعراء يتنفسون برئاتهم
• بعض الحيوان إنسان وكل حيوان يتحرك بالإرادة
∴ فبعض الإنسان يتحرك بالإرادة
• بعض الورد أحمر وكل ورد هو نبات
∴ فبعض الأحمر نبات
من الاقتراني الشرطي:
• قد يكون إذا كان المكان مضيئاً كان ذلك من نور مصباح.
وكلما كان المكان مضيئاً كان صالحاً للرؤية والمشي فيه.
∴ فقد يكون إذا أضاء المكان من نور مصباح كان صالحاً للرؤية
والمشي فيه.
• أحياناً إما أن يكون المصلي راكعاً أو ساجداً.
وكل مصل راكع هو خاضع لربه.
∴ فأحياناً إما أن يكون المصلي ساجداً أو خاضعاً لربه.
• قد يكون إذا كان الشيء طيب الرائحة كان ورداً.
وكل طيب الرائحة يهدى للأحبة.
∴ فبعض الورد يهدى للأحبة.
أو ∴ فربما إذا كان الشيء ورداً يهدى للأحبة.
Minor premise Major premise Conclusion
H — On the fifth mood from it: (j m) + (k m) ∴ (j m)
From the categorical conjunctive:
• Some people are poets and every person breathes with their lungs
∴ So some poets breathe with their lungs
• Some animals are humans and every animal moves by will
∴ So some humans move by will
• Some roses are red and every rose is a plant
∴ So some red things are plants
From the conditional conjunctive:
• It may be that if the place is illuminated, that is from the light of a lamp. And whenever the place is illuminated, it is suitable for vision and walking in it.
∴ So it may be that if the place is illuminated by the light of a lamp, it is suitable for vision and walking in it.
• Sometimes either the prayer is bowing or prostrating. And every praying person who is bowing is humble before his Lord.
∴ So sometimes either the prayer is prostrating or humble before his Lord.
• It may be that if something is fragrant, it is a rose. And everything fragrant is given as a gift to beloved ones.
∴ So some roses are given as gifts to beloved ones.
Or ∴ So perhaps if something is a rose, it is given as a gift to beloved ones.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the fifth mood of the syllogism. The author first presents the pattern in notation: (ج م) + (ك م) ∴ (ج م), meaning a minor premise with minor term (ك) and major term (م), and a major premise with middle term (ج) and major term (م), yielding a conclusion with middle term (ج) and major term (م) — a particular affirmative. Then come examples from categorical syllogism (اقتراني حملي): three instances where a particular premise combines with a universal to produce a particular conclusion. For instance, 'Some people are poets and every person breathes with their lungs' yields 'Some poets breathe with their lungs'. The middle term here is 'person'. Next, conditional syllogism (اقتراني شرطي) examples follow. The first uses a universal conditional ('whenever the place is illuminated...') and a particular existential? Actually, it is a conditional with a possible premise. The example about the prayer uses a disjunctive premise ('either bowing or prostrating') combined with a universal affirmative to yield a disjunctive conclusion. The last example gives two possible conclusions: a particular categorical or a conditional. Each example demonstrates the same mood pattern in different propositional forms.

The Sixth Mood: (ج م)+(ك س) ∴ (ج س)
Author presents the sixth mood with categorical and conditional examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sixth mood pattern in categorical and conditional conjunctive syllogisms and formalize its categorical examples.

Core Idea
The sixth mood yields a particular negative conclusion from a particular affirmative and a universal negative premise.

In this lesson, we examine the sixth mood of the categorical syllogism, which produces a particular negative conclusion. The author presents the pattern (ج م)+(ك س) ∴ (ج س) and illustrates it with examples from both categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti) conjunctive syllogisms. By understanding this mood, you will be better equipped to analyze syllogistic arguments that combine affirmative and negative premises.

Guided Path
1
Parse the mood notation
Note that (ج م) stands for a particular affirmative premise (juz'iyya mūjiba), (ك س) for a universal negative premise (kulliyya sāliba), and the conclusion (ج س) is a particular negative. Identify the terms: ج (subject of conclusion), م (middle term), ك (predicate of major premise).
This notation is standard in Arabic logic manuals.
2
Analyze the categorical example
For 'Some roses are red and nothing from roses is blood, therefore some red things are not blood', identify the three terms: red (ج), roses (م), blood (ك). Verify that the premises match the pattern: the minor premise is (ج م) and the major is (ك س). The conclusion is (ج س).
3
Formalize the argument
Write the syllogism in standard form: Minor premise: Some roses are red (I). Major premise: No roses are blood (E). Conclusion: Some red things are not blood (O). This mood corresponds to the third figure (IEO) in traditional terminology.
The author's numbering may differ; focus on the pattern.
4
Examine the conditional examples
Notice how the same pattern applies when the first premise is conditional ('if fish is in water, it is alive') and the second is a universal negative ('nothing from fish is a bird'), leading to a negative conditional conclusion. The structural relationship between premises and conclusion remains the same.
The conditional examples show the mood's broader applicability.
5
Summarize the logical condition
The mood is valid because the particular affirmative and universal negative premises force a particular negative conclusion, given that the middle term is distributed in the universal negative premise.
This is a standard validity condition for this mood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صغرى كبرى نتيجة
و ـ على الضرب السادس منه (ج م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س)
من الاقتراني الحملي:
• بعض الورد أحمر ولا شيء من الورد بدم
∴ فبعض الأحمر ليس بدم
• بعض الماء أجاج ولا شيء من الماء بنفط
∴ فبعض الأجاج ليس بنفط
من الاقتراني الشرطي:
• ربما يكون إذا كان السمك في الماء كان حياً.
ولا شيء من السمك بطائر.
∴ فقد لا يكون إذا كان السمك حياً كان طائراً.
• قد يكون إما أن يكون الباذنجان أبيض أو أسود.
ولا شيء من الباذنجان بذي أشجار معمرة.
∴ فقد لا يكون إما أن يكون الباذنجان أسود أو ذا أشجار معمرة.
Minor premise Major premise Conclusion
W — On the sixth mood from it: (j m) + (k s) ∴ (j s)
From the categorical conjunctive:
• Some roses are red and nothing from roses is blood
∴ So some red things are not blood
• Some water is salty and nothing from water is oil
∴ So some salty things are not oil
From the conditional conjunctive:
• Perhaps if the fish is in water, it is alive.
And nothing from fish is a bird.
∴ So it is not the case that if the fish is alive, it is a bird.
• It may be that either the eggplant is white or black.
And nothing from eggplant is from long-lived trees.
∴ So it is not the case that either the eggplant is black or from long-lived trees.

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the sixth mood of the syllogism. The pattern is (ج م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س), where 'س' likely stands for a negative predicate. This mood yields a particular negative conclusion. The author first gives categorical examples: 'Some roses are red and no rose is blood, therefore some red things are not blood.' The middle term is 'rose'. The negative premise uses the universal negative 'لا شيء من الورد بدم'. Then conditional examples: a conditional premise 'perhaps if the fish is in water, it is alive' combined with 'nothing from fish is a bird' yields a negative conditional conclusion. The second conditional example uses a disjunctive premise 'either white or black' with a universal negative to negate one of the disjuncts. Each example follows the same mood pattern.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Identifying the Fourth Figure of the Syllogism
Author defines the fourth figure by the position of the middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth figure of a categorical syllogism by checking the position of the middle term.

Core Idea
The fourth figure is defined by the middle term being subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major.

After mastering the first three figures, the fourth figure completes the set. Its distinguishing feature is the placement of the middle term (الحد الأوسط). Memorizing this definition allows you to quickly classify any syllogism's figure and check its validity.

Guided Path
1
Locate the middle term
The middle term appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. Identify it first.
2
Identify the minor and major premises
The minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion; the major premise contains the predicate. In the fourth figure definition, we care about the middle term's role in each.
In standard order, the major premise is listed first, but the definition refers to 'minor' and 'major' regardless of order.
3
Check the middle term's position in the minor premise
In the fourth figure, the middle term must be the subject (موضوع) in the minor premise. See if the middle term is the subject of that statement.
4
Check the middle term's position in the major premise
In the fourth figure, the middle term must be the predicate (محمول) in the major premise. Confirm this.
5
Confirm the figure
If both conditions hold, the syllogism belongs to the fourth figure. Otherwise, it belongs to one of the other three figures.
The fourth figure is sometimes called 'Galenic' and its moods produce existential conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكل الرابع وضروبه المنتجة
وهو ما يكون الحد الأوسط فيه (موضوعاً) في الصغرى (محمولاً) في الكبرى.
The fourth figure and its productive moods:
It is that in which the middle term is (subject) in the minor [premise] and (predicate) in the major [premise].
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth figure (الشكل الرابع) of the syllogism. The author states that it is characterized by the position of the middle term: it is the subject in the minor premise (محمولاً? Actually 'موضوعاً' means subject) and predicate in the major premise (محمولاً). This is the distinguishing feature from the other three figures. The text notes that it has 'productive moods' (ضروبه المنتجة), meaning valid moods that yield conclusions. The definition is concise and serves as a memorization anchor for the student.

Key Terms
الشكل الرابع
الحد الأوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Subject in both premises
B) Predicate in both premises
C) Subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major premise
D) Predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major premise

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure is uniquely defined by the middle term being subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

(b) 'All knowledgeable people are trustworthy' (العلماء ثقات). Identify the middle term and its role in each premise. Then state the conclusion if valid.

Fourth Figure of the Categorical Syllogism: Condition and Productive Moods
Author defines fourth figure, its condition, exceptions, and lists five productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the fourth figure's non-combination condition to determine validity and list its five universally accepted productive moods.

Core Idea
The fourth figure yields valid conclusions only when its premises avoid both negation and particularity simultaneously.

In this passage, the author defines the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism and explains its unique condition for validity. Unlike the first three figures, the fourth figure is highly restricted: it generally fails if the premises combine negation and particularity in any way. However, two specific exceptions exist. After mastering this condition, you will be able to identify the five moods that are universally accepted as productive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth figure's structure
In the fourth figure, the middle term is the subject of the minor premise and the predicate of the major premise. Given a syllogism, check this structural feature.
If the middle term appears elsewhere, it is a different figure.
2
Understand the khusṭān condition
The condition for validity is that the two characteristics (khusṭān) — negation (salb) and particularity (juzʾiyya) — must not be combined. This means: do not have a particular negative premise, and do not have one premise particular and the other negative.
Negation is present in universal or particular negatives; particularity is in particular affirmatives or negatives.
3
Memorize the two exceptions
Only two premise pairs where khusṭān combine are still valid: (1) minor particular affirmative + major universal negative; (2) the reverse: minor universal negative + major particular affirmative. These are the exceptions 'by direct order and by reverse.'
The second exception is not listed in the five moods, but is added by later scholars.
4
List the five agreed productive moods
The author lists five moods from the sixteen possible that are universally accepted: (1) both U A -> P A; (2) U A + P A -> P A; (3) U A + U N -> P N; (4) U N + U A -> U N; (5) P A + U N -> P N. Learn these with their proposition types.
Use the abbreviations: U = universal, P = particular, A = affirmative, N = negative.

الشكل الرابع وضروبه المنتجة وهو ما يكون الحد الأوسط فيه (موضوعاً) في الصغرى (محمولاً) في الكبرى. ولا ينتج هذا الشكل من أشكال القياس إنتاجاً صحيحاً مطرداً إلا بتحقق ضابط واحد فيه : وهو أن لا تجتمع فيه الخستان (أي : السلب والجزئية) سواء أكان ذلك في مقدمتيه أو في مقدمة واحدة، كأن تكون الصغرى أو الكبرى (جزئية سالبة) أو إحداهما جزئية والأخرى سالبة . ويستثنى من ذلك صورتان إحداهما على الطرد والأخرى على العكس، فتجتمع الخستان في الموجبة الجزئية (صغرى) مع السالبة الكلية (كبرى) ومع ذلك تنتج، ولا تجتمع الخستان في الموجبة الجزئية (صغرى) مع الموجبة الكلية (كبرى)، ومع ذلك لا تنتج وكأن السالبة الكلية سلبت حق الموجبة الكلية هنا. وبتتبع ضروب هذا الشكل الستة عشر نلاحظ أنه لا ينتج منها إلا خمسة أضرب فقط، زادها المتأخرون ثلاثة أخرى، وهذه الثلاثة معترضة بأنها غير مطردة الإنتاج، فلنقتصر على الأضرب الخمسة المتفق عليها . الضرب مقدمته الصغرى مقدمته الكبرى إنتاجه الأول كلية موجبة (ك م) كلية موجبة (ك م) جزئية موجبة (ج م) الثاني كلية موجبة (ك م) جزئية موجبة (ج م) جزئية موجبة (ج م) الثالث كلية موجبة (ك م) كلية سالبة (ك س) جزئية سالبة (ج س) الرابع كلية سالبة (ك س) كلية موجبة (ك م) كلية سالبة (ك س) الخامس جزئية موجبة (ج م) كلية سالبة (ك س) جزئية سالبة (ج س) (١) وعلى هذا يكون شرط صاحب السلم ناقصاً إذ قال : ورابع عدم جمع الخستين إلا بصورة ففيها يستبين صغراهما موجبة جزئية كبراهما سالبة كلية ويمكن إصلاحه على الوجه التالي : ورابع عدم جمع الخستين إلا بصورتين بين الحالتين =
The fourth figure and its productive moods: It is the one in which the middle term is (subject) in the minor premise and (predicate) in the major premise. This figure does not produce valid consistent inference except by fulfilling one condition: that it does not combine the two characteristics (i.e., negation and particularity) whether in both its premises or in one premise, such as the minor or major being (particular negative) or one being particular and the other negative. Two forms are excepted from this: one by direct order and the other by reverse, so the two characteristics combine in the particular affirmative (minor) with the universal negative (major) and yet it produces, and the two characteristics do not combine in the particular affirmative (minor) with the universal affirmative (major) and yet it does not produce, as if the universal negative negated the entitlement of the universal affirmative here. By examining the sixteen moods of this figure, we note that only five moods are productive; the later scholars added three others, and these three are objected to as not consistently productive, so we confine ourselves to the five agreed-upon moods. Mood: 1st: minor universal affirmative (U A), major universal affirmative (U A), conclusion particular affirmative (P A). 2nd: minor universal affirmative (U A), major particular affirmative (P A), conclusion particular affirmative (P A). 3rd: minor universal affirmative (U A), major universal negative (U N), conclusion particular negative (P N). 4th: minor universal negative (U N), major universal affirmative (U A), conclusion universal negative (U N). 5th: minor particular affirmative (P A), major universal negative (U N), conclusion particular negative (P N). (1) According to this, the condition of the author of al-Sullam is deficient when he said: 'And the fourth avoids combining the two characteristics except in one form which clarifies its two premises: minor particular affirmative, major universal negative.' It can be corrected as follows: 'And the fourth avoids combining the two characteristics except in two forms between the two states ='

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and syllogistic analysis of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by defining the fourth figure: it is that figure where the middle term is the subject of the minor premise and the predicate of the major premise. The author then states the key condition for this figure to produce valid, consistent conclusions: it must not combine the two characteristics of negation (salb) and particularity (juz'iyya) in the same premise or across the premises. That is, you cannot have a particular negative premise alone or a particular and a negative across the two premises—except in two exceptions. The first exception is when the minor premise is a particular affirmative and the major is a universal negative, which surprisingly yields a valid conclusion. The second exception is the mirror case (presumably the reverse), but the text here is somewhat elliptical. The author contrasts this with a case where particular affirmative with universal affirmative does NOT produce a valid conclusion, showing that the universal negative 'negates the right' of the universal affirmative. After establishing the condition, the author examines the sixteen possible moods of the fourth figure and states that only five are consistently productive. He mentions that later scholars added three more, but those are objected to as not consistently productive, so he restricts attention to the five agreed-upon moods. He then presents a table listing these moods: (1) both premises universal affirmative, conclusion particular affirmative; (2) minor universal affirmative, major particular affirmative, conclusion particular affirmative; (3) minor universal affirmative, major universal negative, conclusion particular negative; (4) minor universal negative, major universal affirmative, conclusion universal negative; (5) minor particular affirmative, major universal negative, conclusion particular negative. The footnote criticizes the condition in al-Sullam by al-Akhdari, who only allowed one exception, but the author argues it should be two exceptions. The examples used throughout are drawn from natural phenomena and everyday comparisons, but the logical structure is clear and applies to any categorical syllogism in the fourth figure. In Islamic logic, this figure is used less frequently than the first, but understanding its condition is essential for evaluating arguments in fiqh and kalam where analogical reasoning is common.

B) Minor: particular affirmative, Major: universal negative
C) Minor: universal negative, Major: universal affirmative
D) Minor: particular affirmative, Major: particular affirmative

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism is productive only if its premises do not combine negation and particularity, except in two specific cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid fourth-figure syllogism in mood 4 (universal negative minor, universal affirmative major) on an Islamic legal example, such as relating to purity or marriage. Verify that it avoids the combination of khusṭān.
?

Explain your answer using the passage.

The Syllogism

The Condition for Fourth Figure Validity
Author states condition for fourth figure validity and lists five productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a fourth figure syllogism satisfies the condition of not combining negativity and particularity, and recognize the two exceptional cases.

Core Idea
The fourth figure requires that negation and particularity never meet in the premises, except in two specific cases.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism has a unique condition: it only produces a valid conclusion consistently if the premises do not combine a negative and a particular. In this lesson, we unpack this condition, see the two exceptions, and understand why this rule matters for syllogistic validity in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of al-khasataan
The two restrictions refer to negativity (salab) and particularity (juz’iyya). In the fourth figure, a valid mood must avoid having both in the premises simultaneously.
The term 'al-khasataan' is specific to this author's explanation.
2
Identify the general rule
The condition states that the two restrictions must not combine in the premises, whether in both premises or in just one. For example, a particular negative minor or major violates this.
3
Spot the exceptions
The author gives two exceptions: (1) when the minor is particular affirmative and major is universal negative (they combine but still produce), and (2) when the minor is particular affirmative and major is universal affirmative (they do not combine but fail to produce). This shows the condition is necessary but not sufficient.
4
Apply the condition to examples
Look at the provided examples: check if the combination occurs and whether the conclusion is produced. This trains you to evaluate fourth figure moods.

ولا ينتج هذا الشكل من أشكال القياس إنتاجاً صحيحاً مطرداً إلا بتحقق ضابط واحد فيه : وهو أن لا تجتمع فيه الخستان (أي : السلب والجزئية) سواء أكان ذلك في مقدمتيه أو في مقدمة واحدة، كأن تكون الصغرى أو الكبرى (جزئية سالبة) أو إحداهما جزئية والأخرى سالبة . ويستثنى من ذلك صورتان إحداهما على الطرد والأخرى على العكس، فتجتمع الخستان في الموجبة الجزئية (صغرى) مع السالبة الكلية (كبرى) ومع ذلك تنتج، ولا تجتمع الخستان في الموجبة الجزئية (صغرى) مع الموجبة الكلية (كبرى)، ومع ذلك لا تنتج وكأن السالبة الكلية سلبت حق الموجبة الكلية هنا(١). وبتتبع ضروب هذا الشكل الستة عشر نلاحظ أنه لا ينتج منها إلا خمسة أضرب فقط، زادها المتأخرون ثلاثة أخرى، وهذه الثلاثة معترضة بأنها غير مطردة الإنتاج، فلنقتصر على الأضرب الخمسة المتفق عليها . الضرب مقدمته الصغرى مقدمته الكبرى إنتاجه الأول كلية موجبة (ك م) كلية موجبة (ك م) جزئية موجبة (ج م) الثاني كلية موجبة (ك م) جزئية موجبة (ج م) جزئية موجبة (ج م) الثالث كلية موجبة (ك م) كلية سالبة (ك س) جزئية سالبة (ج س) الرابع كلية سالبة (ك س) كلية موجبة (ك م) كلية سالبة (ك س) الخامس جزئية موجبة (ج م) كلية سالبة (ك س) جزئية سالبة (ج س) ويلاحظ على هذا الشكل أنه لم ينتج إلا جزئية عدا الضرب الرابع منه، أما الضروب الأخرى العقيمة فهي مبينة مع الضروب المنتجة في الجدول التربيعي التالي: شكل ٤ (ض ح) المنتجة بالرمز مع التسلسل: ضروب الشكل الرابع (ض ٤) منتجها وعقيمها صغرى كبرى نتيجة ١- (ك م) + (ك م) ∴ (ج م) ٢- (ك م) + (ج م) ∴ (ج م) ٣- (ك م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س) ٤- (ك س) + (ك م) ∴ (ك س) ٥- (ج م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س) الكبرى الصغرى ك م ج م ك س ج س ك م ينتج ج م ينتج ج م ينتج ج س ج م ينتج ج س ك س ينتج ك س ج س الفراغ : عقيم تطبيقات على الشكل الرابع (ض ح): صغرى كبرى نتيجة أ- على الضرب الأول منه (ك م) + (ك م) ∴ (ج م) من الاقتراني الحملي: • كل من يخشى الله حقاً فهو عالم به. وكل ملتزم بأحكام الدين تماماً فهو يخشى الله حقاً. ∴ فبعض من هو عالم بالله ملتزم بأحكام الدين. • كل نجم هو زينة للسماء. وكل جرم سماوي ناري نجم. ∴ فبعض ما هو زينة للسماء جرم سماوي ناري. = موجبة جزئية ذي الصغرى كلية بحالتيها الكبرى ما اجتمعا وسقط الإنتاج واجتمعا وأنتج الحجاج أو: ما اجتمعا وضربه قد خرجا واجتمعا وضربه قد أنتجا
This figure of the syllogism does not produce a correct and consistent product except by the realization of one condition in it: that the two restrictions (i.e., negation and particularity) do not combine in it, whether that be in its two premises or in one premise, such that the minor or major is a particular negative, or one of them is particular and the other negative. Two cases are exempted from this, one consistent and the other reverse: the two restrictions combine in the particular affirmative (minor) with the universal negative (major) and yet it produces, and the two restrictions do not combine in the particular affirmative (minor) with the universal affirmative (major) and yet it does not produce, as if the universal negative negated the right of the universal affirmative here (1). By tracing the sixteen moods of this figure, we note that only five moods produce, to which the later scholars added three others, and these three are objected to as not being consistent in production, so we limit ourselves to the five agreed-upon moods. The mood, its minor premise, its major premise, its conclusion: first: universal affirmative (U A) + universal affirmative (U A) → particular affirmative (P A); second: universal affirmative (U A) + particular affirmative (P A) → particular affirmative (P A); third: universal affirmative (U A) + universal negative (U N) → particular negative (P N); fourth: universal negative (U N) + universal affirmative (U A) → universal negative (U N); fifth: particular affirmative (P A) + universal negative (U N) → particular negative (P N). It is observed regarding this figure that it only produces particulars except for the fourth mood. As for the other sterile moods, they are shown together with the productive moods in the following square table: Figure 4 (D H) the productive ones by symbol with sequence: Moods of the fourth figure (D4), their productive and sterile: minor major conclusion 1- (U A)+(U A) → (P A) 2- (U A)+(P A) → (P A) 3- (U A)+(U N) → (P N) 4- (U N)+(U A) → (U N) 5- (P A)+(U N) → (P N) The major: across the top; minor: down the side. U A, P A, U N, P N. Cells: (U A, U A) produces P A; (U A, P A) produces P A; (U A, U N) produces P N; (P A, U N) produces P N; (U N, U A) produces U N; blank: sterile. Applications on the fourth figure (D H): minor, major, conclusion. A. On the first mood (U A)+(U A) → (P A) from the categorical conjunctive: • Everyone who fears God truly is knowledgeable of Him. And every one who fully adheres to the rulings of religion truly fears God. ∴ So some of those knowledgeable of God adhere to the rulings of religion. • Every star is an adornment of the sky. And every fiery celestial body is a star. ∴ So some of what is an adornment of the sky is a fiery celestial body. = Particular affirmative with the minor universal in both its states (the two combined and production ceased, and they combined and produced argument). Or: They combined and its mood has exited, and they combined and its mood has produced.

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism, its validity condition, and its productive moods. The author begins by stating the general rule: the fourth figure only produces a correct conclusion reliably when one condition is met — that the two restrictions (al-khasataani: negativity and particularity) are not combined in the premises. This means you cannot have both a negative premise and a particular premise together, whether in both premises or in one premise (e.g., a particular negative as one premise, or one particular and the other negative). Then two exceptions are given: when the minor is a particular affirmative (J M) and the major is a universal negative (K S), the restrictions do combine but production still occurs. Conversely, when the minor is a particular affirmative (J M) and the major is a universal affirmative (K M), the restrictions do not combine, yet no production occurs. This shows that the condition is not sufficient. Next, the author notes that out of the sixteen possible moods in the fourth figure, only five are productive. Later scholars added three more, but those are criticized as inconsistent, so only the five agreed-upon moods are presented. The moods are listed in a table: (1) U A + U A → P A, (2) U A + P A → P A, (3) U A + U N → P N, (4) U N + U A → U N, (5) P A + U N → P N. The author observes that all conclusions are particular except the fourth mood, which is universal. Then a square table is provided for visual reference, with the major premises across the top and the minor down the side. Among the 16 cells, only five are productive; the rest are sterile. Finally, two applications of the first mood are given: one using a theological example about fearing God and knowing Him, and another using an astronomical example about stars and celestial bodies. The passage ends with a poetic summary line contrasting when the restrictions combine but fail to produce versus when they combine and produce. All examples are from Islamic sciences.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid fourth figure syllogism of the first mood (universal affirmative + universal affirmative → particular affirmative) on a fiqh topic. For example: 'Every prayer is an act of worship. Every act of worship requires intention. Therefore, some prayer requires intention.' Verify that it satisfies the condition.
?
Identify whether the following fourth figure syllogism satisfies the condition: 'Some animals are lawful to eat. Every lawful thing is pure. Therefore, some animals are pure.' (This is P A + U A, which is not in the list of productive moods; check if it violates the condition.)

The Five Productive Moods of the Fourth Figure
Author lists five productive moods and provides a table.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five agreed-upon productive moods of the fourth figure and identify which one yields a universal conclusion.

Core Idea
Only five out of sixteen possible fourth figure moods produce a valid conclusion, and only one gives a universal result.

After understanding the condition for the fourth figure, we now turn to the specific moods that actually produce conclusions. Out of sixteen possible combinations of premise types, only five are consistently productive. Later scholars added three more, but they are disputed. In this lesson, we memorize and analyze these five moods, noting that all but one yield particular conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the sixteen possible combinations
Each premise can be one of four types: universal affirmative (K M), particular affirmative (J M), universal negative (K S), particular negative (J S). That gives 4 × 4 = 16 possible moods for the fourth figure.
2
Identify the five productive moods from the list
The author lists: (1) K M + K M → J M; (2) K M + J M → J M; (3) K M + K S → J S; (4) K S + K M → K S; (5) J M + K S → J S. Memorize these five.
The order of premises: minor first, then major.
3
Observe the pattern of conclusions
Notice that all conclusions are particular (J M or J S) except mood 4, which yields universal negative (K S). This is because the major is universal negative and the minor universal affirmative, which reverses the typical production pattern.
4
Use the square table for verification
The author provides a table with major across the top and minor down the side. The productive cells correspond to the five moods. Practice locating them to reinforce the patterns.

ولا ينتج هذا الشكل من أشكال القياس إنتاجاً صحيحاً مطرداً إلا بتحقق ضابط واحد فيه : وهو أن لا تجتمع فيه الخستان (أي : السلب والجزئية) سواء أكان ذلك في مقدمتيه أو في مقدمة واحدة، كأن تكون الصغرى أو الكبرى (جزئية سالبة) أو إحداهما جزئية والأخرى سالبة . ويستثنى من ذلك صورتان إحداهما على الطرد والأخرى على العكس، فتجتمع الخستان في الموجبة الجزئية (صغرى) مع السالبة الكلية (كبرى) ومع ذلك تنتج، ولا تجتمع الخستان في الموجبة الجزئية (صغرى) مع الموجبة الكلية (كبرى)، ومع ذلك لا تنتج وكأن السالبة الكلية سلبت حق الموجبة الكلية هنا(١). وبتتبع ضروب هذا الشكل الستة عشر نلاحظ أنه لا ينتج منها إلا خمسة أضرب فقط، زادها المتأخرون ثلاثة أخرى، وهذه الثلاثة معترضة بأنها غير مطردة الإنتاج، فلنقتصر على الأضرب الخمسة المتفق عليها . الضرب مقدمته الصغرى مقدمته الكبرى إنتاجه الأول كلية موجبة (ك م) كلية موجبة (ك م) جزئية موجبة (ج م) الثاني كلية موجبة (ك م) جزئية موجبة (ج م) جزئية موجبة (ج م) الثالث كلية موجبة (ك م) كلية سالبة (ك س) جزئية سالبة (ج س) الرابع كلية سالبة (ك س) كلية موجبة (ك م) كلية سالبة (ك س) الخامس جزئية موجبة (ج م) كلية سالبة (ك س) جزئية سالبة (ج س) ويلاحظ على هذا الشكل أنه لم ينتج إلا جزئية عدا الضرب الرابع منه، أما الضروب الأخرى العقيمة فهي مبينة مع الضروب المنتجة في الجدول التربيعي التالي: شكل ٤ (ض ح) المنتجة بالرمز مع التسلسل: ضروب الشكل الرابع (ض ٤) منتجها وعقيمها صغرى كبرى نتيجة ١- (ك م) + (ك م) ∴ (ج م) ٢- (ك م) + (ج م) ∴ (ج م) ٣- (ك م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س) ٤- (ك س) + (ك م) ∴ (ك س) ٥- (ج م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س) الكبرى الصغرى ك م ج م ك س ج س ك م ينتج ج م ينتج ج م ينتج ج س ج م ينتج ج س ك س ينتج ك س ج س الفراغ : عقيم تطبيقات على الشكل الرابع (ض ح): صغرى كبرى نتيجة أ- على الضرب الأول منه (ك م) + (ك م) ∴ (ج م) من الاقتراني الحملي: • كل من يخشى الله حقاً فهو عالم به. وكل ملتزم بأحكام الدين تماماً فهو يخشى الله حقاً. ∴ فبعض من هو عالم بالله ملتزم بأحكام الدين. • كل نجم هو زينة للسماء. وكل جرم سماوي ناري نجم. ∴ فبعض ما هو زينة للسماء جرم سماوي ناري. = موجبة جزئية ذي الصغرى كلية بحالتيها الكبرى ما اجتمعا وسقط الإنتاج واجتمعا وأنتج الحجاج أو: ما اجتمعا وضربه قد خرجا واجتمعا وضربه قد أنتجا
This figure of the syllogism does not produce a correct and consistent product except by the realization of one condition in it: that the two restrictions (i.e., negation and particularity) do not combine in it, whether that be in its two premises or in one premise, such that the minor or major is a particular negative, or one of them is particular and the other negative. Two cases are exempted from this, one consistent and the other reverse: the two restrictions combine in the particular affirmative (minor) with the universal negative (major) and yet it produces, and the two restrictions do not combine in the particular affirmative (minor) with the universal affirmative (major) and yet it does not produce, as if the universal negative negated the right of the universal affirmative here (1). By tracing the sixteen moods of this figure, we note that only five moods produce, to which the later scholars added three others, and these three are objected to as not being consistent in production, so we limit ourselves to the five agreed-upon moods. The mood, its minor premise, its major premise, its conclusion: first: universal affirmative (U A) + universal affirmative (U A) → particular affirmative (P A); second: universal affirmative (U A) + particular affirmative (P A) → particular affirmative (P A); third: universal affirmative (U A) + universal negative (U N) → particular negative (P N); fourth: universal negative (U N) + universal affirmative (U A) → universal negative (U N); fifth: particular affirmative (P A) + universal negative (U N) → particular negative (P N). It is observed regarding this figure that it only produces particulars except for the fourth mood. As for the other sterile moods, they are shown together with the productive moods in the following square table: Figure 4 (D H) the productive ones by symbol with sequence: Moods of the fourth figure (D4), their productive and sterile: minor major conclusion 1- (U A)+(U A) → (P A) 2- (U A)+(P A) → (P A) 3- (U A)+(U N) → (P N) 4- (U N)+(U A) → (U N) 5- (P A)+(U N) → (P N) The major: across the top; minor: down the side. U A, P A, U N, P N. Cells: (U A, U A) produces P A; (U A, P A) produces P A; (U A, U N) produces P N; (P A, U N) produces P N; (U N, U A) produces U N; blank: sterile. Applications on the fourth figure (D H): minor, major, conclusion. A. On the first mood (U A)+(U A) → (P A) from the categorical conjunctive: • Everyone who fears God truly is knowledgeable of Him. And every one who fully adheres to the rulings of religion truly fears God. ∴ So some of those knowledgeable of God adhere to the rulings of religion. • Every star is an adornment of the sky. And every fiery celestial body is a star. ∴ So some of what is an adornment of the sky is a fiery celestial body. = Particular affirmative with the minor universal in both its states (the two combined and production ceased, and they combined and produced argument). Or: They combined and its mood has exited, and they combined and its mood has produced.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism, its validity condition, and its productive moods. The author begins by stating the general rule: the fourth figure only produces a correct conclusion reliably when one condition is met — that the two restrictions (al-khasataani: negativity and particularity) are not combined in the premises. This means you cannot have both a negative premise and a particular premise together, whether in both premises or in one premise (e.g., a particular negative as one premise, or one particular and the other negative). Then two exceptions are given: when the minor is a particular affirmative (J M) and the major is a universal negative (K S), the restrictions do combine but production still occurs. Conversely, when the minor is a particular affirmative (J M) and the major is a universal affirmative (K M), the restrictions do not combine, yet no production occurs. This shows that the condition is not sufficient. Next, the author notes that out of the sixteen possible moods in the fourth figure, only five are productive. Later scholars added three more, but those are criticized as inconsistent, so only the five agreed-upon moods are presented. The moods are listed in a table: (1) U A + U A → P A, (2) U A + P A → P A, (3) U A + U N → P N, (4) U N + U A → U N, (5) P A + U N → P N. The author observes that all conclusions are particular except the fourth mood, which is universal. Then a square table is provided for visual reference, with the major premises across the top and the minor down the side. Among the 16 cells, only five are productive; the rest are sterile. Finally, two applications of the first mood are given: one using a theological example about fearing God and knowing Him, and another using an astronomical example about stars and celestial bodies. The passage ends with a poetic summary line contrasting when the restrictions combine but fail to produce versus when they combine and produce. All examples are from Islamic sciences.

ضرب
المنتجة
العقيمة

Construct a valid fourth figure syllogism of mood 4 (universal negative major + universal affirmative minor → universal negative) on a fiqh topic. For example: 'No prayer is valid without intention. Every obligatory prayer is a prayer. Therefore, no obligatory prayer is valid without intention.' Verify the mood.
?
Identify which of the five productive moods is being used in the following syllogism: 'Every star is an adornment of the sky. Every fiery celestial body is a star. Therefore, some adornment of the sky is a fiery celestial body.' (Answer: Mood 1, U A + U A → P A.)

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Moods and Examples of the Fourth Figure
Author enumerates the moods of the fourth figure and provides categorical and conditional examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the productive moods of the fourth figure and determine which conclusion follows from given premises.

Core Idea
The fourth figure yields only particular conclusions, except its fourth mood which gives a universal negative.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism has a unique property: it only produces particular conclusions, except for its fourth mood. In this lesson, we will examine its moods and see examples from both categorical and conditional syllogisms, grounding them in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the figure's property
Note the statement: the fourth figure produces only particular conclusions, except the fourth mood. Understand why – the middle term positions limit distribution.
The exception is the fourth mood with universal negative major.
2
Read the mood table
Identify the five mood combinations using symbols: ك م (universal affirmative), ج م (particular affirmative), ك س (universal negative), ج س (particular negative). Determine which are productive.
Blank cells in the grid show sterile combinations.
3
Analyze a categorical example
Take the first mood example: every fearer of Allah is knowledgeable; every adherent is a fearer; therefore some knowledgeable are adherents. Confirm the premises are universal affirmative and conclusion particular affirmative.
This matches the table: (U A)+(U A) → (P A).
4
Examine conditional examples
See how the same figure works with conditional and disjunctive premises, e.g., 'Whenever the mattress is soft, it is comfortable; some bridal equipment is a soft mattress; therefore it may be that if comfortable, it is bridal equipment.'
Conditional syllogisms also follow fourth figure mood rules.
5
Practice with a new pair
Given a pair of premises, determine if they match a productive mood of the fourth figure and what conclusion follows.
Use the table as reference.

From the categorical syllogism:
• Every milk is white, and some edible things are white.
.. So some white things are edible.

.. So it may be that if the mattress is more comfortable, it is bridal equipment.

.. So it may be that either the child is successful in his life or not talented.

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth figure of the syllogism (الشكل الرابع) and its moods with examples. The author begins by noting a key property: the fourth figure produces only particular conclusions except for the fourth mood, which yields a universal negative. A table is provided listing the five possible mood combinations (minor premise + major premise → conclusion) using symbolic notation: ك م (universal affirmative), ج م (particular affirmative), ك س (universal negative), ج س (particular negative). The table shows which moods are productive (produce a valid conclusion) and which are sterile (do not). For example, mood 1: universal affirmative minor + universal affirmative major produces particular affirmative conclusion. Mood 4: universal negative minor + universal affirmative major produces universal negative conclusion. The table also includes a grid showing when two premises of various types yield a conclusion; blank spaces indicate sterile combinations. The author then provides applications (تطبيقات) for the first two moods, giving examples from both categorical syllogisms (اقتراني حملي) and conditional syllogisms (اقتراني شرطي). For the first mood, a categorical example: 'Everyone who fears Allah is knowledgeable of Him; everyone who fully adheres to religion fears Allah; therefore some knowledgeable people adhere to religion.' The conclusion is particular affirmative because the middle term is distributed differently. The conditional examples involve hypothetical and disjunctive premises. For the second mood, similar examples are given, emphasizing that the conclusion remains particular affirmative. The passage effectively illustrates how to apply the rules of the fourth figure.

The Syllogism

The Connected Conditional Syllogism (Muttasil)
First example of a connected conditional syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a connected conditional syllogism (muttasil) and extract its antecedent, consequent, and conclusion.

Core Idea
A connected conditional uses an 'if-then' link between premises.

The first example in the text uses 'wherever you are' as a conditional connection. Let's dissect how this type of syllogism works and how to recognize its parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
Find the premise that contains the conditional link (often with 'if' or 'wherever'). Here it is 'Wherever you are, death will overtake you'. The antecedent is 'you are at a place', the consequent is 'death overtakes you'.
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise is 'All your appointed times come wherever you are'. It introduces another conditional with the same antecedent (place).
3
Extract the conclusion
The conclusion is a new conditional: 'It may be that if death overtakes you, your appointed times come'. Notice it combines the consequents of the two premises.
4
Determine the type of conditional syllogism
Since both premises are conditionals and the conclusion is also a conditional, this is a connected conditional conjunctive (iqtirani sharti muttasil).
This is different from istithna'i (exceptive) syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الاقتراني الشرطي:
• أينما تكونوا يدرككم الموت وكل آجالكم تأتي أينما تكونون
.. قد يكون إذا أدرككم الموت جاءت آجالكم.
• دائماً إما أن يكون الحي ذا بصر أو أعمى.
وكل من يرى بالنظارات فهو ذو بصر.
.. فقد يكون إما أن يكون الحي أعمى أو يرى بالنظارات.
From the conditional conjunctive [syllogism]:
• Wherever you are, death will overtake you, and all your appointed times come wherever you are
.. so it may be that if death overtakes you, your appointed times come.
• Always, either the animal has sight or is blind.
And everyone who sees with glasses has sight.
.. so it may be that either the animal is blind or sees with glasses.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage provides two examples of conditional (sharti) syllogisms. The first is a conditional conjunctive (mutasil) syllogism: the major premise is 'Wherever you are, death will overtake you' (an if-then type), the minor premise is 'all your appointed times come wherever you are', leading to the conclusion 'it may be that if death overtakes you, your appointed times come.' The second is a disjunctive (munfasil) syllogism: the major premise is 'Always, either the animal has sight or is blind', the minor is 'everyone who sees with glasses has sight', and the conclusion is 'so it may be that either the animal is blind or sees with glasses.' The author labels the parts as minor premise (صغرى), major premise (كبرى), and conclusion (نتيجة) in the layout. These examples illustrate how conditional syllogisms are constructed. Note that the conclusions are expressed as possibilities ('قد يكون' or 'فقد يكون').

Key Terms
الاقتراني الشرطي
مقدم
تالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) You are at a place
D) It may be that death overtakes you

Key Takeaway
A connected conditional syllogism has both premises as conditionals and yields a new conditional conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Disjunctive Conditional Syllogism (Munfasil)
Second example of a disjunctive conditional syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a disjunctive conditional syllogism (munfasil) and extract its disjuncts, minor premise, and conclusion.

Core Idea
A disjunctive conditional uses an 'either-or' major premise to eliminate possibilities.

The second example in the text uses 'always, either the animal has sight or is blind'. This is a disjunctive syllogism. Let's see how its parts combine to reach a conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive major premise
Find the premise that presents a choice: 'Always, either the animal has sight or is blind'. The two disjuncts are 'has sight' and 'is blind'.
This is a real disjunction (mani'at al-jam' wal-khalā' – exclusive or).
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise is 'Everyone who sees with glasses has sight'. This affirms one of the disjuncts: 'has sight'.
3
Derive the conclusion
Since the minor premise affirms one disjunct, the conclusion negates the other: 'so it may be that either the animal is blind or sees with glasses' – effectively, if the animal sees with glasses, it is not blind; but the conclusion restates the disjunction with a new disjunct 'sees with glasses' representing having sight.
The conclusion is a possibility ('قد يكون') and rephrases the original disjunction.
4
Determine the form of the syllogism
This is a disjunctive conditional conjunctive (iqtirani sharti munfasil). The major premise is a disjunction, and the minor premise eliminates one option, leading to the other.
In standard logic, affirming one disjunct in an exclusive or does not necessarily deny the other, but the author presents it as valid here.

• أينما تكونوا يدرككم الموت وكل آجالكم تأتي أينما تكونون
.. قد يكون إذا أدرككم الموت جاءت آجالكم.

• Wherever you are, death will overtake you, and all your appointed times come wherever you are
.. so it may be that if death overtakes you, your appointed times come.

And everyone who sees with glasses has sight.

Commentary
This passage provides two examples of conditional (sharti) syllogisms. The first is a conditional conjunctive (mutasil) syllogism: the major premise is 'Wherever you are, death will overtake you' (an if-then type), the minor premise is 'all your appointed times come wherever you are', leading to the conclusion 'it may be that if death overtakes you, your appointed times come.' The second is a disjunctive (munfasil) syllogism: the major premise is 'Always, either the animal has sight or is blind', the minor is 'everyone who sees with glasses has sight', and the conclusion is 'so it may be that either the animal is blind or sees with glasses.' The author labels the parts as minor premise (صغرى), major premise (كبرى), and conclusion (نتيجة) in the layout. These examples illustrate how conditional syllogisms are constructed. Note that the conclusions are expressed as possibilities ('قد يكون' or 'فقد يكون').
