Second Figure Syllogisms: Universal and Particular Affirmative Mood
Author applies the second figure syllogism with categorical and conditional examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second figure syllogism with mood (ك م)+(ج م)→(ج م) and construct a valid example from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The second figure can yield an affirmative particular conclusion even though both premises are affirmative, through conversion.

In this passage, the author applies the second figure using the mood 'kulli mujab' (universal affirmative) as major and 'juz'i mujab' (particular affirmative) as minor, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. The examples range from categorical to conditional syllogisms. We will focus on the categorical pattern and learn how to recognise and construct similar arguments using Islamic concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. For example, in 'Every prophet is truthful' and 'Some men are truthful', the middle term is 'truthful'.
2
Extract the mood notation
The major premise is universal affirmative (ك م), the minor is particular affirmative (ج م), and the conclusion is particular affirmative (ج م). The notation is (ك م)+(ج م)→(ج م).
3
Check the conclusion through conversion
The conclusion is the converse of the minor premise. Since the minor is a particular affirmative, its converse is also valid and yields the same truth value.
4
Construct an Islamic example
Using Islamic terms, create a valid second figure syllogism: Major: 'Every prophet is truthful' (ك م), Minor: 'Some men are truthful' (ج م), Conclusion: 'Some truthful are men' (ج م).
Ensure the middle term is the predicate in both premises.

.. فبعض الأبيض مأكول

B - Applications of the second figure (kulli mujab) + (juz'i mujab) → (juz'i mujab)
From the categorical conjunctive [syllogism]:
• Every milk is white and some edible substance is white
.. so some white is edible.
• Every camel has a long neck and some of what is sold in the butcher's market is a camel
.. so some long-necked are sold in the butcher's market.

.. so some that have a calcareous shell break.

.. so some who are prepared to have a male partner are affectionate.
From the conditional conjunctive [syllogism]:
• Whenever the bed is soft, it is more comfortable.
And some wedding furniture is a soft bed.
.. so it may be that if the bed is more comfortable, it is wedding furniture.
• Always either the boy is noble or not noble.
And some successful in their lives are noble.
.. so it may be: either the boy is successful in his life or not noble.

The author gives four examples from categorical syllogisms: e.g., 'Every milk is white, and some edible substance is white, therefore some white is edible.' These examples follow the second figure where the middle term (e.g., 'white') is predicated in both premises. Then two examples of conditional syllogisms in the same figure are given: one conditional conjunctive (mutasil) with 'كلما' (whenever) and one disjunctive (munfasil) with 'دائماً إما' (always either). The conclusions are expressed with 'فقد يكون' (so it may be) indicating possibility. The lesson demonstrates how the same figure applies to both hamli and sharti syllogisms.

Identifying and Validating the Fourth Figure Syllogism
Author applies the fourth figure syllogism with categorical examples only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a fourth figure categorical syllogism and verify its mood (ك س)+(ك م)→(ك س) in any example.

Core Idea
Fourth figure mood (ك س)+(ك م)→(ك س) yields a valid universal negative conclusion.

This lesson demonstrates the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism using two concrete examples: one from biology and one from Islamic eschatology. You will learn to recognize the pattern, extract the mood, and confirm the conclusion follows necessarily.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three propositions
Locate the two premises and the conclusion. Ensure each is a categorical proposition (subject-copula-predicate). In the first example: 'No human is a fish' (major premise), 'Every laughing thing is human' (minor premise), 'No fish is laughing' (conclusion).
2
Classify each proposition by quantity and quality
Determine if each proposition is universal affirmative (ك م) or universal negative (ك س). The major and conclusion are universal negative (ك س); the minor is universal affirmative (ك م).
3
Find the middle term
The middle term appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. In the first example, 'human' appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. In the fourth figure (as defined by the textbook), the middle term is the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise.
In the first example, 'human' is subject of the major ('No human...') and predicate of the minor ('Every laughing thing is human').
4
Verify the mood and figure
The mood is (ك س)+(ك م)→(ك س). Check that the premises match this pattern. The figure is fourth because the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor.
5
Confirm validity
In the fourth figure, this mood is valid: if no humans are fish and all laughing things are human, then necessarily no laughing thing is a fish. The same structure holds for the Judgment Day example.
The second example uses the same mood: No parent avails his child; every ancestor is a parent; therefore no ancestor avails his descendant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
د - تطبيقات على الضرب الرابع منه (ك س) + (ك م) ∴ (ك س)
من الاقتراني الحملي:
• لا أحد من الناس هو سمك وكل ضاحك هو من الناس
∴ فلا أحد من السمك هو ضاحك
• لا يجزي والد عن ولده يوم القيامة شيئاً.
وكل واحد من الأصول والد.
∴ فلا جازي يوم القيامة أحدٌ من الأصول عن أحد من الفروع.

From the categorical conjunctive [syllogism]:
• No human is a fish, and every laughing [thing] is human
∴ so no fish is laughing.
• No parent will avail his child on the Day of Resurrection at all.
And every one of the ancestors is a parent.
∴ so no one on the Day of Resurrection will avail one of the descendants.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth figure with mood (ك س)+(ك م)→(ك س): major universal negative, minor universal affirmative, conclusion universal negative. Two examples are given: e.g., no human is a fish, every laughing thing is human, therefore no fish is laughing. The second example uses a Quranic concept of intercession on Judgment Day. Note that only categorical (hamli) examples are provided for this figure; no conditional example is included.

Third Figure Syllogisms: Categorical and Conditional Applications
Applying the third figure mood with categorical and conditional examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the mood (ك م)+(ك س) in both categorical and conditional syllogisms and produce the correct particular negative conclusion in Islamic reasoning contexts.

Core Idea
The third figure with a universal affirmative major and universal negative minor always yields a particular negative conclusion.

This passage demonstrates how the third figure mood works with both categorical and conditional premises. Understanding this pattern helps you recognize valid syllogistic forms in Islamic theological and juristic arguments such as qiyas (analogy) and proofs for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the mood label (ك م)+(ك س)
Identify that the major premise is universal affirmative (ك م) and the minor premise is universal negative (ك س). The conclusion is particular negative (ج س).
The symbols are derived from Arabic: ك for كلي (universal), م for موجبة (affirmative), س for سالبة (negative), ج for جزئية (particular).
2
Extract the terms from a categorical example
Take the camel example: major term 'hoofed animal' (predicate of conclusion), minor term 'long-necked' (subject of conclusion), middle term 'camel' (appears in both premises). Verify the mood: major is universal affirmative, minor is universal negative.
3
Verify the conclusion is particular negative
Check that the conclusion uses 'some...not' (the particular negative). In the camel example, it is 'some of what has a long neck is not from the hoofed animals'.
4
Apply to conditional examples
Notice that the same pattern appears with conditional and disjunctive premises. The major is a universal conditional/affirmative, the minor is universal negative, and the conclusion is a particular negative conditional.
The conditional examples use the same logical structure even though the propositions are conditional or disjunctive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جـ - تطبيقات على الضرب الثالث منه (ك م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س)
من الاقتراني الحملي :
• كل جمل ذو رقبة طويلة ولا شيء من ذوات الحافر بجمل
∴ فبعض ما هو ذو رقبة طويلة ليس من ذوات الحافر.
• كل عالم أرفع منزلة من الجاهل ولا واحد من الثيران بعالم.
∴ فبعض من هو أرفع منزلة من الجاهل ليس بثور.
من الاقتراني الشرطي :
• كلما كان الظل ممتداً فالشمس بعيدة عن وسط السماء.
ولا شيء من الهواء له ظل يمتد.
∴ فقد لا يكون إذا كانت الشمس بعيدة عن وسط السماء كان الهواء له ظل.
• في كل الأحيان إما أن يكون الحي بصيراً أو أعمى.
ولا شيء من حيوان الخُلد ببصير.
∴ فقد لا يكون إما أن يكون الحي أعمى أو هو خلد.
C - Applications of the third figure of it (universal affirmative major + universal affirmative minor = particular negative conclusion) from categorical syllogisms: • Every camel has a long neck and none of the hoofed animals is a camel. Therefore some of what has a long neck is not from the hoofed animals. • Every scholar is more honorable than the ignorant and no ox is a scholar. Therefore some of who is more honorable than the ignorant is not an ox. From conditional syllogisms: • Whenever the shadow is extended, the sun is far from the middle of the sky. And no air has an extended shadow. Therefore it may not be that when the sun is far from the middle of the sky, the air has a shadow. • At all times, either the living being is sighted or blind. And none of the mole animals are sighted. Therefore it may not be that either the living being is blind or it is a mole.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage provides applications of the third figure mood (ك م)+(ك س) producing a particular negative conclusion (ج س). The author gives two categorical examples: first, every camel has a long neck and no hoofed animal is a camel, so some long-necked are not hoofed; second, every scholar is more honorable than the ignorant and no ox is a scholar, so some honorable ones are not oxen. Then two conditional examples: first, whenever the shadow is extended the sun is far from the middle, and no air has an extended shadow, so it may not be that when the sun is far the air has a shadow; second, always either the living is sighted or blind, and no mole is sighted, so it may not be that either the living is blind or it is a mole. The conditional examples use the istithna'i form, but the structure is analogous to the categorical third figure.

Applications of the Fourth Figure Mood (ج م)+(ك س)→(ج س)
Applying fourth figure mood with particular affirmative major and universal negative minor yielding particular negative conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth figure mood in categorical and conditional syllogisms and verify that the conclusion follows from the premises.

Core Idea
A particular affirmative and universal negative combine to give a particular negative conclusion in the fourth figure.

We now apply the fourth figure mood with concrete examples from everyday and religious concepts. The examples include categorical and conditional forms, showing the versatility of this mood. Pay close attention to how the 'particular affirmative major' and 'universal negative minor' yield a 'particular negative conclusion' in each case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
The major premise must be a particular affirmative statement. In Arabic, look for expressions like 'بعض' (some) for categorical or 'قد يكون' (it may be) for conditional. This premise carries the major term, which is the predicate of the conclusion.
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise must be a universal negative statement. Look for 'لا أحد/ولا شيء' (no/nothing) for categorical or 'ولا ليل موجود' (and no night exists) for conditional. This premise contains the minor term, the subject of the conclusion.
3
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is always a particular negative statement. In categorical, it appears as 'بعض... ليس...' (some... are not...); in conditional, as 'قد لا يكون' (it may not be). Verify that it follows from the premises by checking the mood pattern.
4
Verify the mood pattern
Ensure the premises and conclusion match the fourth figure mood: particular affirmative major (ج م) + universal negative minor (ك س) → particular negative conclusion (ج س). This pattern is valid regardless of the content.
5
Note the conditional analogy
The conditional examples (with 'qad yakun' and 'la yujad') follow the same logical pattern. They demonstrate that the fourth figure mood applies not only to categorical but also to conditional propositions, expanding its utility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هـ - تطبيقات على الضرب الرابع منه (ج م)+(ك س) .. (ج س)
من الاقتراني الحملي :
• بعض الناس مؤمنون ولا أحد من الملائكة بإنسان
.. فبعض المؤمنين ليسوا بملائكة
• بعض اللحم حلال أكله ولا شيء من القثاء بلحم
.. فبعض ما هو حلال أكله ليس بقثاء
من الاقتراني الشرطي :
• قد يكون إذا كانت الشمس طالعة في السماء فالأرض مضيئة بها .
ولا ليل موجود تطلع فيه الشمس في السماء
.. فقد لا يكون إذا كانت الأرض مضيئة بالشمس فالليل موجود
• أحياناً إما أن يكون العابد صائماً أو قائماً في الليل .
ولا أحد يشرب الماء هو صائم .
.. فقد لا يكون : إما أن يكون العابد قائماً في الليل أو يشرب الماء .
E - Applications of the fourth figure of it (particular affirmative major + universal negative minor = particular negative conclusion) from categorical syllogisms: • Some people are believers and none of the angels is a human. Therefore some believers are not angels. • Some meat is permissible to eat and nothing of the cucumber is meat. Therefore some of what is permissible to eat is not cucumber. From conditional syllogisms: • It may be that when the sun is rising in the sky, the earth is illuminated by it. And no night exists in which the sun rises in the sky. Therefore it may not be that when the earth is illuminated by the sun, the night exists. • Sometimes either the worshiper is fasting or standing in the night. And no one who drinks water is fasting. Therefore it may not be that either the worshiper is standing in the night or drinks water.

Commentary
This passage applies the fourth figure mood with particular affirmative major (ج م) and universal negative minor (ك س) yielding a particular negative conclusion (ج س). Two categorical examples: First, some people are believers and no angel is human, so some believers are not angels. Second, some meat is halal to eat and no cucumber is meat, so some halal food is not cucumber. Two conditional examples: First, it may be that when the sun rises, the earth is lit, and no night has a sun rising, so it may not be that when the earth is lit, night exists. Second, sometimes the worshiper is fasting or standing at night, and no drinker of water is fasting, so it may not be that he is standing or drinks water. The conditional examples again mimic the categorical structure using possibility and negation.

The Syllogism

The Fourth Mood in Categorical Syllogisms
Categorical examples of the fourth mood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth mood (Ferio) in categorical syllogisms and verify that the premises match the EIO pattern.

Core Idea
The fourth mood (Ferio) combines a universal negative major with a particular affirmative minor to yield a particular negative conclusion.

In this passage, the author provides two categorical examples that illustrate the fourth mood. The second example, involving meat and cucumber, is particularly clear. Let's examine it step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise (particular affirmative)
Find the premise containing 'some' (بعض). In the second example, 'بعض اللحم حلال أكله' (Some meat is permissible to eat) is the minor premise. It is particular and affirmative.
2
Identify the major premise (universal negative)
Find the premise containing 'no' or 'nothing' (لا شيء). Here, 'ولا شيء من القثاء بلحم' (Nothing of cucumber is meat) is the major premise. It is universal and negative.
3
Identify the conclusion (particular negative)
The conclusion starts with 'some...not' (فبعض ما هو حلال أكله ليس بقثاء). It is particular and negative.
4
Verify the mood: major E, minor I, conclusion O
The mood is EIO (Ferio). The major is universal negative (E), the minor is particular affirmative (I), the conclusion is particular negative (O). This is a valid first-figure syllogism provided the middle term is properly placed.
In this example, the middle term 'meat' is the predicate of the major and subject of the minor, which is correct for Ferio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هـ ـ تطبيقات على الضرب الرابع منه (ج م) + (ك س) .. (ج س)
من الاقتراني الشرطي :
• ليس البتة كلما كان العدد فرداً كان منقسماً بمتساويين صحيحين .
وكل عدد إما زوج وإما فرد .
.. فليس البتة إما أن يكون العدد منقسماً بمتساويين أو هو زوج .
• ليس البتة كلما كان النبات زهراً فهو ذو رائحة طيبة .
وكل تفتح الثمر قبل عقده زهر .
.. فليس البتة كلما كان النبات ذا رائحة طيبة فهو تفتح الثمر قبل عقده .
صغرى كبرى نتيجة
من الاقتراني الحملي :
• بعض الناس مؤمنون ولا أحد من الملائكة بإنسان
.. فبعض المؤمنين ليسوا بملائكة
• بعض اللحم حلال أكله ولا شيء من القثاء بلحم
.. فبعض ما هو حلال أكله ليس بقثاء
من الاقتراني الشرطي :
• قد يكون إذا كانت الشمس طالعة في السماء فالأرض مضيئة بها .
ولا ليل موجود تطلع فيه الشمس في السماء
.. فقد لا يكون إذا كانت الأرض مضيئة بالشمس فالليل موجود
• أحياناً إما أن يكون العابد صائماً أو قائماً في الليل .
ولا أحد يشرب الماء هو صائم .
.. فقد لا يكون : إما أن يكون العابد قائماً في الليل أو يشرب الماء .
H. Applications of the fourth mood of it (J M) + (K S) .. (J S)
From the conditional conjunctive syllogism:
• Not at all whenever a number is odd it is divisible by two equal integers.
And every number is either even or odd.
.. So not at all either the number is divisible by two equals or it is even.
• Not at all whenever a plant is a flower it has a pleasant smell.
And every opening of fruit before its setting is a flower.
.. So not at all whenever a plant has a pleasant smell it is the opening of fruit before its setting.
Minor, major, conclusion
From the categorical syllogism:
• Some people are believers and no angel is a human.
.. So some believers are not angels.
• Some meat is permissible to eat and nothing of cucumber is meat.
.. So some of what is permissible to eat is not cucumber.
From the conditional syllogism:
• It may be that if the sun is rising in the sky then the earth is illuminated by it.
And there is no night in which the sun rises in the sky.
.. So it may not be that if the earth is illuminated by the sun then the night exists.
• Sometimes it is either that the worshiper is fasting or standing in the night.
And no one who drinks water is fasting.
.. So it may not be: either the worshiper is standing in the night or drinks water.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents applications of the fourth mood of syllogism, which is symbolized as (particular affirmative minor plus universal negative major yields particular negative conclusion). The author first illustrates this mood in conditional disjunctive syllogisms. The first conditional example: 'Not at all whenever a number is odd it is divisible by two equal integers. And every number is either even or odd. Therefore, not at all either the number is divisible by two equals or it is even.' This shows the pattern where the minor is a conditional (with 'not at all every...' indicating a universal negative? Actually the translation shows 'not at all whenever' which is a universal negative conditional). The second conditional example follows the same pattern. Then the author gives two categorical examples: 'Some people are believers and no angel is a human. Therefore, some believers are not angels.' This fits the (ج م)+(ك س)→(ج س) pattern: minor is particular affirmative, major is universal negative, conclusion is particular negative. The second categorical example uses meat and cucumber. Finally, the author gives two more conditional examples, but this time with 'قد يكون' (it may be) and 'أحياناً' (sometimes), which are particular affirmative conditional and disjunctive premises. The pattern remains: particular affirmative minor and universal negative major yield a particular negative conclusion. Note that in conditional logic, the premises are not simply categorical propositions but conditional or disjunctive statements. The author is careful to show that the same mood can be applied to different types of propositions, strengthening the understanding of the logical form.

صغرى

Key Takeaway

The Fourth Mood in Conditional Syllogisms
Conditional and disjunctive examples of the fourth mood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth mood pattern in conditional and disjunctive syllogisms.

Core Idea
The fourth mood works with conditional and disjunctive propositions too, as long as the quantifier pattern is maintained.

The author extends the fourth mood to syllogisms whose premises are not categorical but conditional or disjunctive. These examples show that the same logical form holds when the minor is a particular conditional or disjunctive and the major is a universal negative categorical.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise as a particular conditional or disjunctive
Look for phrases indicating particularity, such as 'قد يكون' (it may be) or 'أحياناً' (sometimes). In the third example, 'قد يكون إذا كانت الشمس طالعة في السماء فالأرض مضيئة بها' is a particular affirmative conditional.
2
Identify the major premise as a universal negative categorical
Find a universal negative categorical statement, often starting with 'لا' or 'ولا'. In the third example, 'ولا ليل موجود تطلع فيه الشمس في السماء' (No night exists in which the sun rises) is universal negative.
3
Identify the conclusion as a particular negative conditional or disjunctive
The conclusion begins with 'فقد لا يكون' (so it may not be) or 'فقد لا يكون : إما أن يكون' indicating a particular negative. In the third example, the conclusion is 'فقد لا يكون إذا كانت الأرض مضيئة بالشمس فالليل موجود' (a particular negative conditional).
4
Check that the terms align analogously to the categorical Ferio
The middle term is the 'sun rising' (or the condition) connecting the minor and major. The pattern of quantifiers (particular affirmative minor, universal negative major, particular negative conclusion) mirrors the categorical Ferio.

The Syllogism

The Four Figures and Their Productive Moods
Tabulated summary of the four figures and their productive moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure of a categorical syllogism from the position of the middle term and determine whether a given mood is productive using the conditions provided.

Core Idea
Knowing the four figures and their conditions lets you instantly check the validity of any categorical syllogism.

This passage is a mnemonic summary used by classical logicians to memorize all valid syllogistic forms. You will learn to read the Arabic symbols for quantity and quality, identify figure positions, and apply the productivity conditions. This is the cornerstone of qiyas (syllogistic reasoning) in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Decode the Arabic symbols
Identify what the letters ك, ج, م, س stand for: ك = universal (kulliyya), ج = particular (juz'iyya), م = affirmative (mujiba), س = negative (saliba). In the text, (ك م) means universal affirmative, (ك س) universal negative, etc.
These symbols appear in parentheses for each premise and conclusion.
2
Determine the figure from its positional symbol
Each figure is given a two-letter code indicating the position of the middle term: first figure (ح ض), second (ع ع), third (ض ض), fourth (ض ح). Match each to the description: first figure middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor.
The passage lists figures as الأول (ح ض), الثاني (ع ع), etc.
3
Identify the productivity condition for each figure
For the first figure, condition: minor must be affirmative and major universal. Second: premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and major universal. Third: minor affirmative and at least one premise universal. Fourth: not both negative nor both particular (except two exceptions).
These conditions are given under ضابط إنتاجه for each figure.
4
List the valid moods from the tables
Using the tables, enumerate the premise combinations that produce a conclusion. For example, first figure has four moods: (ك م)+(ك م), (ك م)+(ك س), (ج م)+(ك م), (ج م)+(ك س). Note the conclusion for each.
The mood is the combination of quantity and quality of minor and major.
5
Apply to a real Islamic syllogism
Take a classic kalam argument (e.g., 'The world is temporally originated (ج م), everything temporal has a creator (ك م), so the world has a creator (ج م)'). Write it symbolically and check if it matches a valid mood in the appropriate figure.
Ensure minor and major are correctly identified: minor is the premise with the subject of the conclusion.

٣- (ج م) + (ك م) ∴ (ج م)
٤- (ج م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س)
ب- ضروب الشكل الثاني المنتجة (أربعة)
(ع ع) صغرى كبرى نتيجة ضابط إنتاجه

٢- (ك س) + (ك م) ∴ (ك س) اختلاف مقدمتيه أن تكون كبراه

٣- (ك س) + (ك م) ∴ (ج س) ١- (ج م) + (ك س) فتنتج رغم أن حذف
٤- (ك س) + (ج م) ∴ (ج س) الشرط وهي الضرب الخامس
٥- (ج م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س) ٢- (ج م) + (ك م) فلا تنتج رغم تحقق الشرط
General summary of the four figures and their productive moods with symbols
Figures: First Second Third Fourth
(ح ض) (ع ع) (ض ض) (ض ح)
A. Productive moods of the first figure (four)
(ح ض) Minor Major Conclusion Condition for its productivity
1- (K M) + (K M) ∴ (K M) 1 2
2- (K M) + (K S) ∴ (K S) Affirmative of minor, universal of major
3- (J M) + (K M) ∴ (J M)
4- (J M) + (K S) ∴ (J S)
B. Productive moods of the second figure (four)
(ع ع) Minor Major Conclusion Condition for its productivity
1- (K M) + (K S) ∴ (K S) 1 2
2- (K S) + (K M) ∴ (K S) Difference of its premises: that its major be
3- (J M) + (K S) ∴ (J S) universal in affirmation and negation (quality)
4- (J S) + (K M) ∴ (J S)
C. Productive moods of the third figure (six)
(ض ض) Minor Major Conclusion Condition for its productivity
1- (K M) + (K M) ∴ (J M) 1 2
2- (K M) + (K S) ∴ (J S) Affirmative of minor, universal of one
3- (J M) + (K M) ∴ (J M) of the two premises
4- (K M) + (J M) ∴ (J M)
5- (J M) + (K M) ∴ (J M)
6- (J M) + (K S) ∴ (J S)
D. Productive moods of the fourth figure (five)
(ض ح) Minor Major Conclusion Condition for its productivity
1- (K M) + (K M) ∴ (J M) That the two 'khishtayn' (negation and particularity) are not combined
2- (K M) + (J M) ∴ (J M) And two cases are excepted:
3- (K S) + (K M) ∴ (J S) 1- (J M) + (K S) produces despite the condition being removed, which is the fifth mood
4- (K S) + (J M) ∴ (J S) 2- (J M) + (K M) does not produce despite the condition being met
5- (J M) + (K S) ∴ (J S)
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage provides a compact summary of the four figures of the syllogism and their valid moods, each represented by symbolic notation. The author uses Arabic letters: 'ك' for universal (kulliyya), 'ج' for particular (juz'iyya), 'م' for affirmative (mujiba), 'س' for negative (saliba). The summary begins by labeling each figure with a pair of letters indicating the position of the middle term in the premises: first figure (ح ض) where the middle term is subject of the major and predicate of the minor; second figure (ع ع) where the middle term is predicate in both; third figure (ض ض) where the middle term is subject in both; fourth figure (ض ح) where the middle term is predicate of the minor and subject of the major. For the first figure, there are four productive moods, and the condition is that the minor must be affirmative and the major universal. The moods are: 1. Universal affirmative minor + Universal affirmative major → Universal affirmative conclusion. 2. Universal affirmative + Universal negative → Universal negative. 3. Particular affirmative + Universal affirmative → Particular affirmative. 4. Particular affirmative + Universal negative → Particular negative. The second figure has four moods with the condition that the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major must be universal. The moods are: 1. Universal affirmative + Universal negative → Universal negative. 2. Universal negative + Universal affirmative → Universal negative. 3. Particular affirmative + Universal negative → Particular negative. 4. Particular negative + Universal affirmative → Particular negative. The third figure has six moods; the condition is that the minor must be affirmative and one premise must be universal. The moods list various combinations: two universal premises produce a particular conclusion; one universal and one particular produce a particular conclusion. The fourth figure has five moods, with the general condition that the two premises cannot both be negative or both particular (i.e., the two 'khishtayn' of negativity and particularity cannot combine). However, there are two exceptions: (1) (J M)+(K S) produces despite the condition being violated (this is the fifth mood), and (2) (J M)+(K M) does not produce despite the condition being satisfied. This summary serves as a mnemonic for valid syllogistic forms.

ضابط إنتاجه

Symbolic Notation and the Four Figures
Introduction to symbolic notation and figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decode the symbolic notation (ك م, ك س, ج م, ج س) for categorical propositions and identify the arrangement of terms in each of the four syllogistic figures.

Core Idea
Every categorical syllogism has a figure determined by the position of the middle term.

This passage uses a compact symbolic shorthand to represent all possible valid syllogisms. Before we can apply these moods to Islamic theological or legal arguments, we must first learn how to read the symbols and understand what makes a figure distinct. Let's break down the notation and the four figures step by step.

Guided Path
1
Decode the symbols
The text uses (ك م) for universal affirmative (every S is P), (ك س) for universal negative (no S is P), (ج م) for particular affirmative (some S is P), (ج س) for particular negative (some S is not P). Identify these from the Arabic: ك = كلي, ج = جزئي, م = موجب, س = سالب.
These are standard in the tradition.
2
Understand the figure notation
The four figures are listed as الأول, الثاني, الثالث, الرابع. Each figure has a specific pattern of premise placement: first figure (ح ض) means minor premise first, major premise second (the natural order). The second figure (ع ع) has both premises with the middle term as predicate in both. The third figure (ض ض) has the middle term as subject in both. The fourth figure (ض ح) has the middle term as predicate in the minor and subject in the major.
The letters ح and ض are abbreviations for حمل (predicate) and وضع (subject) or maybe else; but the pattern is clear.
3
Identify the defining feature of each figure
Figure depends on the position of the middle term. In first figure: middle is subject of major and predicate of minor. Second: middle is predicate of both. Third: middle is subject of both. Fourth: middle is predicate of minor and subject of major.
Memorize these patterns.

١- (ك م) + (ك م) ∴ (ج م) أن لا تجتمع فيه الخستان (السلب والجزئية)
٢- (ك م) + (ج م) ∴ (ج م) ويستثنى صورتان :
٣- (ك س) + (ك م) ∴ (ج س) ١- (ج م) + (ك س) فتنتج رغم أن حذف
٤- (ك س) + (ج م) ∴ (ج س) الشرط وهي الضرب الخامس
٥- (ج م) + (ك س) ∴ (ج س) ٢- (ج م) + (ك م) فلا تنتج رغم تحقق الشرط
A general summary of the four figures and their productive moods with symbols. Figures: first, second, third, fourth. Their premises: minor, major, minor major. A: First figure productive moods (four). Premises (min, maj): 1. (A A) gives A; condition: minor affirmative, major universal. 2. (A E) gives E; condition: minor affirmative, major universal. 3. (I A) gives I; 4. (I E) gives O. B: Second figure productive moods (four). Premises (maj, maj?): 1. (A E) gives E; condition: premises differ in quality, major universal. 2. (E A) gives E; 3. (I E) gives O; 4. (O A) gives O. C: Third figure productive moods (six). Premises (min, min): 1. (A A) gives I; condition: minor affirmative, one premise universal. 2. (A E) gives O; 3. (I A) gives I; 4. (A I) gives I; 5. (I A) gives I? (repeated?); 6. (I E) gives O. D: Fourth figure productive moods (five). Premises (min, maj): 1. (A A) gives I; condition: the two negatives (negative and particular) do not meet. Except two cases: 1. (I E) produces despite removing the condition, which is the fifth mood; 2. (I A) does not produce despite the condition being met.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the productive moods of categorical syllogism across the four figures. The author uses symbolic notation: (ك م) = universal affirmative, (ك س) = universal negative, (ج م) = particular affirmative, (ج س) = particular negative. The first figure has four moods: AAA, EAE, AII, EIO. The condition: the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise universal. The second figure also has four moods, with the condition that the major premise (كبرى) is universal and the premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). The third figure has six moods, producing only particular conclusions; the condition is that the minor premise is affirmative and at least one premise is universal. The fourth figure has five moods, generally producing particular conclusions with the condition that the two 'lows' (negation and particularity) do not occur together; however, there are two exceptions: (I E) actually produces a conclusion (contrary to the condition), and (I A) does not produce even though the condition is satisfied. The text uses a condensed style typical of a matn or summary. Students should memorize the moods and conditions, and parse the symbolic notation.

The Syllogism

Productive Moods of the First Figure
First figure moods with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four productive moods of the first figure (AAA, EAE, AII, EIO) and verify their validity using the condition that the minor must be affirmative and the major universal.

Core Idea
The first figure is the only one that can produce all four types of conclusions (A, E, I, O) directly.

The first figure is considered the most perfect because its moods are intuitively valid. The text specifies four moods with a clear condition: the minor premise must be affirmative and the major premise must be universal. Let's examine each mood and see how it applies to Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four moods
From the text, the four moods are: 1. (ك م)+(ك م) gives (ك م) [AAA]; 2. (ك م)+(ك س) gives (ك س) [EAE]; 3. (ج م)+(ك م) gives (ج م) [AII]; 4. (ج م)+(ك س) gives (ج س) [EIO].
The order is always minor premise first, then major.
2
Apply the condition
The condition states: the minor premise must be affirmative (not negative) and the major premise must be universal. Check each mood: in AAA, minor is ك م (affirmative universal) and major is ك م (universal) – condition met. In EAE, minor is ك م (affirmative), major is ك س (universal negative) – condition met. In AII, minor is ج م (affirmative particular) – condition met, major is ك م (universal). In EIO, minor is ج م (affirmative particular) – condition met, major is ك س (universal). All four satisfy.
There is no mood with a negative minor in the first figure.
3
Verify with an Islamic example
Take mood AAA: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (major, ك م), 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant' (minor, ك م), conclusion 'Every nabidh is forbidden' (ك م). This is valid. Now try mood EAE: 'No intoxicant is permissible' (major, ك س), 'Every nabidh is an intoxicant' (minor, ك م), conclusion 'No nabidh is permissible' (ك س). Valid.
Memorize the condition: minor positive, major universal.

Productive Moods of the Second Figure
Second figure moods with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive moods of the second figure (AEE, EAE, AOO, EIO) and apply the condition that the major premise must be universal and the two premises differ in quality.

Core Idea
Second figure moods always produce negative conclusions, distinguishing truth from falsehood.

The second figure is used when you want to prove that something is not something else. Its condition is that the major premise must be universal and the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). The text lists four moods with symbolic notation. Let's decode them and see how they function in Islamic argumentation, such as refuting heresies.

Guided Path
1
Parse the four moods
The text lists: 1. (ك م)+(ك س) gives (ك س) [AEE]; 2. (ك س)+(ك م) gives (ك س) [EAE]; 3. (ج م)+(ك س) gives (ج س) [AOO]; 4. (ج س)+(ك م) gives (ج س) [EIO]. Note: the order is minor then major? Actually the header says (ع ع) indicating both premises have the middle as predicate? Wait, second figure has the middle term as predicate in both premises. The order in the text: 'صغرى كبرى' so first is minor, second is major.
Be careful: the second figure moods are often named differently in Western logic (Camestres, Cesare, Baroco, Festino).
2
Apply the condition
The condition: 'اختلاف مقدمتيه أن تكون كبراه في الإيجاب والسلب كلية (الكيف)' which means the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) and the major premise must be universal. Check each mood: 1. minor A (affirmative), major E (negative) → differ, major universal; 2. minor E (negative), major A (affirmative) → differ, major universal; 3. minor I (affirmative), major E (negative) → differ, major universal; 4. minor O (negative), major A (affirmative) → differ, major universal.
All four satisfy the condition. Note that the minor can be particular or universal, but the major must be universal.
3
Test with an Islamic example for mood EAE (minor universal negative, major universal affirmative)
Example: Major: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (A). Minor: 'No nabidh is an intoxicant' (E). Conclusion: 'No nabidh is forbidden' (E). This shows that if nabidh is not an intoxicant, it may not be forbidden (but actual fiqh says nabidh can be intoxicating; this is just a logical exercise). Note: The conclusion is negative, as expected.
Second figure always gives a negative conclusion.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Structure of a Categorical Syllogism
Author states the basic composition of a categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three terms (minor, major, middle) and the two premises in any categorical syllogism and verify that the conclusion follows necessarily.

Core Idea
Every categorical syllogism has exactly three terms and two premises that yield a conclusion.

The categorical syllogism is the workhorse of Islamic logic. Before you can apply validity rules, you must be able to dissect a syllogism into its components: the minor and major terms that appear in the conclusion, and the middle term that links the premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The conclusion is the statement to be proven. Find it first; it contains the minor term (subject) and major term (predicate).
2
Find the middle term
Look for a term that appears in both premises but NOT in the conclusion. That is the middle term that connects the two premises.
3
Separate the premises
The minor premise contains the minor term and the middle term; the major premise contains the major term and the middle term.
4
Verify the three-term rule
Count the distinct terms. If there are more than three (e.g., ambiguity), the syllogism is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضوابط عامة للقياس الاقتراني :
لدى النظر في أضرب القياس المنتجة نلاحظ ما يلي :
١ - كل قياس هو مؤلف من قضيتين : (قضية صغرى) + (قضية كبرى) يشتق منهما بالضرورة (قضية ثالثة) هي النتيجة .
٢ - كل قياس هو مؤلف من ثلاثة حدود فقط : (حدّ أصغر - حدّ أكبر - حدّ أوسط) والحد الأوسط المتكرر في مقدمتي القياس هو الذي يسقط في النتيجة ولا يذكر فيها .
٣ - لا نجد في الضروب المنتجة ضرباً مؤلفاً من قضيتين سالبتين، بل إذا وجد السلب في إحداهما كانت الأخرى موجبة، فباستطاعتنا أن نتخذ من ذلك ضابطاً فنقول : «لا يتألف قياس صحيح من مقدمتين سالبتين» .
٤ - رأينا أنه كلما وجدت قضية سالبة في إحدى مقدمتي القياس كانت النتيجة سالبة، وكلما وجدت قضية جزئية في إحدى مقدمتي القياس كانت النتيجة جزئية .
ولما كان السلب أخس من الإيجاب، وكانت الجزئية أخس من الكلية، صح لنا أن نتخذ ضابطاً من اتباع النتيجة للسلب والجزئية فنقول : «إن النتيجة تتبع الأخس في الكم وفي الكيف» .
٥ - رأينا أن الشكل الثالث لا ينتج إلا (جزئية) ولو كانت مقدمتاه كليتين .
٦ - رأينا أن الشكل الرابع لا ينتج أيضاً إلا (جزئية) ولو كانت مقدمتاه كليتين، باستثناء الضرب الرابع منه، فهو ينتج كلية سالبة ٤ / ض ح .
صغرى كبرى نتيجة [(ك س) + (ك م) ∴ (ك س)]
General rules for categorical syllogism: Upon examining the productive moods of syllogism we note the following: 1. Every syllogism is composed of two propositions: a minor proposition and a major proposition, from which a third proposition, the conclusion, is necessarily derived. 2. Every syllogism is composed of only three terms: minor term, major term, and middle term; the middle term, repeated in the two premises, drops out in the conclusion and is not mentioned in it. 3. We do not find among the productive moods a mood composed of two negative propositions; rather, if negation occurs in one of them, the other is affirmative. So we can take from that a rule and say: 'No valid syllogism is composed of two negative premises.' 4. We saw that whenever a negative proposition occurs in one of the two premises, the conclusion is negative, and whenever a particular proposition occurs in one of the two premises, the conclusion is particular. Since negation is inferior to affirmation, and particularity is inferior to universality, it is correct for us to take a rule from the following of the conclusion for negation and particularity, and say: 'The conclusion follows the inferior in quantity and in quality.' 5. We saw that the third figure produces only a particular, even if its two premises are universal. 6. We saw that the fourth figure also produces only a particular, even if its two premises are universal, except for its fourth mood, for it produces a universal negative: minor (E) + major (A) gives conclusion (E).
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage provides general rules for the categorical syllogism derived from observing the productive moods. The author lists six rules: First, every syllogism consists of two premises and a conclusion. Second, there are exactly three terms: minor, major, and middle; the middle term disappears in the conclusion. Third, no syllogism can have two negative premises; at least one must be affirmative. Fourth, the conclusion 'follows the inferior' in quantity and quality: if a premise is negative, the conclusion is negative; if a premise is particular, the conclusion is particular. Since negation is 'inferior' to affirmation and particularity is 'inferior' to universality, the conclusion takes on the weaker property. Fifth, the third figure always yields a particular conclusion, even if both premises are universal. Sixth, the fourth figure similarly yields particular conclusions except for its fourth mood (EAE) which gives a universal negative conclusion. These rules are foundational for evaluating the validity of categorical syllogisms. For instance, from the third rule, a syllogism like 'No khamr is halal. No nabidh is khamr' would be invalid because both premises are negative. From the fourth rule, if one premise is 'Some scholars are just' (particular affirmative) and the other is 'Every just person is trustworthy' (universal affirmative), the conclusion must be particular: 'Some scholars are trustworthy'.

Key Terms
حد أصغر
حد أكبر
حد أوسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 2
B) 3
C) 4
D) 5

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical syllogism always contains exactly three distinct terms and two premises leading to a conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Khamr is an intoxicant. Therefore khamr is forbidden.' Name the minor term, major term, and middle term.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Rule: No Two Negative Premises
Author states the rule that no valid syllogism has two negative premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an invalid categorical syllogism with two negative premises and explain why no conclusion follows necessarily.

Core Idea
Two negative premises cannot yield a necessary conclusion.

One of the most common errors in reasoning is using two negative premises. The logicians have established that no valid categorical syllogism can have both premises negative. This rule prevents contradictions and empty inferences.

Guided Path
1
Check the quality of each premise
Determine whether each premise is affirmative or negative. A negative premise contains 'no', 'not', or equivalent negation.
2
Apply the rule
If both premises are negative, the syllogism is invalid. No conclusion is forced.
3
Test with an example
Consider: 'No khamr is halal. No nabidh is khamr.' Can we conclude anything about halal and nabidh? No, because both premises are negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضوابط عامة للقياس الاقتراني :
لدى النظر في أضرب القياس المنتجة نلاحظ ما يلي :
١ - كل قياس هو مؤلف من قضيتين : (قضية صغرى) + (قضية كبرى) يشتق منهما بالضرورة (قضية ثالثة) هي النتيجة .
٢ - كل قياس هو مؤلف من ثلاثة حدود فقط : (حدّ أصغر - حدّ أكبر - حدّ أوسط) والحد الأوسط المتكرر في مقدمتي القياس هو الذي يسقط في النتيجة ولا يذكر فيها .
٣ - لا نجد في الضروب المنتجة ضرباً مؤلفاً من قضيتين سالبتين، بل إذا وجد السلب في إحداهما كانت الأخرى موجبة، فباستطاعتنا أن نتخذ من ذلك ضابطاً فنقول : «لا يتألف قياس صحيح من مقدمتين سالبتين» .
٤ - رأينا أنه كلما وجدت قضية سالبة في إحدى مقدمتي القياس كانت النتيجة سالبة، وكلما وجدت قضية جزئية في إحدى مقدمتي القياس كانت النتيجة جزئية .
ولما كان السلب أخس من الإيجاب، وكانت الجزئية أخس من الكلية، صح لنا أن نتخذ ضابطاً من اتباع النتيجة للسلب والجزئية فنقول : «إن النتيجة تتبع الأخس في الكم وفي الكيف» .
٥ - رأينا أن الشكل الثالث لا ينتج إلا (جزئية) ولو كانت مقدمتاه كليتين .
٦ - رأينا أن الشكل الرابع لا ينتج أيضاً إلا (جزئية) ولو كانت مقدمتاه كليتين، باستثناء الضرب الرابع منه، فهو ينتج كلية سالبة ٤ / ض ح .
صغرى كبرى نتيجة [(ك س) + (ك م) ∴ (ك س)]
General rules for categorical syllogism: Upon examining the productive moods of syllogism we note the following: 1. Every syllogism is composed of two propositions: a minor proposition and a major proposition, from which a third proposition, the conclusion, is necessarily derived. 2. Every syllogism is composed of only three terms: minor term, major term, and middle term; the middle term, repeated in the two premises, drops out in the conclusion and is not mentioned in it. 3. We do not find among the productive moods a mood composed of two negative propositions; rather, if negation occurs in one of them, the other is affirmative. So we can take from that a rule and say: 'No valid syllogism is composed of two negative premises.' 4. We saw that whenever a negative proposition occurs in one of the two premises, the conclusion is negative, and whenever a particular proposition occurs in one of the two premises, the conclusion is particular. Since negation is inferior to affirmation, and particularity is inferior to universality, it is correct for us to take a rule from the following of the conclusion for negation and particularity, and say: 'The conclusion follows the inferior in quantity and in quality.' 5. We saw that the third figure produces only a particular, even if its two premises are universal. 6. We saw that the fourth figure also produces only a particular, even if its two premises are universal, except for its fourth mood, for it produces a universal negative: minor (E) + major (A) gives conclusion (E).
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage provides general rules for the categorical syllogism derived from observing the productive moods. The author lists six rules: First, every syllogism consists of two premises and a conclusion. Second, there are exactly three terms: minor, major, and middle; the middle term disappears in the conclusion. Third, no syllogism can have two negative premises; at least one must be affirmative. Fourth, the conclusion 'follows the inferior' in quantity and quality: if a premise is negative, the conclusion is negative; if a premise is particular, the conclusion is particular. Since negation is 'inferior' to affirmation and particularity is 'inferior' to universality, the conclusion takes on the weaker property. Fifth, the third figure always yields a particular conclusion, even if both premises are universal. Sixth, the fourth figure similarly yields particular conclusions except for its fourth mood (EAE) which gives a universal negative conclusion. These rules are foundational for evaluating the validity of categorical syllogisms. For instance, from the third rule, a syllogism like 'No khamr is halal. No nabidh is khamr' would be invalid because both premises are negative. From the fourth rule, if one premise is 'Some scholars are just' (particular affirmative) and the other is 'Every just person is trustworthy' (universal affirmative), the conclusion must be particular: 'Some scholars are trustworthy'.

Key Terms
سالب
موجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If both premises of a categorical syllogism are negative, the syllogism is:
A) Valid if the conclusion is also negative
B) Valid if the conclusion is affirmative
C) Invalid regardless of the conclusion
D) Valid only in the fourth figure

Key Takeaway
Any categorical syllogism with two negative premises is automatically invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify why the following syllogism is invalid: 'No khamr is halal. No nabidh is khamr. Therefore, nabidh is not halal.' (Hint: the conclusion does not necessarily follow; the premises are both negative.)

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Rule: Conclusion Follows the Inferior
Author states the rule that the conclusion follows the inferior in quantity and quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the conclusion from the premises using the 'inferior' rule.

Core Idea
The conclusion always takes the weaker (inferior) property: if a premise is particular, the conclusion is particular; if a premise is negative, the conclusion is negative.

The fourth rule summarises how the conclusion 'follows the inferior' (al-akhass). Negation is inferior to affirmation, and particularity is inferior to universality. When premises differ in quality or quantity, the conclusion adopts the weaker one. This rule is a quick validity check.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantity and quality of each premise
Note whether each premise is universal (A/E) or particular (I/O) and affirmative (A/I) or negative (E/O).
2
Apply the 'inferior' rule for quantity
If at least one premise is particular, the conclusion must be particular. Only if both premises are universal can the conclusion be universal.
3
Apply the 'inferior' rule for quality
If at least one premise is negative, the conclusion must be negative. Only if both premises are affirmative can the conclusion be affirmative.
4
Combine both: the conclusion takes the weaker of each
If one premise is particular negative and the other is universal affirmative, the conclusion is particular negative.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Third Figure Always Yields Particular
Author states that the third figure yields only a particular conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third figure of a categorical syllogism and state that it always produces a particular conclusion.

Core Idea
The third figure, even with two universal premises, can only give a particular conclusion.

The third figure is one of the four figures of categorical syllogism. Its unique property is that it always yields a particular conclusion, never a universal one. This is an important exception to the general rule about universal premises producing universal conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the third figure
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises.
For example: All A are B. All A are C. Therefore, some B are C.
2
Check the premises
Even if both premises are universal (A or E), the conclusion must be particular (I or O).
3
Explain why
Because the middle term is subject in both premises, it cannot distribute the major term sufficiently to give a universal conclusion.

The Syllogism
advanced
5 min
Fourth Figure: Mostly Particular, One Exception
Author states the fourth figure's property with its exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth figure and know that only its fourth mood (EAE) yields a universal negative conclusion; all others yield particular.

Core Idea
The fourth figure mostly yields particular conclusions, except for its fourth mood which gives a universal negative.

The fourth figure is similar to the third in that it typically yields particular conclusions. However, there is an exception: the fourth mood (EAE) produces a universal negative conclusion. This is a specific nuance that logicians have noted.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the fourth figure
In the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate of the minor premise and the subject of the major premise.
2
List the moods
There are four moods: AA, AE, IA, EA (among others). All except EA yield particular conclusions.
3
Identify the exception
The mood with minor universal negative (E) and major universal affirmative (A) gives a universal negative conclusion (E). For example: 'No B is A. All C is B. Therefore, no C is A.'

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Two Distribution Rules for Valid Syllogisms
Author states two rules: middle term distribution and conclusion term distribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two distribution rules to check the validity of categorical syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism must distribute the middle term and must not distribute a term in the conclusion unless it is distributed in the premises.

This lesson derives two essential rules for valid categorical syllogisms from observed productive moods. The rules govern the distribution of the middle term and the distribution of terms in the conclusion. Understanding these rules allows you to quickly identify many invalid syllogisms in arguments from fiqh, kalam, and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term in a syllogism
In a categorical syllogism, the middle term appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. For example, in 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every khamr is an intoxicant; therefore every khamr is forbidden,' the middle term is 'intoxicant'.
The middle term is the link between the other two terms.
2
Apply the first rule: check distribution of the middle term
The first rule states that the middle term must be distributed (taken universally) in at least one premise. In the example, 'intoxicant' is distributed in the first premise ('Every intoxicant...' is an A proposition, subject distributed). So the rule is satisfied.
Distribution means the judgment applies to every individual of that term.
3
Apply the second rule: check distribution of terms in the conclusion
The second rule: if a term is distributed in the conclusion, it must have been distributed in one of the premises. In the example, 'khamr' is distributed in the conclusion ('every khamr'), and it is also distributed in the minor premise ('every khamr'). So the rule holds.
This prevents illicit major or minor fallacies.
4
Recognize necessity but not sufficiency
The author warns that these rules are necessary but not sufficient. A syllogism can satisfy both rules yet be invalid due to other reasons (e.g., negative premises, illicit process). For instance, 'All scholars are trustworthy; some men are scholars; therefore some men are trustworthy' satisfies both rules, but consider 'All A are B; all C are A; therefore all C are B' – that's valid. But if we change quantifiers, it may fail.
Always check all validity conditions, not just distribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٧ - رأينا في أضرب القياس المنتجة أن الحد الأوسط مستغرق جميع أفراده في إحدى المقدمتين أو كلتيهما، فيصح لنا أن نتخذ من ذلك ضابطاً فنقول : «كل قياس صحيح لا بد أن يكون الحد الأوسط فيه مستغرقاً جميع أفراده في إحدى مقدمتيه أو كلتيهما» . ولا يعني هذا أننا كلما وجدنا الاستغراق في الحد الأوسط فالضرب صحيح الإنتاج، فكثير من الضروب غير المنتجة مستغرقة الحد الأوسط، وقد جاء عقمها من سبب آخر. ٨ - رأينا في نتائج أضرب القياس المنتجة، أنها لا تشتمل على حد مستغرق لجميع أفراده، دون أن يكون هذا الحد مستغرقاً في إحدى مقدمتي القياس . فيصح لنا إذن أن نتخذ من ذلك ضابطاً فنقول : «لا يصح أن يكون حد مستغرقاً جميع أفراده في النتيجة، ما لم يكن هذا الحد مستغرقاً في إحدى مقدمتي القياس» . ولا يعني هذا أننا متى وجدنا حداً مستغرقاً في مقدمتي القياس، جئنا به في النتيجة مستغرقاً، فكثير من النتائج الصحيحة غير مستغرقة الحدين، مع استغراقهما في المقدمتين، أو استغراق أحدهما في إحداهما .
7. We have seen in the productive moods of the syllogism that the middle term is distributed over all its individuals in one or both premises. So it is correct for us to take from that a rule and say: 'Every valid syllogism must have its middle term distributed over all its individuals in one or both of its premises.' This does not mean that whenever we find distribution of the middle term, the mood is productive; for many non-productive moods have their middle term distributed, and their sterility comes from another cause. 8. We have seen in the conclusions of the productive moods of the syllogism that they do not include a term distributed over all its individuals without that term being distributed in one of the premises of the syllogism. So it is correct for us to take from that a rule and say: 'It is not correct for a term to be distributed over all its individuals in the conclusion unless this term is distributed in one of the premises of the syllogism.' This does not mean that whenever we find a term distributed in both premises of the syllogism, we bring it in the conclusion distributed; for many correct conclusions are not distributed in both terms, despite their distribution in both premises, or the distribution of one of them in one of them.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents two rules derived from observing productive syllogistic moods. The first rule is that in any valid syllogism, the middle term must be distributed (i.e., taken universally) in at least one premise. The author immediately cautions that distribution of the middle term is necessary but not sufficient for validity, since many invalid moods also have a distributed middle term. The second rule is that if a term is distributed in the conclusion, it must have been distributed in one of the premises. Again, the author cautions that the converse does not hold: a term distributed in the premises may still be undistributed in a valid conclusion. The author uses the technical term 'mustaghriq' (distributed) for a term taken in its full extent.

Key Takeaway
Two necessary conditions for syllogistic validity: the middle term must be distributed in at least one premise, and any term distributed in the conclusion must be distributed in a premise.

Three Rules on Premise Quality and Quantity
Author states three rules for valid syllogisms based on premise quality and quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three rules of premise quality and quantity to determine whether a categorical syllogism can be valid.

Core Idea
Three simple checks determine if a syllogism's premises can yield a valid conclusion.

Every productive categorical syllogism follows certain hidden rules about the quality and quantity of its premises. The author distills these rules from observing which premise combinations actually produce conclusions. In this lesson, we'll unpack each rule and see how they work together to guarantee that a syllogism has any chance of being valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quality of each premise
Check whether each premise is affirmative or negative. Rule 1 says two affirmative premises cannot produce a negative conclusion. If both are affirmative, the conclusion must be affirmative.
2
Check if at least one premise is universal
Rule 2 states that at least one premise must be universal. If both are particular, no conclusion follows. Identify the quantity (universal/particular) of each premise.
3
Inspect the minor premise for negativity
Rule 3 says if the minor premise is negative, the major premise must be universal. If the minor is negative and the major is particular, no conclusion follows.
The minor premise is the one containing the subject of the conclusion.
4
Apply all three rules together
Test a given pair of premises against all three rules. If any rule is violated, the syllogism cannot be valid, regardless of figure or mood.
These rules are necessary but not sufficient conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٩ - رأينا أن المقدمتين إذا كانتا موجبتين، فإنهما لا تنتجان قضية سالبة . ١٠ - رأينا أنه لم يأت ضرب منتج مشتمل على قضيتين جزئيتين، إذن لا بد في كل قياس من أن تكون إحدى مقدمتيه كلية على الأقل . ١١ - رأينا أنه لم يأت ضرب منتج صغراه سالبة وكبراه جزئية، إذن : فلا تنتج صغرى سالبة مع كبرى جزئية .
9. We have seen that if the two premises are affirmative, they do not produce a negative proposition. 10. We have seen that there has not come a productive mood comprising two particular propositions, therefore in every syllogism it is necessary that one of its premises be universal at least. 11. We have seen that there has not come a productive mood whose minor is negative and major is particular, therefore: a negative minor with a particular major does not produce (a conclusion).
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents three more rules for validity based on observation of productive moods. First, two affirmative premises cannot yield a negative conclusion. Second, at least one premise must be universal; two particular premises never yield a valid conclusion. Third, a negative minor premise combined with a particular major premise yields no conclusion. These rules are derived empirically from examining all possible premise combinations and finding no productive mood with these combinations.

Key Takeaway
Three quick checks on premise quality and quantity can eliminate many invalid syllogisms before examining figure and mood.

Quantifying Predicates in Categorical Syllogisms
Proposes explicitly stating predicate quantity and provides examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the proposal to explicitly quantify the predicate in each premise of a categorical syllogism and explain how this simplifies the classification of valid moods.

Core Idea
Quantifying the predicate reduces syllogistic moods by making the logical structure fully explicit.

In standard categorical logic, only subjects are quantified. The author of this passage proposes a modification: explicitly state the quantity of the predicate as well. This addition could streamline the classification of productive moods by clarifying the relationships between terms right in the premises. Let's explore this proposal and see how it works with examples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the standard categorical syllogism
In a standard categorical proposition, only the subject has a quantifier (all/some). The predicate is not quantified. For example, 'Every human is an animal' does not say whether 'animal' is taken universally or particularly.
This is the conventional approach in Aristotelian logic.
2
Learn the author's proposal
The author suggests adding a quantifier to the predicate in both premises. So instead of 'Every A is B', we would say 'Every A is every B' or 'Every A is some B', depending on the intended logical relationship.
This makes the predicate's quantity explicit.
3
Examine the first example
Example: 'Every human is every rational, and every rational is every laughing; therefore every human is every laughing.' Here both predicates are universal, leading to a universal conclusion.
This corresponds to a syllogism where all terms are universal in extension.
4
Examine the second example
Example: 'Every human is some animal, and every animal is some growing; therefore every human is some growing.' Here predicates are particular, leading to a particular conclusion.
This shows a syllogism with particular predicates.
5
Recognize the simplification
By quantifying predicates, the validity conditions become straightforward: the middle term in the major must contain or equal the minor term in the minor. This reduces the need to memorize many moods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعقيب باقتراح على ضروب وأشكال القياس الاقتراني : ربما كان باستطاعتنا اختصار الأمر فيما يتعلق بتفصيل الضروب المنتجة لأشكال القياس الاقتراني، إذا اشترطنا التصريح بكمية المحمول في كل من مقدمتيه الصغرى والكبرى، وذلك بحسب واقع حال القضية، فنضيف إلى المحمول السور الكلي أو السور الجزئي، كما نفعل في الموضوع، فنقول مثلاً : ١ - كل (أ) كل (ب) وكل (ب) كل (ج) كل (إنسان) كل (ناطق) وكل (ناطق) كل (ضاحك) .. فكل (أ) كل (ج) .. فكل (إنسان) كل (ضاحك) ٢ - كل (أ) بعض (ب) وكل (ب) بعض (ج) كل (إنسان) بعض (حيوان) وكل (حيوان) بعض (نامي) .. فكل (أ) بعض (ج) .. فكل (إنسان) بعض (نامي) وعندئذ نقول : ١ - يكون القياس صحيحاً منتجاً، عندما يُسلم فيه بتضمن الحد الأوسط في الكبرى للحد الأصغر في الصغرى، أو مساواته له . والإنتاج هنا يرجع إلى بديهيتين عقليتين : الأولى : أن الحكم على جميع أفراد الكلي بشيء هو حكم بهذا الشيء على كل فرد منه، والقياس الذي نثبت فيه اندراج فرد أو أفراد في ضمن أفراد كلي، ينقلنا بداهة إلى أن ما نحكم به على هذا الكلي حكماً استغراقياً لا بد أن يكون شاملاً لكل ما يندرج فيه، فقولنا : كل إنسان بعض حيوان وكل حيوان بعض نامي تنطبق عليه البديهة العقلية أن بعض الشيء هو بعض لما ذلك الشيء بعض له . الثانية : أن الحكم على جميع أفراد الكلي بشيء، هو حكم على كليّ آخر هو مساوٍ له في الماصدق، نظراً إلى اتحاد الماصدق في كل منهما، فقولنا :
A follow-up with a suggestion on the moods and figures of the categorical syllogism: Perhaps it is possible for us to simplify the matter regarding the detailed productive moods of the categorical syllogism, if we stipulate the explicit mention of the quantity of the predicate in each of its two premises, the minor and the major, according to the actual state of the proposition. So we add to the predicate the universal quantifier or the particular quantifier, as we do with the subject. So we say, for example: 1. Every A is every B, and every B is every C (Every human is every rational, and every rational is every laughing); therefore every A is every C, therefore every human is every laughing. 2. Every A is some B, and every B is some C (Every human is some animal, and every animal is some growing); therefore every A is some C, therefore every human is some growing. Then we say: 1. The syllogism is valid and productive when it is conceded that the middle term in the major contains the minor term in the minor, or equals it. Production here goes back to two rational intuitions: The first: That the judgment upon all individuals of a universal concerning something is a judgment with this thing upon every individual of it. And the syllogism in which we establish the inclusion of an individual or individuals within the individuals of a universal transfers us by intuition to the fact that what we judge upon this universal with a distributive judgment must encompass everything included in it. So our saying: Every human is some animal and every animal is some growing — the rational intuition that some of a thing is some of what that thing is some of applies to it. The second: That the judgment upon all individuals of a universal concerning something is a judgment upon another universal that is equal to it in extension, considering the unity of extension in each of them. So our saying:

Commentary
This section offers a suggestion to simplify the classification of productive moods by explicitly stating the quantity of the predicate in each premise, in addition to the quantity of the subject. The author argues that this would allow us to reduce the number of moods. Two examples are given using notation: first, a syllogism where the predicate is universal in both premises, leading to a universal conclusion; second, a syllogism where the predicate is particular in both premises, leading to a particular conclusion. Then the author states that a syllogism is valid when the middle term in the major premise contains or equals the minor term in the minor premise. The author then explains that the productivity of syllogisms rests on two rational intuitions: (1) if something is predicated of all individuals of a universal, it is predicated of every individual under it, so if we establish that an individual falls under a universal, the judgment on the universal applies to that individual; (2) if two universals have the same extension, a judgment on one is a judgment on the other. These intuitions are illustrated with the examples given. Note: The passage ends abruptly; it likely continued with a third point.

The Syllogism

Two Rational Intuitions for Syllogistic Validity
Explains two rational intuitions underlying syllogism validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two rational intuitions that the author uses to justify syllogistic validity and apply them to evaluate categorical syllogisms.

Core Idea
Two self-evident intuitions—subsumption and coextension—underpin every valid categorical syllogism.

After proposing quantified predicates, the author explains that the productivity of syllogisms rests on two self-evident rational intuitions. These intuitions are the bedrock of categorical reasoning. In this lesson, we'll unpack each intuition and see how they operate in the examples provided.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first rational intuition
The first intuition states: If you judge something about all individuals of a universal, then that judgment applies to every individual that falls under that universal. In the example, 'Every human is some animal' means each human is an individual animal; the judgment 'every animal is some growing' applies to each animal, so each human is some growing.
This is the principle of subsumption: what holds of the universal holds of its particulars.
2
Apply the first intuition to the given syllogism
The syllogism 'Every human is some animal; every animal is some growing' uses the first intuition: since the universal 'animal' is under 'growing', and each human is under 'animal', each human must be under 'growing'.
The conclusion follows by a chain of subsumption.
3
Identify the second rational intuition
The second intuition states: If two universals have the same extension (masadaq), then a judgment on one applies to the other. In the first example, if 'human' and 'rational' have the same extension, then 'every rational is every laughing' implies 'every human is every laughing'.
This is the principle of coextension or extension equality.
4
Apply the second intuition to the given syllogism
The syllogism 'Every human is every rational; every rational is every laughing' relies on the second intuition: since 'human' and 'rational' are coextensive (in the example, all and only humans are rational), the judgment on rational transfers to human.
The conclusion follows from extension equality.
5
Recognize the foundational role of these intuitions
The author claims that all syllogistic production reduces to these two intuitions. They are self-evident and do not require proof.

Source Text

The Syllogism

Condition for Valid Affirmative Syllogism
Condition for valid affirmative syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an affirmative categorical syllogism is valid by checking if the middle term includes or equals the minor term in extension.

Core Idea
A valid affirmative syllogism requires the middle term to include or equal the minor term.

This passage presents the condition for a valid affirmative categorical syllogism. Understanding this condition is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences like kalam and fiqh. The author illustrates with two examples using both symbolic variables and concrete terms from the Islamic logical tradition, and grounds the condition in two intuitive principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
Identify the minor term (subject of conclusion), major term (predicate of conclusion), and middle term (appears in both premises). In the examples: human (asghar), laughing (akbar), rational/animal (awsat).
2
Check quantifiers of premises
Note whether each premise is universal (كل) or particular (بعض). The first example has two universal premises; the second has universal minor and particular major.
The conclusion's quantifier depends on the weakest premise.
3
Apply the inclusion condition
Verify that the middle term in the major premise includes or is equal to the minor term. If the minor is part of the middle term in extension, the syllogism is valid.
Example 1: 'human' is included in 'rational' (identical extension). Example 2: 'human' is a part of 'animal'.
4
Confirm with intuitive principles
Use the two principles: (i) a universal judgment applies to every individual under it, (ii) if two terms have the same extension, predications transfer. This confirms the logical necessity.
For example 2: some of a thing is some of what that thing is some of.

الأولى : أن الحكم على جميع أفراد الكلي بشيء هو حكم بهذا الشيء على كل فرد منه، والقياس الذي نثبت فيه اندراج فرد أو أفراد في ضمن أفراد كلي، ينقلنا بداهة إلى أن ما نحكم به على هذا الكلي حكماً استغراقياً لا بد أن يكون شاملاً لكل ما يندرج فيه، فقولنا :

كل إنسان كل ناطق وكل ناطق كل ضاحك

The predicate either universal or particular quantifier, as we do with the subject. So we say, for example:
1- Every (A) is every (B) and every (B) is every (C)
   Every (human) is every (rational) and every (rational) is every (laughing)
   ... So every (A) is every (C)
   ... So every (human) is every (laughing)
2- Every (A) is some (B) and every (B) is some (C)
   Every (human) is some (animal) and every (animal) is some (growing)
   ... So every (A) is some (C)
   ... So every (human) is some (growing)
Then we say:
1- The syllogism is valid and productive when it is conceded that the middle term in the major premise includes the minor term in the minor premise, or is equal to it.
And production here goes back to two intuitive principles:
The first: that the judgment on all individuals of a universal by something is a judgment by that thing on every individual of it, and the syllogism by which we prove the inclusion of an individual or individuals within the individuals of a universal transfers us intuitively to the fact that what we judge about this universal with a comprehensive judgment must include everything that falls under it. So our saying:
   Every human is some animal and every animal is some growing
   The intuitive principle applies to it that 'some of a thing is some of what that thing is some of'.
The second: that the judgment on all individuals of a universal by something is a judgment on another universal that is equal to it in extension, given the unity of extension in each of them. So our saying:
   Every human is every rational and every rational is every laughing
   means that every individual that is called human is called rational, and every individual that is called rational is called laughing. Therefore, due to the unity of extension in the three terms—the minor, middle, and major—what is predicated of the middle term among them is necessarily predicated of the minor term.

Commentary
This passage explains the condition for a valid affirmative categorical syllogism. The author begins by showing two types of affirmative syllogisms using symbolic examples with variables (أ, ب, ج) and concrete examples from human, rational, laughing, animal, growing. The first example has universal premises leading to a universal conclusion; the second has a universal minor and particular major leading to a particular conclusion. The author then states the general condition: the middle term must include or equal the minor term. He grounds this in two intuitive principles. The first principle is that a universal judgment applies to every individual falling under it. The second is that if two terms have the same extension, whatever is predicated of one is predicated of the other. The second principle is illustrated with the example of human and rational being co-extensive, leading to the conclusion that every human is laughing. All examples are from the Islamic sciences: human as rational animal and laughing (a common example in logic).
