Conditions for Valid Negative Syllogisms
Author presents conditions for valid negative syllogism and general rules including no conclusion from two negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify valid negative categorical syllogisms by checking the opposition conditions and the rule against two negative premises.

Core Idea
A valid negative conclusion requires opposition between terms, never two negatives.

This passage sets out the precise conditions under which a categorical syllogism yields a negative conclusion. Mastering these rules protects you from drawing false inferences in Islamic legal and theological reasoning, where a wrong conclusion can lead to error in sacred matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
Check if the conclusion is a negative proposition (e.g., 'no S is P'). If affirmative, the conditions for negative syllogisms do not apply.
2
Apply condition (2): major opposed to middle
Verify that the major term is opposed to (mubayin) the middle, and the middle includes or equals the minor in a positive judgment. This yields a negative conclusion.
Example: 'Every human is some animal' (positive inclusion) + 'No animal is stone' (opposition) => 'No human is stone'.
3
Apply condition (3): minor opposed to middle
Alternatively, check if the minor term is opposed to the middle, and the middle equals the major in a positive judgment. This also yields a negative conclusion.
Example: 'Every horse is not human' (opposition) + 'Every human is every laughing' (positive equality) => 'Every horse is not laughing'.
4
Enforce the rule against two negative premises
Ensure that not both premises are negative. If both are negative, no valid conclusion follows, regardless of term relations.
This is a universal rule in categorical syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ - ويكون القياس صحيحاً منتجاً عندما يُسلَّم فيه بأن الحد الأكبر مباين للحد الأوسط، ويكون الحد الأوسط متضمناً أو مساوياً للحد الأصغر في حكم موجب، والإنتاج في هذه الحالة إنتاج سلبي، وهو يستند إلى البديهة العقلية التي تقضي بأن مباين كل أفراد الكلي هو مباين لكل فردٍ منه، ومباين لما يساويه في الماصدق، فحين نقول:
كل إنسان بعض حيوان ولا شيء من الحيوان بحجر
فإنه يستلزم بداهة أنه لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، لأن الإنسان مندرج في الحيوان، وإذ قد باين الحجر كل أفراد الحيوان، فلا بد أن يكون مبايناً لكل أفراد الإنسان، لأن هذه الأفراد هي بعض أفراد الحيوان.
٣ - ويكون القياس صحيحاً منتجاً عندما يُسلَّم فيه بأن الحد الأصغر مباين للحد الأوسط، ويكون الحد الأوسط مساوياً للحد الأكبر في حكم موجب .

2- The syllogism is valid and productive when it is conceded that the major term is opposed to the middle term, and the middle term includes or equals the minor term in a positive judgment, and the production in this case is negative production, and it relies on the intuitive principle that what is opposed to all individuals of a universal is opposed to every individual of it, and opposed to what is equal to it in extension. Thus when we say:
Every human is some animal and no animal is stone,
it necessarily follows intuitively that no human is stone, because human is included in animal, and since stone is opposed to all animals, it must be opposed to all humans, because these individuals are some individuals of animal.
3- The syllogism is valid and productive when it is conceded that the minor term is opposed to the middle term, and the middle term equals the major term in a positive judgment. And the production in this case is negative production, and it relies on the intuitive principle that what is opposed to all individuals of a universal is opposed to what is equal to it in extension. Thus when we say:
Every horse is not human and every human is every laughing,
it necessarily follows that every horse is not laughing.
4- No production from two negative premises.
5- The remaining general rules for a valid productive syllogism are observed.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of valid syllogisms, focusing on negative conclusions. The author presents two conditions for a valid negative syllogism: (2) the major term is opposed to the middle term, and the middle term includes or equals the minor in a positive judgment, leading to a negative conclusion. An example is given: 'Every human is some animal, and no animal is stone, therefore no human is stone.' The intuitive principle is that what is opposed to a universal is opposed to every individual under it. (3) The minor term is opposed to the middle term, and the middle equals the major in a positive judgment, also leading to a negative conclusion. The example: 'Every horse is not human, and every human is every laughing, therefore every horse is not laughing.' The principle here is that what is opposed to a universal is opposed to what equals it in extension. The author then states a general rule: (4) no conclusion follows from two negative premises, and (5) the remaining general rules for valid syllogisms must be observed. All examples are from everyday categories or Aristotelian/logical examples, but the concepts are universally applicable. In an Islamic context, examples could be drawn from fiqh or kalam, but here the author uses generic examples.

Three Valid Forms of Categorical Syllogism and the Rule of Two Negatives
Three valid syllogistic forms and the two-negatives rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three valid forms of categorical syllogism described by the author and apply the rule that two negative premises yield no conclusion.

Core Idea
Validity depends on term relations: coextension, inclusion, or exclusion.

This passage from the Dawabit al-Ma'rifa lays out the foundational conditions for a valid categorical syllogism. The author provides three specific forms—one affirmative and two negative—each grounded in rational intuition (badiha 'aqliyya). He also warns that two negative premises never produce a conclusion. Understanding these forms is essential for evaluating arguments in kalam and fiqh, where syllogistic reasoning is used to derive rulings and theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
In each syllogism, locate the minor term (subject of conclusion), middle term (appears in both premises), and major term (predicate of conclusion). For example, in the first form: 'human' (minor), 'speaking' (middle), 'laughing' (major).
2
Understand the first form (affirmative)
When all three terms are coextensive (identical in extension), the conclusion is affirmative. The author gives: every human is every speaking, every speaking is every laughing, therefore every human is every laughing.
3
Understand the second form (negative, major distinct)
When the major term is distinct from the middle (e.g., stone is not animal) and the middle includes the minor (human is part of animal), the conclusion is negative: no human is stone.
This relies on the intuition that what is distinct from all of a universal is distinct from each of its parts.
4
Understand the third form (negative, minor distinct)
When the minor term is distinct from the middle (horse is not human) and the middle is coextensive with the major (human = laughing), the conclusion is negative: no horse is laughing.
5
Apply the rule of two negatives
The author states that from two negative premises, no necessary conclusion follows. Test: 'No human is stone' and 'No stone is animal' do not imply any relation between human and animal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Every human is every speaking [being] and every speaking [being] is every laughing [being]. Its meaning is that every individual of whom 'human' is said, 'speaking' is said, and everyone of whom 'speaking' is said, 'laughing' is said. Therefore, the requirement of the unity of extension in the three terms—the minor, the middle, and the major—is that what is said of the middle is necessarily said of the minor. 2 - The syllogism is valid and productive when it is conceded that the major term is distinct from the middle term, and the middle term is inclusive of or coextensive with the minor term in an affirmative judgment. The production in this case is negative production, and it is based on rational intuition which dictates that what is distinct from all individuals of a universal is distinct from every individual of it, and distinct from what is coextensive with it in extension. For when we say: Every human is some animal, and no animal is a stone, then it necessarily follows that no human is a stone, because the human is included in the animal, and since stone is distinct from all individuals of the animal, it must be distinct from all individuals of the human, because these individuals are some individuals of the animal. 3 - The syllogism is valid and productive when it is conceded that the minor term is distinct from the middle term, and the middle term is coextensive with the major term in an affirmative judgment. The production in this case is negative production, and it is based on rational intuition which dictates that what is distinct from all individuals of a universal is distinct from what is coextensive with it in extension. For when we say: Every horse is not a human, and every human is every laughing [being], then it necessarily follows that every horse is not laughing. 4 - There is no production from two negative premises. 5 - The remaining general conditions for a valid productive syllogism are observed.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents rules for valid categorical syllogisms. It first gives an example of a Barbara syllogism (all A are B, all B are C, so all A are C) to illustrate unity of extension: if the middle term (natiq) is coextensive with both extremes, then the major applies to the minor. Then rule 2: when the major term is distinct from the middle (e.g., 'stone' is not 'animal'), and the middle includes the minor (e.g., 'human' is part of 'animal'), a negative conclusion follows (no human is stone). This relies on the intuition that if something is distinct from every member of a universal, it is distinct from each individual member. Rule 3: when the minor term is distinct from the middle (e.g., 'horse' is not 'human'), and the middle is coextensive with the major (e.g., 'human' equals 'laughing'), a negative conclusion follows (no horse is laughing). Rule 4 states that two negative premises yield no conclusion—a key principle in categorical logic. Rule 5 reminds that other general conditions must also be observed.

Key Takeaway
A valid categorical syllogism requires specific term relations; two negative premises never produce a conclusion.

The Syllogism

Introduction to Conditional Syllogism: Definition and Example
The author defines conditional syllogism and illustrates it with a medical example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent, consequent, and minor premise in a conditional syllogism and recognize the modus ponens form.

Core Idea
The conditional syllogism extracts a categorical premise from a conditional to reach a conclusion.

We have already studied the conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) in both categorical and conditional forms. Now we turn to the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), which has a different structure: it uses a conditional major premise and a categorical minor premise that affirms or denies one part of the conditional. This lesson will walk you through the definition and a concrete example so you can recognize this powerful argument form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional major premise
Look for a statement of the form 'If ... then ...' in the passage. This is the muqaddima shartiyya. In the example, it is 'If a human body is free from diseases, then food without excess does not harm it.'
The conditional premise consists of an antecedent (muqaddam) and a consequent (tali).
2
Extract the categorical minor premise
Notice that the minor premise is a categorical proposition taken from one of the parts of the conditional. In the example, the antecedent 'the body is free from diseases' is extracted and stated as 'But it is free from diseases.' The word 'but' (lakenna) signals the minor premise.
The minor premise can also be the contradictory of a part, but here it affirms the antecedent.
3
Derive the conclusion
When the antecedent is affirmed (modus ponens), the consequent necessarily follows: 'Therefore, food without excess does not harm this body.' Confirm that the conclusion is the consequent of the conditional.
This is the basic valid form: If P then Q; P; therefore Q.
4
Distinguish from conjunctive syllogism
Compare this structure with the conjunctive syllogism you already studied. Here the major is conditional and the minor is categorical; in the conjunctive syllogism, both premises are categorical. This difference is crucial for recognizing which type of syllogism you are dealing with.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القِيَاس الاستثنائي
سبق معنا أن القياس قسمان: اقتراني، واستثنائي، أما الاقتراني فقد وضح لنا تماماً بقسميه الحملي والشرطي، وبقي علينا أن نتعرف على الاستثنائي.
فالقياس الاستثنائي هو عملية فكرية ينطبق عليها التعريف العام للقياس، إلا أنه قائم على مقدمة شرطية توضع، ثم تؤخذ قضية حملية من أحد طرفيها، أو يؤخذ نقيضها، وتوضع في القياس مقرونة بلفظة (لكن) أو نحوها، وتكون هذه مقدمة ثانية، ثم تشتق منهما النتيجة، ويتضح ذلك بالأمثلة :
أ ـ من الشرطية المتصلة :
مقدم تالي
إذا كان جسم الإنسان سليماً من الأمراض ـ فالطعام بلا إسراف لا يضره .
هذه مقدمة كبرى وهي شرطية مؤلفة بالأصل من قضيتين حمليتين، إلا أن ارتباطهما بالشرط جعلهما قضية واحدة شرطية، كما سبق بيانه في القضايا .
ثم ننتزع إحدى قضيتيها الحمليتين، إما المقدم أو التالي فنقول :
لكنه سليم من الأمراض (على طريقة الجزم).
The Conditional Syllogism. It has preceded us that the syllogism is of two types: conjunctive (iqtirani) and conditional (istithna'i). As for the conjunctive, it has become completely clear to us in its two divisions: categorical and conditional. There remains for us to understand the conditional. The conditional syllogism is an intellectual process to which the general definition of syllogism applies, except that it is based on a conditional premise that is placed, then a categorical proposition is taken from one of its two parts, or its contradictory is taken, and it is placed in the syllogism accompanied by the word 'but' (lakenna) or the like, and this becomes a second premise. Then the conclusion is derived from both. This becomes clear with examples: a) From the conditional conjunctive [proposition]: antecedent consequent: If a human body is free from diseases, then food without excess does not harm it. This is a major premise, which is a conditional composed originally of two categorical propositions, but their connection by condition makes them a single conditional proposition, as previously explained in the section on propositions. Then we extract one of its two categorical propositions, either the antecedent or the consequent, and we say: But it is free from diseases (in the assertive manner).
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage introduces the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). The author first recalls that syllogisms are of two types: conjunctive (iqtirani) and conditional (istithna'i). The conjunctive has been covered in its categorical and conditional forms. Now the focus shifts to the conditional syllogism. It is defined as an intellectual process fitting the general definition of syllogism, but it operates on a conditional premise (shartiyya) as the major. Then a categorical proposition is taken from one of the two parts of the conditional (either the antecedent or the consequent, or their contradictories), and it is placed as a minor premise introduced by a word like 'but' (lakenna). The conclusion follows from the combination. An example is given from a conditional conjunctive (muttasila): 'If a human body is free from diseases, then food without excess does not harm it.' The antecedent is 'the body is free from diseases', the consequent is 'food without excess does not harm it'. The major premise is this conditional. Then we extract the antecedent and assert it as the minor premise: 'But it is free from diseases.' This yields the conclusion that food without excess does not harm it (modus ponens). The author notes that the conditional premise is composed of two categorical propositions linked by a condition, making them a single compound proposition.

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism uses a conditional major premise and a categorical minor premise that affirms or denies one part, leading to a necessary conclusion when correctly formed.

The Syllogism

Conditional Exceptive Syllogism
Defines exceptive syllogism with conditional example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a valid conditional exceptive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil) by extracting the antecedent, consequent, or their contradictories as the minor premise.

Core Idea
The exceptive syllogism extracts one part of a conditional to derive a necessary conclusion.

We have seen the conjunctive syllogism; now we turn to the exceptive. Its hallmark is a conditional major premise from which we extract one part (or its contradictory) as the minor premise—announced by 'but'—to obtain a conclusion. This lesson unpacks the structure with a fiqh example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional major premise
Find the 'if...then' statement. It consists of an antecedent (muqaddam) and a consequent (tali). For example: 'If water is pure (tahir), then wudu with it is valid.'
The premise must be a true conditional from Islamic knowledge.
2
Label the antecedent and consequent
Determine the two parts: muqaddam = 'water is pure', tali = 'wudu is valid'.
These are categorical propositions linked by the condition.
3
Choose the extraction type
You may extract the antecedent itself (affirming it) or its contradictory (denying it); or extract the consequent/its contradictory. In the example, extracting the antecedent gives: 'But water is pure.'
The extraction is indicated by 'but' (lākin) or similar.
4
Form the minor premise
Write the extracted proposition as the second premise, prefixed with 'but'. This is the istithna' (exception).
The minor premise is always categorical.
5
Derive the conclusion
If you affirmed the antecedent, the conclusion affirms the consequent (wudu is valid). If you denied the consequent, the conclusion denies the antecedent (water is not pure). Check that the conclusion follows necessarily.
This is modus ponens or modus tollens in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس الاستثنائي
سبق معنا أن القياس قسمان: اقتراني، واستثنائي، أما الاقتراني فقد وضح لنا تماماً بقسميه الحملي والشرطي، وبقي علينا أن نتعرف على الاستثنائي.
فالقياس الاستثنائي هو عملية فكرية ينطبق عليها التعريف العام للقياس، إلا أنه قائم على مقدمة شرطية توضع، ثم تؤخذ قضية حملية من أحد طرفيها، أو يؤخذ نقيضها، وتوضع في القياس مقرونة بلفظة (لكن) أو نحوها، وتكون هذه مقدمة ثانية، ثم تشتق منهما النتيجة، ويتضح ذلك بالأمثلة :
أ ـ من الشرطية المتصلة :
مقدم تالي
إذا كان جسم الإنسان سليماً من الأمراض ـ فالطعام بلا إسراف لا يضره .
هذه مقدمة كبرى وهي شرطية مؤلفة بالأصل من قضيتين حمليتين، إلا أن ارتباطهما بالشرط جعلهما قضية واحدة شرطية، كما سبق بيانه في القضايا .

ومقدمة صغرى : وهي الحملية المأخوذة من أحد طرفي الشرطية،
والواردة على طريقة الاستثناء من الشرطية، مقترنة بلفظ (لكن)(١).

.. فالطعام بلا إسراف لا يضره .
وهذه النتيجة هي تالي الكبرى بعينه .
وقد ننتزع من القضية الشرطية التي هي المقدمة الكبرى نقيض حمليتها
الثانية فنقول :

عندئذ تأتي النتيجة نقيض حمليتها الأخرى فنقول :
.. فهو ليس سليماً من الأمراض (النتيجة نقيض مقدم كبرى)
The Exceptive Syllogism. We previously mentioned that the syllogism is two types: conjunctive and exceptive. As for the conjunctive, it has been fully clarified in its two divisions: categorical and conditional. It remains for us to understand the exceptive. The exceptive syllogism is an intellectual process that falls under the general definition of syllogism, except that it is based on a conditional premise that is stated, then a categorical proposition is taken from one of its two parts, or its contradictory is taken, and it is placed in the syllogism accompanied by the word 'but' (lākin) or similar, and this becomes a second premise, then the conclusion is derived from them. This is clarified by examples: A. From the conditional (muttasila): antecedent consequent: 'If a person's body is free from diseases, then food without excess does not harm him.' This is a major premise, a conditional composed originally of two categorical propositions, but their linking by the condition makes them one conditional proposition, as previously explained in the section on propositions. Then we extract one of its two categorical propositions, either the antecedent or the consequent, and we say: 'But he is free from diseases' (in the manner of certainty). This is the second minor premise, clearly a categorical proposition, and we have thereby extracted the very antecedent of the major. We now have two premises: a major premise: the conditional proposition; and a minor premise: the categorical taken from one of the two parts of the conditional, brought in the manner of extraction from the conditional, accompanied by the word 'but' (lākin). Then we can derive the conclusion with certainty, saying: '... then food without excess does not harm him.' And this conclusion is the very consequent of the major. And we may extract from the conditional proposition (the major premise) the contradictory of its second categorical: we say: 'But food without excess harms him' (extraction of the contradictory of the consequent of the major). Then the conclusion comes as the contradictory of its other categorical: we say: '... then he is not free from diseases' (the conclusion is the contradictory of the antecedent of the major).
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the exceptive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). The author begins by reminding us of the two divisions of syllogism: conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive. Since the conjunctive has been covered, he now focuses on the exceptive. He defines it as a syllogism that starts with a conditional premise (shartiyya) and then extracts one of its two parts (the antecedent or consequent) or its contradictory, placing it as a second premise with the word 'but' (lākin). The conclusion follows directly. He then illustrates with a conditional (muttasila) example: 'If the body is free from diseases, then moderate food does not harm.' The antecedent is 'body free from diseases', the consequent is 'moderate food does not harm'. Two modes are shown: first, affirming the antecedent (he is free from diseases) leads to affirming the consequent (food does not harm). Second, denying the consequent (food harms him) leads to denying the antecedent (he is not free from diseases). This is the standard structure of the conditional exceptive syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil). In Islamic legal reasoning, a similar form is used: 'If the water is pure (tahir), then wudu with it is valid. But the water is pure, so wudu is valid.' This passage ensures the student understands the mechanics of extracting a premise and deriving a necessary conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The conditional exceptive syllogism allows you to affirm the consequent by affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) or deny the antecedent by denying the consequent (modus tollens), using a 'but' to introduce the extracted premise.

Disjunctive Exceptive Syllogism
Example of disjunctive exceptive syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid disjunctive exceptive syllogism and identify its components in an Islamic legal context.

Core Idea
Affirming one disjunct denies the other in an exclusive disjunction.

The author provides a concrete example of an exceptive syllogism from a disjunctive conditional. In this pattern, the major premise is a disjunction, the minor premise affirms one part, and the conclusion denies the other. This logical form is directly applicable to Islamic legal reasoning, where a jurist may be given two mutually exclusive rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive conditional (major premise)
Locate the 'إما...أو' (either...or) statement. In the example: 'Either the number is even or odd.' This is an exclusive disjunction where exactly one option is true.
2
Find the categorical premise (minor premise)
Look for the premise introduced by 'لكن' (but). In the example: 'But it is even.' This affirms one of the disjuncts.
3
Derive the conclusion
Since the disjunction is exclusive, affirming one disjunct forces the denial of the other. The conclusion is the negation of the other disjunct: 'Therefore, it is not odd.'
4
Translate to an Islamic legal example
Substitute the number parity with a fiqh ruling: 'Either this act is wajib or mubah. But it is wajib. Therefore it is not mubah.' This shows the same logical pattern.

Commentary
This passage presents an example of an exceptive syllogism from a disjunctive conditional (shartiyya munfasila). The disjunctive premise is 'Either the number is even or odd'—an exclusive disjunction (true only when one part is true). The minor premise affirms one disjunct: 'But it is even.' This extracts the antecedent (muqaddam) of the major. The conclusion is the negation of the other disjunct: 'Therefore, it is not odd.' This is the standard pattern for disjunctive syllogism: affirming one part denies the other. In Islamic legal reasoning, analogous reasoning appears: 'Either this act is obligatory (wajib) or forbidden (haram). It is obligatory, so it is not forbidden.' The footnote notes that the word 'but' (lākin) is called the 'letter of exception' because it extracts a certain proposition from an uncertain conditional. This passage reinforces the two types of exceptive syllogism (conditional and disjunctive) and their structure.

Connected Conditional Syllogism
Demonstrates connected conditional syllogism with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate the two valid moods (modus ponens and modus tollens) of a connected conditional syllogism using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
In a connected conditional, the conclusion follows only when the minor affirms the antecedent or denies the consequent.

This lesson focuses on the connected conditional syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i al-muttasil). The passage shows how to derive a conclusion by taking the antecedent or its negation as the minor premise. You will learn to identify the major conditional, the minor exception, and the resulting categorical conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
The major is a conditional proposition, e.g., 'If food is without excess, then it does not harm.' Note its antecedent and consequent.
The conditional must be true or assumed for the argument.
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor is a categorical that states or denies one part of the conditional. In the example, 'Food is without excess' affirms the antecedent. This is called an 'exception' (istithna').
The minor is always categorical and uses the particle 'lakenna' (but).
3
Derive the conclusion
If the minor affirms the antecedent, the conclusion is the consequent ('It does not harm'). If the minor denies the consequent (e.g., 'But food harms him'), the conclusion is the negation of the antecedent ('He is not healthy').
These correspond to modus ponens and modus tollens.
4
Verify validity
Check that the conclusion necessarily follows. Any other combination (e.g., denying antecedent) would be invalid.
Use an Islamic example to test: 'If the sale is usurious, it is forbidden.'

This passage demonstrates the mechanics of conditional syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i). The passage illustrates how the conclusion is always a categorical proposition that is either the antecedent, the consequent, or their contradictories, depending on the exception made.

Disjunctive Conditional Syllogism
Demonstrates disjunctive conditional syllogism with four moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the four valid moods of a disjunctive conditional syllogism and apply them to Islamic legal examples.

Core Idea
In a disjunctive syllogism with mutually exclusive parts, affirming one disjunct negates the other, and negating one affirms the other.

This lesson examines the disjunctive conditional syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i al-munfasil) as shown in the passage. The example 'the number is either even or odd' illustrates four valid forms. You will learn to identify the disjunction and apply the correct conclusion based on which part is affirmed or negated in the minor premise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive major premise
The major is a disjunctive conditional (shartiyya munfasila) with two alternatives, e.g., 'Either the number is even or odd.' Note that the disjunction is mutually exclusive (mani'at al-jam' wal-khuluw) – both cannot be true and both cannot be false.
In Islamic logic, this type is often used for binary legal categories (halal/haram, valid/invalid).
2
Examine the four possible minor premises
The minor can affirm one disjunct, deny one disjunct, affirm the negation of one, etc. The passage shows four: affirming 'even', affirming 'odd', negating 'odd', negating 'even'.
Each minor corresponds to a distinct rule for the conclusion.
3
Derive the conclusion for each mood
If the minor affirms one disjunct, the conclusion negates the other. If the minor negates one, the conclusion affirms the other. For example, 'it is even' yields 'it is not odd'; 'it is not even' yields 'it is odd'.
This is the law of excluded middle applied to binary exclusive disjunction.
4
Verify validity with an Islamic example
Take the disjunction: 'This animal is either halal or haram to eat.' If the minor affirms 'halal', conclusion is 'not haram'. If minor negates 'haram' (i.e., not haram), conclusion is 'halal'.
Ensure the disjunction is truly exclusive; otherwise the syllogism may be invalid.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This is the second minor premise, and it is a categorical proposition as is clear, and we have excepted in it the very antecedent of the major. Now we have two premises: a major premise: which is the conditional proposition. And a minor premise: which is the categorical taken from one of the two parts of the conditional, and presented in the manner of exception from the conditional, accompanied by the word (but). Then we can derive the conclusion with certainty and say: .. So food without excess does not harm him. And this conclusion is the very consequent of the major. And we may extract from the conditional proposition which is the major premise the contradictory of its second categorical and say: But food without excess harms him (exception of the contradictory of the consequent of the major). Then the conclusion comes as the contradictory of its other categorical, and we say: .. So he is not healthy from diseases (the conclusion is the contradictory of the antecedent of the major). B - From the disjunctive conditional: Antecedent Consequent: Either the number is even or odd (a conditional premise is the major). But it is even (a categorical premise is the minor). We excepted with it the very antecedent of the major. .. So it is not odd (the conclusion). And this conclusion is the contradictory of the consequent of the major. (1) The logicians call this letter the letter of exception, because it excepts from the uncertain conditional a categorical proposition that is certain. Or but it is odd (minor. We excepted with it the very consequent of the major) .. So it is not even (and this conclusion is the contradictory of the antecedent). Or but it is not odd (minor. We excepted with it the contradictory of the consequent) .. So it is even (and this conclusion is the very antecedent of the major). Or but it is not even (minor. We excepted with it the contradictory of the antecedent) .. So it is odd (and this conclusion is the very consequent of the major).
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage demonstrates the mechanics of conditional syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i). It begins by identifying the minor premise as a categorical proposition that is an 'exception' (istithna') taken from one of the two parts (antecedent or consequent) of the conditional major premise, accompanied by the exception particle 'lakenna' (but). The author then provides two sets of examples. First, with a connected conditional (shartiyya muttasila): from a conditional like 'If food is without excess, then it does not harm,' taking the antecedent as the minor premise yields the consequent as the conclusion. Conversely, taking the contradictory of the consequent as the minor yields the contradictory of the antecedent. Second, with a disjunctive conditional (shartiyya munfasila): from 'Either the number is even or odd,' four valid moods are shown—affirming the antecedent negates the consequent, affirming the consequent negates the antecedent, negating the consequent affirms the antecedent, and negating the antecedent affirms the consequent. A footnote clarifies that 'lakenna' is called the letter of exception because it extracts a certain categorical from an uncertain conditional. The passage illustrates how the conclusion is always a categorical proposition that is either the antecedent, the consequent, or their contradictories, depending on the exception made.

Classification of Conditional Syllogism into Connected and Disjunctive
Classification of conditional syllogism by major premise type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between connected and disjunctive conditional syllogisms by identifying their major premise types.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms split into two types based on the major premise: connected (if...then) or disjunctive (either...or).

In this passage, the author classifies the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) into two fundamental types. The key difference lies in the major premise: is it a connected conditional (shartiyya muttasila) or a disjunctive conditional (shartiyya munfasila)? Understanding this division is essential for applying the correct rules of inference in each type. The minor premise is always a categorical proposition with the exception particle 'lakenna', and the conclusion follows based on implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two divisions
The author states conditional syllogism is divided into two sections. Read the first sentence and note the two names: connected (muttasil) and disjunctive (munfasil).
2
Examine the major premise type
For each division, look at what type of proposition the major premise is. Connected has a connected conditional (if...then); disjunctive has a disjunctive conditional (either...or).
3
Analyze the minor premise
The text says the minor is a categorical proposition accompanied by the exception particle 'lakenna'. This particle indicates that the minor affirms or negates one part of the major.
The minor is always categorical, not conditional.
4
Understand the conclusion possibilities
The conclusion can be the antecedent, the consequent, or their contradictories, depending on what the minor exception is. The type of the major premise affects which conclusion follows validly.
For connected, the valid inferences are modus ponens and modus tollens. For disjunctive, the valid inference eliminates one disjunct.

The Syllogism

The Connected Conditional Syllogism: Definition, Conditions, and Valid Moods
Author defines the connected conditional syllogism, its conditions, and its two valid moods with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a connected conditional syllogism and determine whether a given mood is valid.

Core Idea
Only affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent yields certainty.

In this lesson, we explore the connected conditional syllogism, a key tool in Islamic logic. The author defines its structure, sets conditions for its validity, and identifies the only two moods that produce certain conclusions. Understanding these moods is essential for sound reasoning in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Find the major premise: a connected conditional (If A, then B). Find the minor premise: a categorical 'but' clause that either affirms A or denies B.
The minor is called istithna'iyya (exceptive).
2
Check the conditions
The major must be affirmative and express necessary implication (luzum), not coincidence. The minor must use 'but' or similar. At least one premise must be universal (or treated as such).
A coincidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) is invalid.
3
Identify the two valid moods
Mood 1: Affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) yields the consequent. Mood 2: Denying the consequent (modus tollens) yields denial of the antecedent.
Affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent are fallacies.
4
Apply to a fiqh example
Example: 'If wine is intoxicating, it is forbidden (major). But wine is intoxicating (minor, affirming antecedent). Therefore wine is forbidden (conclusion).' This is a valid modus ponens.
This syllogism is used by jurists to derive rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو لكنه فرد (صغرى. استثنينا بها تالي الكبرى بعينه) .. فهو ليس زوجاً (وهذه النتيجة هي نقيض المقدم) أو لكنه ليس فرداً (صغرى. استثنينا بها نقيض التالي) .. فهو زوج (وهذه النتيجة هي مقدم الكبرى بعينه) أو لكنه ليس زوجاً (صغرى. استثنينا بها نقيض المقدم) .. فهو فرد (وهذه النتيجة هي تالي الكبرى بعينه) أقسام القياس الاستثنائي: غدا من الواضح لدينا عن طريق الأمثلة أن القياس الاستثنائي ينقسم إلى قسمين: القسم الأول: استثنائي متصل، وهو ما كانت مقدمته الكبرى شرطية متصلة. القسم الثاني: استثنائي منفصل، وهو ما كانت مقدمته الكبرى شرطية منفصلة. وصغرى كلّ منهما قضية حملية مقترنة بأداة الاستثناء: (لكن)، والاستثناء بها يكون لمقدم الكبرى أو لتاليها. والنتيجة تأتي قضية حملية مجزوماً بها، هي مقدم الكبرى أو تاليها، أو نقيض مقدمها، أو نقيض تاليها، حسبما يقتضيه اللزوم. ولكل قسم من هذين القسمين ضوابط لإنتاجه يجب مراعاتها، حتى يكون إنتاجاً صحيحاً مسلماً به، لا تتخلف عنه الصحة متى حصل التسليم بقضيتيه الكبرى والصغرى. فلنبين ذلك فيما يلي: القياس الاستثنائي المتصل: هو ـ كما علمنا ـ القياس المؤلف من مقدمتين: كبرى وصغرى. المقدمة الكبرى: قضية شرطية متصلة، ويشترط فيها أن تكون موجبة لزومية لا اتفاقية. المقدمة الصغرى: قضية حملية مقترنة بلفظة (لكن) أو نحوها، وتسمى استثنائية. ويستثنى بالمقدمة الصغرى مقدَّمُ الشرطية المتصلة الموجبة اللزومية أو نقيض تاليها. ويجب أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين كلية (والشخصية بقوة الكلية). وهذا القياس ينتج إنتاجاً صحيحاً مطرداً في حالتين فقط: الحالة الأولى: وضع المقدم (أي إثباته) ينتج وضع التالي (أي: ينتج إثبات التالي). الحالة الثانية: رفع التالي (أي نفيه) ينتج رفع المقدم. وذلك لأن إثبات الملزوم يقتضي عقلاً إثبات لازمه، ونفي اللازم يقتضي عقلاً نفي الملزوم. فوجود السقف المرفوع يستلزم وجود القواعد الرافعة له، فالسقف المرفوع ملزوم، والقواعد الرافعة له لازمة له. عندئذ نصوغ قضية شرطية تبين هذا اللزوم فنقول: دائماً إذا كان السقف مرفوعاً فهو على قواعد تحمله. وهنا فقد يظهر لنا سقف مرفوع، وتختفي عنا القواعد التي رفعته، فنقول بموجب القياس: لكن هذا السقف مرفوع. (فنضع مقدم قضيتنا الكبرى). وهذا يستلزم وضع التالي، وعندئذ يسوغ لنا أن نستنتج القضية المطلوبة فنقول:
Or but it is odd (minor premise: we have excluded the consequent of the major premise itself) ... then it is not even (and this result is the contradictory of the antecedent). Or but it is not odd (minor premise: we have excluded the contradictory of the consequent) ... then it is even (and this result is the antecedent of the major premise itself). Or but it is not even (minor premise: we have excluded the contradictory of the antecedent) ... then it is odd (and this result is the consequent of the major premise itself). Sections of the conditional syllogism: It has become clear to us through examples that the conditional syllogism is divided into two sections: First section: connected conditional, which is that whose major premise is a connected conditional proposition. Second section: disjunctive conditional, which is that whose major premise is a disjunctive conditional proposition. And the minor premise of each is a categorical proposition connected with the exception tool: (but), and the exception by it is for the antecedent of the major premise or for its consequent. And the result comes as a categorical proposition that is certain, being the antecedent of the major premise or its consequent, or the contradictory of its antecedent, or the contradictory of its consequent, according to what the implication requires. And for each of these two sections there are controls for its production that must be observed, so that it is a correct and accepted production, whose correctness does not fail when the two premises (major and minor) are granted. So let us explain that in the following: The connected conditional syllogism: It is, as we know, the syllogism composed of two premises: major and minor. The major premise: a connected conditional proposition, and it is required that it be affirmative and implicative (luzumiyya), not coincidental (ittifaqiyya). The minor premise: a categorical proposition connected with the word (but) or similar, and it is called the exception (istithna'iyya). And by the minor premise, the antecedent of the affirmative implicative connected conditional or the contradictory of its consequent is excepted. And one of the two premises must be universal (and the particular is like the universal in strength). And this syllogism produces a correct and consistent result only in two cases: The first case: affirming the antecedent (i.e., proving it) produces affirming the consequent (i.e., produces proving the consequent). The second case: denying the consequent (i.e., negating it) produces denying the antecedent. That is because proving the implied (malzum) rationally necessitates proving its implication (lazim), and negating the implication rationally necessitates negating the implied. So the existence of the raised roof necessitates the existence of the supports raising it. The raised roof is the implied, and the supports raising it are its implication. At that point we formulate a conditional proposition showing this implication, saying: Always if the roof is raised, then it is on supports carrying it. And here, a raised roof may appear to us, and the supports that raised it may be hidden from us, so we say according to the syllogism: But this roof is raised. (So we affirm the antecedent of our major premise.) And this necessitates affirming the consequent, and then it is permissible for us to conclude the required proposition, saying:
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage explains the connected conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil), a type of syllogism where the major premise is a connected conditional (e.g., 'If A, then B'), and the minor premise is a categorical 'but' clause that either affirms the antecedent or denies the consequent. The author first distinguishes this from the disjunctive type, then gives its specific conditions: the major must be affirmative and implicative (luzumiyya), not coincidental (ittifaqiyya); the minor must be a categorical with 'but' or similar; and one of the two premises must be universal (with particulars treated as universal in strength). The syllogism yields a correct conclusion only in two moods: (1) affirming the antecedent (wad' al-muqaddam) leads to affirming the consequent (wad' al-tali)—e.g., 'If the world is temporally originated, it has a creator; but the world is temporally originated; therefore it has a creator'; and (2) denying the consequent (raf' al-tali) leads to denying the antecedent (raf' al-muqaddam)—e.g., 'If the world is temporally originated, it has a creator; but it has no creator (contradictory of consequent); therefore the world is not temporally originated.' The author provides a concrete example of a raised roof requiring supports: the raised roof is the implied (malzum), its supports are the implication (lazim). Affirming the existence of the raised roof forces us to affirm the existence of the supports. Denying the existence of the supports forces us to deny the raised roof. The logical principle is that affirming the malzum necessitates affirming its lazim, and negating the lazim necessitates negating the malzum. This is a foundational rule in Islamic logic used extensively in fiqh (e.g., if a contract is valid, it produces its legal effects; but this contract is valid, therefore it produces its effects) and kalam (e.g., if the world is created, it needs a creator; but the world is created, so it needs a creator).

Key Terms
قياس استثنائي (qiyas istithna'i): A syllogism whose major premise is a conditional (muttasil or munfasil) and minor premise is a categorical exceptive ('but') clause that affirms or denies part of the major. [Note: Stable across the tradition; fakhr al-Din al-Razi uses the same division.]
متصل (muttasil): A conditional proposition that asserts a relation of implication (luzum) between antecedent and consequent, not mere coincidence. [Note: No significant shift.]
مقدم (muqaddam): The antecedent of a conditional proposition; in the syllogism, it is the part that may be affirmed or denied in the minor premise. [Note: Stable term.]
تالي (tali): The consequent of a conditional proposition; in the syllogism, its denial leads to denial of the antecedent. [Note: Stable.]
وضع المقدم (wad' al-muqaddam): Affirming the antecedent in the minor premise; one of the two valid moods producing the consequent. [Note: Standard terminology.]
رفع التالي (raf' al-tali): Denying the consequent in the minor premise; the other valid mood producing the denial of the antecedent. [Note: Standard.]
لزوم (luzum): Implication or necessary connection between malzum (implied) and lazim (implication); the major premise must express this, not mere accidental conjunction. [Note: In kalam, luzum often refers to causal necessity; in mantiq, it is broader.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Affirming the antecedent

Key Takeaway
The connected conditional syllogism yields certainty only through affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) or denying the consequent (modus tollens).

The Syllogism

Valid and Invalid Moods of Conditional Syllogism
Author defines conditional syllogism, its premises, valid moods, and invalid combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two valid moods of a conditional syllogism (modus ponens and modus tollens) and distinguish them from the invalid moods (denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent).

Core Idea
Conditional syllogism has only two valid moods: affirming antecedent or denying consequent.

Conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i muttasil) are widely used in Islamic legal reasoning and theology. A correct understanding of which moods yield certain conclusions is essential to avoid fallacious arguments. This lesson walks through the structure and the two valid moods as taught in classical logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise
The major premise is a conditional (shartiyya muttasila) that must be affirmative and implicative (luzumiyya), meaning the consequent necessarily follows from the antecedent. Note the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali).
Example: 'If the roof is elevated, then it is upon pillars that bear it.'
2
Identify the minor premise
The minor premise is a categorical proposition introduced by 'lakinna' (but) that either affirms the antecedent (wad' al-muqaddam) or denies the consequent (raf' al-tali). These are the only two that produce valid conclusions.
Affirming antecedent: 'But this roof is elevated.' Denying consequent: 'But there are no pillars bearing this roof.'
3
Determine the valid conclusion
If the minor premise affirms the antecedent, conclude that the consequent is true (modus ponens). If it denies the consequent, conclude that the antecedent is false (modus tollens). These are the only two valid moods.
Invalid: denying antecedent or affirming consequent produce no necessary conclusion.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Apply to a fiqh example: Major: 'If something is intoxicating, it is forbidden.' Minor (modus ponens): 'But this drink is intoxicating.' Conclusion: 'Therefore, it is forbidden.' Check that the major premise is luzumiyya.

المقدمة الكبرى: قضية شرطية متصلة، ويشترط فيها أن تكون موجبة لزومية لا اتفاقية. المقدمة الصغرى: قضية حملية مقترنة بلفظة (لكن) أو نحوها، وتسمى استثنائية. ويستثنى بالمقدمة الصغرى مقدَّمُ الشرطية المتصلة الموجبة اللزومية أو نقيض تاليها. ويجب أن تكون إحدى المقدمتين كلية (والشخصية بقوة الكلية). وهذا القياس ينتج إنتاجاً صحيحاً مطرداً في حالتين فقط: الحالة الأولى: وضع المقدم (أي إثباته) ينتج وضع التالي (أي: ينتج إثبات التالي). الحالة الثانية: رفع التالي (أي نفيه) ينتج رفع المقدم. وذلك لأن إثبات الملزوم يقتضي عقلاً إثبات لازمه، ونفي اللازم يقتضي عقلاً نفي الملزوم. فوجود السقف المرفوع يستلزم وجود القواعد الرافعة له، فالسقف المرفوع ملزوم، والقواعد الرافعة له لازمة له. عندئذ نصوغ قضية شرطية تبين هذا اللزوم فنقول: دائماً إذا كان السقف مرفوعاً فهو على قواعد تحمله. وهنا فقد يظهر لنا سقف مرفوع، وتختفي عنا القواعد التي رفعته، فنقول بموجب القياس: لكن هذا السقف مرفوع. (فنضع مقدم قضيتنا الكبرى). وهذا يستلزم وضع التالي، وعندئذ يسوغ لنا أن نستنتج القضية المطلوبة فنقول: فهو على قواعد تحمله. وقد يختفي عنا واقع حال السقف هل هو مرفوع أو لا؟ ولكن يظهر لنا عدم وجود القواعد التي يمكن أن تكون رافعة له، فنقول بموجب القياس: لكن لا توجد قواعد تحمل السقف فنرفع تالي قضيتنا الكبرى). ورفع هذا التالي يستلزم رفع المقدم، وعندئذ يسوغ لنا أن نستنتج القضية المطلوبة فنقول: فالسقف غير مرفوع. لكن رفع المقدم لا يستلزم رفع التالي ولا وضعه، وكذلك وضع التالي لا يستلزم وضع المقدم ولا رفعه، فهاتان الحالتان إذن لا تنتجان إنتاجاً صحيحاً مطرداً. فإذا قلنا: لكن السقف غير مرفوع (فرفعنا المقدم). فهل يستلزم هذا كون قواعد السقف غير قائمة أو هي قائمة؟ من الممكن أن توجد قواعد قائمة لا سقف عليها، ومن الممكن أن لا يوجد سقف ولا قواعد، إذن فرفع المقدم لا يستلزم شيئاً في التالي، فهو غير منتج. وإذا قلنا: لكن قواعد السقف قائمة (فوضعنا التالي). فهل يستلزم هذا كون السقف مرفوعاً أو غير مرفوع؟ من الممكن أن توجد قواعد قائمة لا سقف عليها، ومن الممكن أن توجد قواعد قائمة وعليها سقف، إذن فوضع التالي لا يستلزم شيئاً في المقدم، فهو غير منتج. قد نجد في بعض الأمثلة من القضايا الشرطية المتصلة أن وضع كل من المقدم والتالي يستلزم وضع الآخر، ورفع كل من المقدم والتالي يستلزم رفع الآخر، ولكن هذا يرجع إلى وجود التلازم المطرد المنعكس بين المقدم
The major premise: a conditional conjunctive proposition, and it is required that it be affirmative and implicative, not accidental. The minor premise: a categorical proposition coupled with the word 'but' or similar, and it is called exceptive. By the minor premise, the antecedent of the affirmative implicative conditional is excepted, or the contradictory of its consequent. And one of the two premises must be universal (and the personal is in the strength of the universal). This syllogism yields correct and consistent production in only two cases: The first case: positing the antecedent (i.e., affirming it) yields positing the consequent (i.e., yields affirming the consequent). The second case: removing the consequent (i.e., denying it) yields removing the antecedent. That is because affirming the necessitating entailment rationally necessitates affirming its necessary consequent, and denying the necessary consequent rationally necessitates denying the necessitating. So the existence of an elevated roof necessitates the existence of pillars raising it; the elevated roof is the necessitating, and the pillars raising it are its necessary consequent. At that point we formulate a conditional proposition showing this necessitation, saying: Whenever the roof is elevated, it is upon pillars that bear it. Here, an elevated roof may appear to us, while the pillars that raised it are hidden from us, so we say by the syllogism: But this roof is elevated. (Thus we posit the antecedent of our major premise.) And this necessitates positing the consequent, and at that point it is permissible for us to conclude the desired proposition, saying: Thus it is upon pillars that bear it. And the actual state of the roof—whether it is elevated or not—may be hidden from us. But we see the absence of pillars that could be raising it, so we say by the syllogism: But there are no pillars bearing the roof (thus we remove the consequent of our major premise). And removing this consequent necessitates removing the antecedent, and at that point it is permissible for us to conclude the desired proposition, saying: Thus the roof is not elevated. However, removing the antecedent does not necessitate removing the consequent nor positing it, and similarly positing the consequent does not necessitate positing the antecedent nor removing it. These two cases, then, do not yield correct and consistent production. So if we say: But the roof is not elevated (thus we removed the antecedent), does this necessitate that the pillars of the roof are not standing or are standing? It is possible that standing pillars exist without a roof upon them, and it is possible that there is neither roof nor pillars. So removing the antecedent does not necessitate anything in the consequent; it is unproductive. And if we say: But the pillars of the roof are standing (thus we posited the consequent), does this necessitate that the roof is elevated or not elevated? It is possible that standing pillars exist without a roof upon them, and it is possible that standing pillars exist with a roof upon them. So positing the consequent does not necessitate anything in the antecedent; it is unproductive. We may find in some examples of conditional conjunctive propositions that positing each of the antecedent and consequent necessitates positing the other, and removing each necessitates removing the other, but this is due to the existence of reciprocal constant necessitation between the antecedent
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage explains the structure and valid moods of the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil). The author begins by defining the two premises. The major premise is a conditional proposition (shartiyya muttasila) that must be affirmative (mujiba) and implicative (luzumiyya), not accidental (ittifaqiyya)--meaning the connection between antecedent and consequent is a necessary logical consequence, not a coincidental conjunction. The minor premise is a categorical proposition (hamliyya) introduced by a word like 'lakinna' (but) or similar, and is called 'istithna'iyya' (exceptive) because it affirms or denies one part of the conditional. Specifically, the minor premise either affirms the antecedent (muqaddam) of the major premise or denies the consequent (tali). Additionally, one of the two premises must be universal (kulliyya); a singular proposition (shakhsiyya) has the force of a universal because its subject is one individual, so it counts as universal for the purpose of the syllogism. The author then states that this syllogism yields correct and consistent results in only two cases. The first case is 'wad' al-muqaddam' (positing the antecedent): affirming the antecedent in the minor premise necessitates affirming the consequent in the conclusion. This is valid because affirming a necessitating principle (malzum) implies affirming its necessary consequent (lazim). The second case is 'raf' al-tali' (removing the consequent): denying the consequent necessitates denying the antecedent, because denying the necessary consequent implies denying the necessitating principle that would entail it. To illustrate, the author gives a concrete example: a roof and its supporting pillars. The major premise states a necessary relation: 'Whenever the roof is elevated, it is upon pillars that bear it.' The roof is the necessitating (malzum), and the pillars are the necessary consequent (lazim). If we observe an elevated roof (affirming the antecedent), we infer that pillars must exist (affirming the consequent). If we observe the absence of pillars (denying the consequent), we infer that the roof is not elevated (denying the antecedent). The author then explains why the other two moods are invalid. 'Raf' al-muqaddam' (removing the antecedent): if we deny the antecedent (say the roof is not elevated), we cannot conclude anything about the consequent (pillars may still exist or not). 'Wad' al-tali' (positing the consequent): if we affirm the consequent (say pillars are standing), we cannot conclude whether the roof is elevated or not. These are invalid because they do not produce certain conclusions. Finally, the author notes that in some conditional propositions, there may be a reciprocal necessitation (talazum mutarid mun'akis) where the antecedent and consequent imply each other. But that is a special case due to the nature of the relation, not a general rule. This lesson is crucial because it teaches the proper use of conditional syllogisms, which are common in Islamic legal reasoning (istidlal) and theological arguments (kalam). For example, in jurisprudence: 'If something is intoxicating, then it is forbidden (haram). This drink is intoxicating (minor premise affirming antecedent). Therefore, it is forbidden.' Or: 'This drink is not forbidden (denying consequent). Therefore, it is not intoxicating.'

A conditional syllogism yields a certain conclusion only when the minor premise affirms the antecedent or denies the consequent; all other moods are invalid in the general case.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Valid and Invalid Modes of Conditional Syllogism
Author explains the two valid modes and the two invalid modes of conditional syllogism with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two valid modes of a conditional syllogism and distinguish them from the invalid modes using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Only affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent are universally valid in a conditional syllogism.

This passage from Dawabid al-Marifa explains the four possible modes of a conditional (istithna'i muttaşil) syllogism. You will learn why two modes are always valid and two are not, and how a special case of reciprocal entailment can trick the unwary. The lesson ends with a powerful Quranic example that demonstrates the valid mode of denying the consequent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional major premise
Identify the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) in a conditional statement of the form 'if p then q'.
Example from the passage: 'If the ceiling is raised, then there are supports holding it.' Antecedent: ceiling raised; consequent: supports exist.
2
Identify the two valid modes
Valid mode 1: Affirm antecedent → affirm consequent (modus ponens). Valid mode 2: Deny consequent → deny antecedent (modus tollens). The passage shows these with the ceiling example.
These are the only universally productive modes.
3
Identify the two invalid modes
Invalid mode 1: Deny antecedent → no necessary conclusion about consequent. Invalid mode 2: Affirm consequent → no necessary conclusion about antecedent. The passage explains why these fail with possibility cases.
These are called 'non-productive' (ghayr muntij).
4
Examine the special case of reciprocal entailment
When antecedent and consequent share a reciprocal constant entailment (talazum muttarid munakis), all four modes appear valid. The passage uses the example 'If sun is risen, then day exists'.
This is not a general rule; it depends on the specific relationship between p and q.
5
Apply the valid modes to the Quranic example
From Quran 21:22: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted.' The minor premise denies the consequent: 'But they have not been corrupted.' Conclusion denies the antecedent: 'So there are not in them gods besides Allah.' This is modus tollens.
This shows the real use of conditional syllogism in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يختفي عنا واقع حال السقف هل هو مرفوع أو لا؟ ولكن يظهر لنا عدم وجود القواعد التي يمكن أن تكون رافعة له، فنقول بموجب القياس: لكن لا توجد قواعد تحمل السقف فنرفع تالي قضيتنا الكبرى. ورفع هذا التالي يستلزم رفع المقدم، وعندئذ يسوغ لنا أن نستنتج القضية المطلوبة فنقول: فالسقف غير مرفوع.
لكن رفع المقدم لا يستلزم رفع التالي ولا وضعه، وكذلك وضع التالي لا يستلزم وضع المقدم ولا رفعه، فهاتان الحالتان إذن لا تنتجان إنتاجاً صحيحاً مطرداً.
فإذا قلنا: لكن السقف غير مرفوع (فرفعنا المقدم). فهل يستلزم هذا كون قواعد السقف غير قائمة أو هي قائمة؟ من الممكن أن توجد قواعد قائمة لا سقف عليها، ومن الممكن أن لا يوجد سقف ولا قواعد، إذن فرفع المقدم لا يستلزم شيئاً في التالي، فهو غير منتج.
وإذا قلنا: لكن قواعد السقف قائمة (فوضعنا التالي). فهل يستلزم هذا كون السقف مرفوعاً أو غير مرفوع؟ من الممكن أن توجد قواعد قائمة لا سقف عليها، ومن الممكن أن توجد قواعد قائمة وعليها سقف، إذن فوضع التالي لا يستلزم شيئاً في المقدم، فهو غير منتج.
فمن الأمثلة المشتملة على التلازم المطرد المنعكس بين مقدم القضية الشرطية المتصلة وتاليها، المثال الذي يتكرر استعماله على ألسنة المناطقة: دائماً إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود. ففي هذا المثال ونظائره نلاحظ أن رفع كل من المقدم والتالي يستلزم رفع الآخر، ووضع كل من المقدم والتالي يستلزم وضع الآخر. فنقول: لكن الشمس غير طالعة ∴ فالنهار غير موجود. لكن النهار غير موجود ∴ فالشمس غير طالعة. لكن الشمس طالعة ∴ فالنهار موجود. لكن النهار موجود ∴ فالشمس طالعة. وصدق الإنتاج هنا في الحالات الأربع، لخصوص المثال وما فيه من تلازم مطرد منعكس بين المقدم والتالي، ولا يصدق في كل الأمثلة.
لذلك فالإنتاج الصحيح المطرد للقياس الاستثنائي المتصل منحصر في حالتين فقط، هما كما سبق: وضع المقدم ∴ وضع التالي، رفع التالي ∴ رفع المقدم.
تطبيقات على القياس الاستثنائي المتصل: مقدم تالي. أ ـ لو كان فيهما آلهة إلا الله لفسدتا (الكبرى). أي: لكنهما لم تفسدا (الصغرى. وقد رفع فيها التالي). ∴ فليس فيهما آلهة إلا الله (النتيجة. وقد رفع فيها المقدم).
And the actual state of the ceiling may be hidden from us: is it raised or not? But the absence of the supports that could hold it appears to us, so we say by virtue of the syllogism: But there are no supports holding the ceiling, so we deny the consequent of our major proposition. And this denial of the consequent necessitates the denial of the antecedent, and then it is permissible for us to deduce the required proposition, so we say: So the ceiling is not raised.
But the denial of the antecedent does not necessitate the denial of the consequent nor its affirmation, and similarly the affirmation of the consequent does not necessitate the affirmation of the antecedent nor its denial. So these two cases, then, do not yield a correct and uniform conclusion.
For if we say: But the ceiling is not raised (so we denied the antecedent). Does this necessitate that the supports of the ceiling are non-existent or existent? It is possible that there are existing supports without a ceiling, and it is possible that there is neither a ceiling nor supports. So the denial of the antecedent does not necessitate anything in the consequent; it is non-productive.
And if we say: But the supports of the ceiling are existent (so we affirmed the consequent). Does this necessitate that the ceiling is raised or not raised? It is possible that there are existing supports without a ceiling, and it is possible that there are existing supports with a ceiling on them. So the affirmation of the consequent does not necessitate anything in the antecedent; it is non-productive.
Among examples that contain reciprocal constant entailment between the antecedent and consequent of a conditional conjunctive proposition is the example that is frequently used by logicians: Always if the sun is risen then day exists. In this example and its likes, we observe that denying each of the antecedent and the consequent necessitates denying the other, and affirming each of the antecedent and the consequent necessitates affirming the other. So we say: But the sun is not risen ∴ day does not exist. But day does not exist ∴ the sun is not risen. But the sun is risen ∴ day exists. But day exists ∴ the sun is risen. The truth of the conclusion here in all four cases is due to the specificity of the example and the reciprocal constant entailment between the antecedent and the consequent, and it is not true in all examples.
Therefore, the correct and uniform production of the conjunctive exceptional syllogism is confined to two cases only, as previously: affirming the antecedent ∴ affirming the consequent, denying the consequent ∴ denying the antecedent.
Applications of the conjunctive exceptional syllogism: Antecedent, consequent. A: If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted (major). i.e.: But they have not been corrupted (minor, where the consequent is denied). ∴ So there are not in them gods besides Allah (conclusion, where the antecedent is denied).
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage explains the valid and invalid modes of a conditional syllogism (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī al-muttaṣil). The author begins by illustrating a scenario: we do not know if a ceiling is raised, but we observe that its supports are absent. Using a conditional major premise (e.g., "If the ceiling is raised, then there are supports holding it"), we reason: "But there are no supports; therefore the ceiling is not raised." This is the valid mode of denying the consequent to deny the antecedent (rafʿ al-tālī yastalzim rafʿ al-muqaddam).

The author then warns: denying the antecedent (rafʿ al-muqaddam) does not yield a necessary conclusion about the consequent—the ceiling might not be raised even if supports exist, or both could be absent. Similarly, affirming the consequent (waḍʿ al-tālī) does not necessitate affirming the antecedent—supports can exist without a ceiling. Thus, these two modes are invalid (ghayr muntij).

To contrast, the author gives the classic logical example "If the sun is risen, then day exists," where there is a reciprocal constant entailment (talāzum muṭṭarid munʿakis) between antecedent and consequent. In such a case, all four modes appear valid, but this is due to the specific nature of the example—sunrise and daylight are mutually entailing—not a general rule.

The author concludes that the only two universally valid modes for a conditional syllogism are: (1) affirming the antecedent to affirm the consequent (waḍʿ al-muqaddam → waḍʿ al-tālī), and (2) denying the consequent to deny the antecedent (rafʿ al-tālī → rafʿ al-muqaddam).

Finally, an Islamic application is provided from Quran 21:22: "If there were in them (the heavens and earth) gods besides Allah, they would both have been corrupted." The minor premise denies the consequent: "But they have not been corrupted." Therefore, the antecedent is denied: "So there are not in them gods besides Allah." This demonstrates the valid mode of denying the consequent.

Key Terms
القضية الشرطية المتصلة (al-qaḍiyya al-sharṭiyya al-muttaṣila): A conditional proposition of the form 'if p then q' (law kāna ... la-...), where truth depends on the connection between antecedent and consequent. [Note: This term is standard across Mantiq texts; no significant shift.]
المقدم (al-muqaddam): The antecedent clause in a conditional proposition (the 'if' part). [Note: Stable term.]
التالي (al-tālī): The consequent clause in a conditional proposition (the 'then' part). [Note: Stable term.]
القياس الاستثنائي المتصل (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī al-muttaṣil): A syllogism composed of a conditional major premise and a categorical minor premise that either affirms or denies one part of the conditional, leading to a conclusion about the other part. [Note: Standard term across Mantiq; no significant shift.]
التلازم المطرد المنعكس (al-talāzum al-muṭṭarid al-munʿakis): Reciprocal constant entailment between antecedent and consequent, such that each implies the other and each negation implies the other's negation. [Note: This concept appears in discussions of material equivalence; the term is used in some later commentaries but not in earlier matn texts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Affirming the antecedent and affirming the consequent

C) Affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent
D) Denying the antecedent and denying the consequent

Key Takeaway
In a conditional syllogism, only affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent yields a necessary conclusion.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Two Valid Moods of Conditional Syllogism
Author defines valid moods and provides four applied examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two valid moods of a conditional syllogism and evaluate whether an example correctly applies them.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogism: only affirm the antecedent or deny the consequent.

In this passage, the author explains the core rule of conditional syllogism and then demonstrates its application in four Quranic and historical examples. This is a classic instance of theory meeting practice in Islamic logic, showing how logical tools are used in tawhid and prophetic proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional major premise
Find the 'if...then...' statement. This is the major premise (al-kubra). It establishes a necessary connection between antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali).
In the examples, the major premise is a Quranic verse.
2
Identify the minor premise action
Determine whether the minor premise affirms the antecedent (waḍ‘) or denies the consequent (raf‘). The valid moods are exactly these two.
Denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent are invalid unless entailment is reciprocal.
3
Check the conclusion
If you affirmed the antecedent, the conclusion must affirm the consequent. If you denied the consequent, the conclusion must deny the antecedent.
The conclusion follows necessarily from the premises if the conditional is true.
4
Test for reciprocal entailment
Some conditionals, like 'if sun is rising then daytime exists', have reciprocal entailment. In such cases all four moods are valid, but this is rare.
Do not assume reciprocal entailment unless proven.
5
Evaluate soundness
A syllogism may be formally valid but unsound if a premise is false. The polytheists' argument is formally valid but the major premise is false.
Always verify the truth of the conditional premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتالي، وهذا لا يوجد في كل الأمثلة، لذلك لا يصح أن يتخذ مستنداً ثابتاً للقياس.
فمن الأمثلة المشتملة على التلازم المطرد المنعكس بين مقدم القضية الشرطية المتصلة وتاليها، المثال الذي يتكرر استعماله على ألسنة المناطقة: دائماً إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود.
ففي هذا المثال ونظائره نلاحظ أن رفع كل من المقدم والتالي يستلزم رفع الآخر، ووضع كل من المقدم والتالي يستلزم وضع الآخر، فنقول:
لكن الشمس غير طالعة ∴ فالنهار غير موجود
لكن النهار غير موجود ∴ فالشمس غير طالعة
لكن الشمس طالعة ∴ فالنهار موجود
لكن النهار موجود ∴ فالشمس طالعة
وصدق الإنتاج هنا في الحالات الأربع، لخصوص المثال وما فيه من تلازم مطرد منعكس بين المقدم والتالي، ولا يصدق في كل الأمثلة.
لذلك فالإنتاج الصحيح المطرد للقياس الاستثنائي المتصل منحصر في حالتين فقط، هما كما سبق:
وضع المقدم ∴ وضع التالي قانون إنتاج القياس
رفع التالي ∴ رفع المقدم الاستثنائي المتصل
تطبيقات على القياس الاستثنائي المتصل:

أ ـ لو كان فيهما آلهة إلا الله لفسدتا (الكبرى).
أي : لكنهما لم تفسدا (الصغرى. وقد رفع فيها التالي).
∴ فليس فيهما آلهة إلا الله (النتيجة. وقد رفع فيها المقدم).

مقدم تالي
[لو كان خيراً ما سبقونا إليه] (الكبرى).
أي : لكنهم سبقونا إليه (الصغرى. وقد رفع فيها التالي).
.. فهو ليس خيراً (النتيجة التي استنتجوها. وقد رفع فيها المقدم).
وقياس المشركين هذا فاسد، لأن كبراه غير مُسَلَّم بها، فسبق المؤمنين إلى الإيمان لا يدل على أن الإيمان ليس خيراً، ولو كان السابقون إليه من ضعفاء القوم وفقرائهم.
د - في حق الرسول ﷺ:
مقدم تالي
[ولو تقول علينا بعض الأقاويل لأخذنا منه باليمين ثم لقطعنا منه الوتين] (الكبرى).
أي : لكننا لم نأخذ منه باليمين ولم نقطع منه الوتين (الصغرى. وقد رفع فيها التالي).
.. فما تقول علينا بعض الأقاويل (النتيجة. وقد رفع فيها المقدم).
and the consequent, and this does not exist in all examples, so it cannot be taken as a constant basis for the syllogism. Among the examples that contain the constant reciprocal entailment between the antecedent of the conditional proposition and its consequent, the example that is repeatedly used by logicians: Always if the sun is rising then daytime exists. In this example and its likes, we note that denying each of the antecedent and consequent necessitates denying the other, and affirming each of the antecedent and consequent necessitates affirming the other, so we say: But the sun is not rising, therefore daytime does not exist; But daytime does not exist, therefore the sun is not rising; But the sun is rising, therefore daytime exists; But daytime exists, therefore the sun is rising. And the truth of the inference here in the four cases is due to the particularity of the example and what it contains of constant reciprocal entailment between the antecedent and the consequent, and it is not true in all examples. Therefore, the correct constant inference for the conditional syllogism is confined to only two cases, as previously: Affirming the antecedent leads to affirming the consequent; Denying the consequent leads to denying the antecedent. Applications of the conditional syllogism: Antecedent Consequent. A: 'If there were in them other gods besides Allah, they would have been corrupted' (major premise). That is: But they have not been corrupted (minor premise, in which the consequent is denied). Therefore, there are no other gods besides Allah (conclusion, in which the antecedent is denied). B: Regarding Yunus, peace be upon him: Antecedent Consequent: 'Had he not been of those who exalt Allah, he would have remained inside its belly until the Day they are resurrected.' That is: But he did not remain in its belly (minor premise, in which the consequent is denied). Therefore, he was of those who exalt Allah (conclusion, in which the antecedent is denied). C: The saying of the polytheists regarding the believers: Antecedent Consequent: 'If it (i.e., faith) had been good, they would not have preceded us to it' (major premise). That is: But they have preceded us to it (minor premise, in which the consequent is denied). Therefore, it is not good (conclusion they inferred, in which the antecedent is denied). And this syllogism of the polytheists is corrupt, because its major premise is not conceded, for the precedence of the believers to faith does not indicate that faith is not good, even if those who preceded to it were among the weak and poor of the people. D: Regarding the Messenger, peace be upon him: Antecedent Consequent: 'And if he had invented against Us some sayings, We would have seized him by the right hand, then We would have cut from him the aorta' (major premise). That is: But We have not seized him by the right hand nor cut his aorta (minor premise, in which the consequent is denied). Therefore, he did not invent against Us some sayings (conclusion, in which the antecedent is denied).
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage explains the two valid moods of the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil), then provides four practical examples from Islamic sources. The author begins by noting a common misconception: some examples, like the standard 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists', exhibit reciprocal entailment between the antecedent and consequent, making all four possible inferences (affirming antecedent, denying antecedent, affirming consequent, denying consequent) valid. However, such reciprocal entailment is rare; in most conditional statements, the entailment is one-way. Therefore, the only universally valid inferences are: (1) Affirming the antecedent leads to affirming the consequent, and (2) Denying the consequent leads to denying the antecedent. These two rules are the foundation of the conditional syllogism. The author then applies these rules to four examples: A) The Quranic argument against multiple gods (Surah Al-Anbiya' 22): 'If there were other gods besides Allah, the heavens and earth would be corrupted. They are not corrupted, so there are no other gods.' This is a perfect example of denying the consequent. B) The story of Prophet Yunus (Surah As-Saffat 143-144): 'Had he not been of those who exalt Allah, he would have remained in the whale's belly. He did not remain, so he was of those who exalt Allah.' Again, denying the consequent. C) The polytheists' flawed argument: 'If faith were good, they would not have preceded us to it. But they preceded us, so it is not good.' The author points out the fallacy: the major premise is false because precedence does not indicate goodness. This shows that even a formally valid syllogism can be unsound if its premises are false. D) The divine proof of the Prophet's truthfulness (Surah Al-Haqqah 44-46): 'If he had fabricated sayings against Us, We would have seized him and cut his aorta. We did not seize him, so he did not fabricate.' This is a valid denial of the consequent. The passage thus demonstrates how conditional syllogisms are used in Islamic theology and disputation, emphasizing the importance of both formal validity and premise truth.

Key Takeaway
The correct constant inference for the conditional syllogism is confined to two cases: affirming the antecedent leads to affirming the consequent, and denying the consequent leads to denying the antecedent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
