The Syllogism
beginner
10 min
Recognizing Modus Ponens and Modus Tollens in Conditional Syllogisms
Author illustrates modus ponens and modus tollens with six examples from Quran and science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conditional syllogism uses modus ponens or modus tollens and derive the correct conclusion from the minor premise.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism is valid only when you affirm the antecedent or deny the consequent.

This lesson uses Quranic, scientific, and political examples to teach the two valid forms of conditional reasoning. Each example is structured with a major premise (if-then) and a minor premise that either affirms the antecedent or denies the consequent, leading to a necessary conclusion. You will learn to recognize these patterns and apply them in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise (al-kubra)
Locate the conditional statement (if A then B) in the example. This is the rule or general principle.
In the Quranic examples, the major premise is often a quoted verse.
2
Identify the minor premise (al-sughra)
Find the proposition that either affirms the antecedent (وضع المقدم) or denies the consequent (رفع التالي). This determines which form is used.
Look for keywords like 'but' or 'actually' that introduce the minor premise.
3
Determine the logical operation
If the minor affirms the antecedent, the form is modus ponens (the conclusion affirms the consequent). If the minor denies the consequent, the form is modus tollens (the conclusion denies the antecedent).
Only these two operations yield a valid conclusion.
4
Derive the correct conclusion
Based on the operation, state the conclusion. For modus ponens: conclude B. For modus tollens: conclude not A.
Verify that the conclusion matches the last line of the example.
5
Check the soundness of the major premise
Even if the form is valid, the argument is only sound if the major premise is conceded (musallam biha). Example C shows an unsound major leading to a false conclusion despite valid form.
This step distinguishes formal validity from material truth.

So it expands with heat (result. And in it the consequent is affirmed). Or: But by experiment it does not expand with heat (minor premise. And in it the consequent is negated). .. So it is not mercury (result. And in it the antecedent is negated). G - Whenever the sky is clear, no rain falls on the earth (major premise). But it is actually clear (minor premise. And in it the antecedent is affirmed). .. So no rain falls on the earth (result. And in it the consequent is affirmed). Or: But rain falls on the earth (minor premise. And in it the consequent is negated). .. So the sky is not clear (result. And in it the antecedent is negated). H - Whenever authority weakens or becomes corrupt, security is disrupted (major premise). But security is not disrupted. (minor premise. And in it the consequent is negated). .. So authority has not weakened nor become corrupt. (result. And in it the antecedent is negated).
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage provides a series of examples illustrating the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), which is a cornerstone of Islamic logic. The author presents seven distinct cases drawn from the Quran, historical polemics, and natural sciences. Each example is structured with a major premise (الكبرى) that is a conditional statement (if A then B), followed by a minor premise (الصغرى) that either affirms the antecedent (وضع المقدم) or negates the consequent (رفع التالي), leading to a conclusion. The first three examples (ب, ج, د) demonstrate modus tollens: denying the consequent to deny the antecedent. In example ب (Jonah), the Quranic verse implies a conditional: 'If he were not of those who glorify, he would have remained in the belly.' The minor premise negates the consequent (he did not remain), so the antecedent is negated (he was of those who glorify). Example ج (polytheists) is critiqued for an unsound major premise, teaching that formal validity does not guarantee truth. Examples هـ and the first variant of و are modus ponens: affirming the antecedent to affirm the consequent. The second variants of و and ز show the alternative modus tollens route. Example ح reinforces modus tollens with a political example. The author thus demonstrates the two valid forms of conditional syllogism: affirming antecedent yields consequent, denying consequent yields denial of antecedent.

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism is valid only when the minor premise affirms the antecedent (modus ponens) or denies the consequent (modus tollens); these two forms yield necessary conclusions.

?

Why Formally Valid Conditional Syllogisms Can Still Be Unsound
Author critiques the polytheists' conditional syllogism as corrupt due to an unsound major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a conditional syllogism's major premise is conceded and thus whether the argument is sound, using the polytheists' example as a case study.

Core Idea
Formal validity does not guarantee truth: the major premise must be conceded for the conclusion to be sound.

The polytheists' argument against the believers appears formally valid—it uses modus tollens—but the author declares it corrupt because the major premise is not conceded. This lesson explores the distinction between formal validity (صورة) and material soundness (مادة) and teaches how to critically assess premises in Islamic debates.

Guided Path
1
State the polytheists' argument in logical form
Identify the major premise: 'If it (faith) were good, they (the believers) would not have preceded us to it.' The minor: 'But they preceded us.' Conclusion: 'Therefore it is not good.' Note the form: modus tollens (denying consequent).
The form is valid, yet the author says the syllogism is corrupt.
2
Identify the author's critique
The author states that the major premise is 'غير مسلّم بها' (not conceded). This means the conditional link between 'goodness' and 'not being preceded' is not accepted as true.
The major premise is not a universally recognized truth; it is an assumption of the polytheists.
3
Understand why the major is unsound
The major premise implies that if something is good, then no one can precede you to it. But precedence does not negate goodness; the believers may have preceded for other reasons (e.g., they were weak and poor). The premise falsely assumes a correlation.
A sound major must be based on a genuine necessary connection.
4
Distinguish form from soundness
Formal validity concerns the logical connection between premises and conclusion. Soundness requires both valid form and true premises. The polytheists' argument is formally valid but unsound because the major is false.
In Islamic debate, one must challenge not only the form but also the truth or acceptance of premises.

And in it the antecedent is negated). E - [And if you believe and fear Allah, then for you is a great reward] (major premise). But the people of the Pledge of the Tree believed and feared Allah (minor premise. And in it the antecedent is affirmed). .. So for them is a great reward (result. And in it the consequent is affirmed). F - If this liquid is mercury, then it expands with heat (major premise). But it is actually mercury (minor premise. And in it the antecedent is affirmed). .. So it expands with heat (result. And in it the consequent is affirmed). Or: But by experiment it does not expand with heat (minor premise. And in it the consequent is negated). .. So it is not mercury (result. And in it the antecedent is negated). G - Whenever the sky is clear, no rain falls on the earth (major premise). But it is actually clear (minor premise. And in it the antecedent is affirmed). .. So no rain falls on the earth (result. And in it the consequent is affirmed). Or: But rain falls on the earth (minor premise. And in it the consequent is negated). .. So the sky is not clear (result. And in it the antecedent is negated). H - Whenever authority weakens or becomes corrupt, security is disrupted (major premise). But security is not disrupted. (minor premise. And in it the consequent is negated). .. So authority has not weakened nor become corrupt. (result. And in it the antecedent is negated).
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage provides a series of examples illustrating the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), which is a cornerstone of Islamic logic. The author presents seven distinct cases drawn from the Quran, historical polemics, and natural sciences. Each example is structured with a major premise (الكبرى) that is a conditional statement (if A then B), followed by a minor premise (الصغرى) that either affirms the antecedent (وضع المقدم) or negates the consequent (رفع التالي), leading to a conclusion. The first three examples (ب, ج, د) demonstrate modus tollens: denying the consequent to deny the antecedent. In example ب (Jonah), the Quranic verse implies a conditional: 'If he were not of those who glorify, he would have remained in the belly.' The minor premise negates the consequent (he did not remain), so the antecedent is negated (he was of those who glorify). Example ج (polytheists) is critiqued for an unsound major premise, teaching that formal validity does not guarantee truth. Examples هـ and the first variant of و are modus ponens: affirming the antecedent to affirm the consequent. The second variants of و and ز show the alternative modus tollens route. Example ح reinforces modus tollens with a political example. The author thus demonstrates the two valid forms of conditional syllogism: affirming antecedent yields consequent, denying consequent yields denial of antecedent.

الصغرى
غير مسلّم بها

Checkpoint
Why does the author describe the polytheists' syllogism as corrupt (فاسد)?
A) Because it does not follow the valid form of conditional syllogism
B) Because its major premise is not conceded (غير مسلّم بها)
C) Because the minor premise affirms the antecedent
D) Because the conclusion contradicts the Quran

Key Takeaway
Formal validity is necessary but not sufficient for a sound argument; the major premise must be conceded (musallam biha) otherwise the argument fails, even if the form is correct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'If prayer is invalid without purification, then one must repeat it. This prayer is valid, therefore it must have been with purification.' Identify the form and evaluate whether the major premise is conceded in Islamic jurisprudence.
?
Find another example from the Quran where an argument is critiqued for having an unsound premise. Hint: look at Quran 2:111 'They say: None shall enter Paradise except those who are Jews or Christians...' How does the Quran respond?

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Conditional Syllogism from the Quran: Modus Ponens
Author provides a Quranic example of conditional syllogism with affirmation of antecedent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent, consequent, and logical form of a conditional syllogism from a Quranic example, and construct a valid modus ponens argument on an Islamic topic.

Core Idea
The Quran itself provides examples of valid logical inference, affirming the antecedent in a conditional.

The author presents a model conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil) drawn directly from the Quran. The major premise is a divine conditional: 'If you believe and fear God, then you have a great reward.' The minor premise affirms the antecedent for a specific group—the Companions of the Tree Pledge—and the conclusion affirms the consequent. This illustrates the most common valid form of conditional inference, known in Western logic as modus ponens. In Islamic logic, it is called 'wad' al-muqaddam' (affirming the antecedent). This lesson will walk you through the logical anatomy of this Quranically anchored syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major premise (al-kubra)
The major premise is the conditional statement itself: 'If you believe and fear God, then for you is a great reward.' Notice it has two parts: the antecedent (muqaddam) and the consequent (tali).
The major premise is a complete conditional proposition, linking faith/taqwa to reward.
2
Identify the minor premise (al-sughra)
The minor premise affirms the antecedent specifically for the 'people of the Tree pledge' (the Companions who gave allegiance under the tree at Hudaybiyya). They indeed believed and feared God.
The minor premise does not introduce a new condition; it simply asserts that the antecedent applies to this group.
3
Identify the conclusion (al-natija)
The conclusion affirms the consequent for that group: 'Therefore for them is a great reward.' This follows necessarily from the two premises.
This is the defining feature of modus ponens: affirming the antecedent leads to affirming the consequent.
4
Recognise the overall form
The entire argument is: If P then Q; P is true; therefore Q is true. This is a valid conditional syllogism of the type 'affirming the antecedent' (wad' al-muqaddam).
In Islamic logic, this is one of two valid forms of qiyas istithna'i muttasil (the other being modus tollens).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هـ - [وإن تؤمنوا وتتقوا فلكم أجر عظيم] (الكبرى).
لكن أهل بيعة الشجرة قد آمنوا واتقوا (الصغرى. وقد وضع فيها المقدم).
.. فلهم أجر عظيم (النتيجة. وقد وضع فيها التالي).
H: 'And if you believe and fear God, then for you is a great reward' (major premise). But the people of the Tree pledge have believed and feared (minor premise, in which the antecedent is affirmed). Therefore for them is a great reward (conclusion, in which the consequent is affirmed).
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents an example of a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil) in the form of affirming the antecedent (wad' al-muqaddam). The major premise is a conditional statement from the Quran: 'If you believe and fear God, then you have a great reward.' The minor premise affirms the antecedent: the people of the Tree pledge (the companions who gave allegiance under the tree) have believed and feared God. The conclusion affirms the consequent: they have a great reward. This demonstrates the validity of modus ponens. In Islamic logic, such examples from revelation are used to illustrate logical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
